Remark. Let $\mathcal{H}_{\gamma}(\omega)$ be the set of times at which the path $\omega \in C$ is Hölder continuous of order $\gamma$. Theorem 7.1.5 shows that $P\left(\mathcal{H}_{\gamma}=\right. [0, \infty))=1$ for $\gamma<1 / 2$. Exercise 7.1.5 shows that $P\left(\mathcal{H}_{\gamma}=\emptyset\right)=1$ for $\gamma>1 / 2$. Exercise 7.2.4 shows $P\left(t \in \mathcal{H}_{1 / 2}\right)=0$ for each $t$, but B. Davis (1983) has shown $P\left(\mathcal{H}_{1 / 2} \neq \emptyset\right)=1$. Perkins (1983) has computed the Hausdorff dimension of

$$
\left\{t \in(0,1): \limsup _{h \downarrow 0} \frac{\left|B_{t+h}-B_{t}\right|}{h^{1 / 2}} \leq c\right\}
$$

## Multidimensional Brownian motion

All of the result in this section have been for one-dimensional Brownian motion. To define a $d$-dimensional Brownian motion starting at $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$ we let $B_{t}^{1}, \ldots B_{t}^{d}$ be independent Brownian motions with $B_{0}^{i}=x_{i}$. As in the case $d=1$ these are realized as probability measures $P_{x}$ on ( $C, \mathcal{C}$ ) where $C=\left\{\right.$ continuous $\left.\omega:[0, \infty) \rightarrow \mathbf{R}^{d}\right\}$ and $\mathcal{C}$ is the $\sigma$-field generated by the coordinate maps. Since the coordinates are independent, it is easy to see that the finite dimensional distributions satisfy (7.1.2) with transition probability

$$
\begin{equation*}
p_{t}(x, y)=(2 \pi t)^{-d / 2} \exp \left(-|y-x|^{2} / 2 t\right) \tag{7.1.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

## Exercises

7.1.1. Given $s<t$ find $P(B(s)>0, B(t)>0)$. Hint: write the probability in terms of $B(s)$ and $B(t)-B(s)$.
7.1.2. Find $E\left(B_{1}^{2} B_{2} B_{3}\right)$.
7.1.3. Let $W=\int_{0}^{t} B_{s} d s$. Find $E W$ and $E W^{2}$. What is the distribution of $W$
7.1.4. $A \in \mathcal{F}_{o}$ if and only if there is a sequence of times $t_{1}, t_{2}, \ldots$ in $[0, \infty)$ and a $B \in \mathcal{R}^{\{1,2, \ldots\}}$ so that $A=\left\{\omega:\left(\omega\left(t_{1}\right), \omega\left(t_{2}\right), \ldots\right) \in B\right\}$. In words, all events in $\mathcal{F}_{o}$ depend on only countably many coordinates.
7.1.5. Looking at the proof of Theorem 7.1.6 carefully shows that if $\gamma>5 / 6$ then $B_{t}$ is not Hölder continuous with exponent $\gamma$ at any point in $[0,1]$. Show, by considering $k$ increments instead of 3 , that the last conclusion is true for all $\gamma>1 / 2+1 / k$.
7.1.6. Fix $t$ and let $\Delta_{m, n}=B\left(t m 2^{-n}\right)-B\left(t(m-1) 2^{-n}\right)$. Compute

$$
E\left(\sum_{m \leq 2^{n}} \Delta_{m, n}^{2}-t\right)^{2}
$$

and use Borel-Cantelli to conclude that $\sum_{m \leq 2^{n}} \Delta_{m, n}^{2} \rightarrow t$ a.s. as $n \rightarrow \infty$.
Remark. The last result is true if we consider a sequence of partitions $\Pi_{1} \subset \Pi_{2} \subset \ldots$ with mesh $\rightarrow 0$. See Freedman (1971a) p. 42-46. However, the true quadratic variation, defined as the sup over all partitions, is $\infty$.

### 7.2 Markov Property, Blumenthal's 0-1 Law

Intuitively, the Markov property says "if $s \geq 0$ then $B(t+s)-B(s), t \geq 0$ is a Brownian motion that is independent of what happened before time $s$." The first step in making this into a precise statement is to explain what we mean by "what happened before time $s$." The first thing that comes to mind is

$$
\mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}=\sigma\left(B_{r}: r \leq s\right)
$$

For reasons that will become clear as we go along, it is convenient to replace $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$ by

$$
\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}=\cap_{t>s} \mathcal{F}_{t}^{o}
$$

The fields $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$are nicer because they are right continuous:

$$
\cap_{t>s} \mathcal{F}_{t}^{+}=\cap_{t>s}\left(\cap_{u>t} \mathcal{F}_{u}^{o}\right)=\cap_{u>s} \mathcal{F}_{u}^{o}=\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}
$$

In words, the $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$allow us an "infinitesimal peek at the future," i.e., $A \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$if it is in $\mathcal{F}_{s+\epsilon}^{o}$ for any $\epsilon>0$. If $f(u)>0$ for all $u>0$, then in $d=1$ the random variable

$$
\limsup _{t \downarrow s} \frac{B_{t}-B_{s}}{f(t-s)}
$$

is measurable with respect to $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$but not $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$. We will see below that there are no interesting examples, i.e., $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$and $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$ are the same (up to null sets).

To state the Markov property, we need some notation. Recall that we have a family of measures $P_{x}, x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$, on ( $C, \mathcal{C}$ ) so that under $P_{x}$, $B_{t}(\omega)=\omega(t)$ is a Brownian motion starting at $x$. For $s \geq 0$, we define the shift transformation $\theta_{s}: C \rightarrow C$ by

$$
\left(\theta_{s} \omega\right)(t)=\omega(s+t) \quad \text { for } t \geq 0
$$

In words, we cut off the part of the path before time $s$ and then shift the path so that time $s$ becomes time 0 .

Theorem 7.2.1. Markov property. If $s \geq 0$ and $Y$ is bounded and $\mathcal{C}$ measurable, then for all $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$

$$
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=E_{B_{s}} Y
$$

where the right-hand side is the function $\varphi(x)=E_{x} Y$ evaluated at $x= B_{s}$.

Proof. By the definition of conditional expectation, what we need to show is that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} ; A\right)=E_{x}\left(E_{B_{s}} Y ; A\right) \quad \text { for all } A \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+} \tag{7.2.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

We will begin by proving the result for a carefully chosen special case and then use the monotone class theorem (MCT) to get the general case. Suppose $Y(\omega)=\prod_{1 \leq m \leq n} f_{m}\left(\omega\left(t_{m}\right)\right)$, where $0<t_{1}<\ldots<t_{n}$ and the $f_{m}$ are bounded and measurable. Let $0<h<t_{1}$, let $0<s_{1} \ldots<s_{k} \leq s+h$, and let $A=\left\{\omega: \omega\left(s_{j}\right) \in A_{j}, 1 \leq j \leq k\right\}$, where $A_{j} \in \mathcal{R}$ for $1 \leq j \leq k$. From the definition of Brownian motion, it follows that

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} ; A\right)= & \int_{A_{1}} d x_{1} p_{s_{1}}\left(x, x_{1}\right) \int_{A_{2}} d x_{2} p_{s_{2}-s_{1}}\left(x_{1}, x_{2}\right) \cdots \\
& \int_{A_{k}} d x_{k} p_{s_{k}-s_{k-1}}\left(x_{k-1}, x_{k}\right) \int d y p_{s+h-s_{k}}\left(x_{k}, y\right) \varphi(y, h)
\end{aligned}
$$

where

$$
\varphi(y, h)=\int d y_{1} p_{t_{1}-h}\left(y, y_{1}\right) f_{1}\left(y_{1}\right) \ldots \int d y_{n} p_{t_{n}-t_{n-1}}\left(y_{n-1}, y_{n}\right) f_{n}\left(y_{n}\right)
$$

For more details, see the proof of (5.2.3), which applies without change here. Using that identity on the right-hand side, we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} ; A\right)=E_{x}\left(\varphi\left(B_{s+h}, h\right) ; A\right) \tag{7.2.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

The last equality holds for all finite dimensional sets $A$ so the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem, Theorem 2.1.6, implies that it is valid for all $A \in \mathcal{F}_{s+h}^{o} \supset \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$.

It is easy to see by induction on $n$ that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\psi\left(y_{1}\right)= & f_{1}\left(y_{1}\right) \int d y_{2} p_{t_{2}-t_{1}}\left(y_{1}, y_{2}\right) f_{2}\left(y_{2}\right) \\
& \ldots \int d y_{n} p_{t_{n}-t_{n-1}}\left(y_{n-1}, y_{n}\right) f_{n}\left(y_{n}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

is bounded and measurable. Letting $h \downarrow 0$ and using the dominated convergence theorem shows that if $x_{h} \rightarrow x$, then

$$
\phi\left(x_{h}, h\right)=\int d y_{1} p_{t_{1}-h}\left(x_{h}, y_{1}\right) \psi\left(y_{1}\right) \rightarrow \phi(x, 0)
$$

as $h \downarrow 0$. Using (7.2.2) and the bounded convergence theorem now gives

$$
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} ; A\right)=E_{x}\left(\varphi\left(B_{s}, 0\right) ; A\right)
$$

for all $A \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$. This shows that (7.2.1) holds for $Y=\prod_{1 \leq m \leq n} f_{m}\left(\omega\left(t_{m}\right)\right)$ and the $f_{m}$ are bounded and measurable.

The desired conclusion now follows from the monotone class theorem, 5.2.2. Let $\mathcal{H}=$ the collection of bounded functions for which (7.2.1) holds. $\mathcal{H}$ clearly has properties (ii) and (iii). Let $\mathcal{A}$ be the collection of sets of the form $\left\{\omega: \omega\left(t_{j}\right) \in A_{j}\right\}$, where $A_{j} \in \mathcal{R}$. The special case treated above shows (i) holds and the desired conclusion follows.

The reader will see many applications of the Markov property below, so we turn our attention now to a "triviality" that has surprising consequences. Since

$$
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=E_{B(s)} Y \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}
$$

it follows from Theorem 4.1.12 that

$$
E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}\right)
$$

From the last equation, it is a short step to:
Theorem 7.2.2. If $Z \in \mathcal{C}$ is bounded then for all $s \geq 0$ and $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$,

$$
E_{x}\left(Z \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=E_{x}\left(Z \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}\right)
$$

Proof. As in the proof of Theorem 7.2.1, it suffices to prove the result when

$$
Z=\prod_{m=1}^{n} f_{m}\left(B\left(t_{m}\right)\right)
$$

and the $f_{m}$ are bounded and measurable. In this case, $Z$ can be written as $X\left(Y \circ \theta_{s}\right)$, where $X \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$ and $Y$ is $\mathcal{C}$ measurable, so

$$
E_{x}\left(Z \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=X E_{x}\left(Y \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}\right)=X E_{B_{s}} Y \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}
$$

and the proof is complete.
If we let $Z \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$, then Theorem 7.2.2 implies $Z=E_{x}\left(Z \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}\right) \in \mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$, so the two $\sigma$-fields are the same up to null sets. At first glance, this conclusion is not exciting. The fun starts when we take $s=0$ in Theorem 7.2.2 to get:

Theorem 7.2.3. Blumenthal's $\mathbf{0 - 1}$ law. If $A \in \mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}$then for all $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$,

$$
P_{x}(A) \in\{0,1\} .
$$

Proof. Using $A \in \mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}$, Theorem 7.2.2, and $\mathcal{F}_{0}^{o}=\sigma\left(B_{0}\right)$ is trivial under $P_{x}$ gives

$$
1_{A}=E_{x}\left(1_{A} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}\right)=E_{x}\left(1_{A} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}^{o}\right)=P_{x}(A) \quad P_{x} \text { a.s. }
$$

This shows that the indicator function $1_{A}$ is a.s. equal to the number $P_{x}(A)$, and the result follows.

In words, the last result says that the germ field, $\mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}$, is trivial. This result is very useful in studying the local behavior of Brownian paths. For the rest of the section we restrict our attention to $d=1$.

Theorem 7.2.4. If $\tau=\inf \left\{t \geq 0: B_{t}>0\right\}$ then $P_{0}(\tau=0)=1$.

Proof. $P_{0}(\tau \leq t) \geq P_{0}\left(B_{t}>0\right)=1 / 2$ since the normal distribution is symmetric about 0 . Letting $t \downarrow 0$, we conclude

$$
P_{0}(\tau=0)=\lim _{t \downarrow 0} P_{0}(\tau \leq t) \geq 1 / 2
$$

so it follows from Theorem 7.2.3 that $P_{0}(\tau=0)=1$.
Once Brownian motion must hit ( $0, \infty$ ) immediately starting from 0 , it must also hit $(-\infty, 0)$ immediately. Since $t \rightarrow B_{t}$ is continuous, this forces:

Theorem 7.2.5. If $T_{0}=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t}=0\right\}$ then $P_{0}\left(T_{0}=0\right)=1$.
Theorem 7.2.3 concerns the behavior of $B_{t}$ as $t \rightarrow 0$. By using a trick, we can use this result to get information about the behavior as $t \rightarrow \infty$.

Theorem 7.2.6. If $B_{t}$ is a Brownian motion starting at 0 , then so is the process defined by $X_{0}=0$ and $X_{t}=t B(1 / t)$ for $t>0$.

Proof. Here we will check the second definition of Brownian motion. To do this, we note: (i) If $0<t_{1}<\ldots<t_{n}$, then $\left(X\left(t_{1}\right), \ldots, X\left(t_{n}\right)\right)$ has a multivariate normal distribution with mean 0 . (ii) $E X_{s}=0$ and if $s<t$ then

$$
E\left(X_{s} X_{t}\right)=\operatorname{stE}(B(1 / s) B(1 / t))=s
$$

For (iii) we note that $X$ is clearly continuous at $t \neq 0$.
To handle $t=0$, we begin by observing that the strong law of large numbers implies $B_{n} / n \rightarrow 0$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$ through the integers. To handle values in between integers, we note that Kolmogorov's inequality, Theorem 2.5.5, implies

$$
P\left(\sup _{0<k \leq 2^{m}}\left|B\left(n+k 2^{-m}\right)-B_{n}\right|>n^{2 / 3}\right) \leq n^{-4 / 3} E\left(B_{n+1}-B_{n}\right)^{2}
$$

Letting $m \rightarrow \infty$, we have

$$
P\left(\sup _{u \in[n, n+1]}\left|B_{u}-B_{n}\right|>n^{2 / 3}\right) \leq n^{-4 / 3}
$$

Since $\sum_{n} n^{-4 / 3}<\infty$, the Borel-Cantelli lemma implies $B_{u} / u \rightarrow 0$ as $u \rightarrow \infty$. Taking $u=1 / t$, we have $X_{t} \rightarrow 0$ as $t \rightarrow 0$.

Theorem 7.2.6 allows us to relate the behavior of $B_{t}$ as $t \rightarrow \infty$ and as $t \rightarrow 0$. Combining this idea with Blumenthal's 0-1 law leads to a very useful result. Let

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathcal{F}_{t}^{\prime}=\sigma\left(B_{s}: s \geq t\right) & =\text { the future at time } t \\
\mathcal{T}=\cap_{t \geq 0} \mathcal{F}_{t}^{\prime} & =\text { the tail } \sigma \text {-field. }
\end{aligned}
$$

Theorem 7.2.7. If $A \in \mathcal{T}$ then either $P_{x}(A) \equiv 0$ or $P_{x}(A) \equiv 1$.
Remark. Notice that this is stronger than the conclusion of Blumenthal's 0-1 law. The examples $A=\{\omega: \omega(0) \in D\}$ show that for $A$ in the germ $\sigma$-field $\mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}$, the value of $P_{x}(A), 1_{D}(x)$ in this case, may depend on $x$.

Proof. Since the tail $\sigma$-field of $B$ is the same as the germ $\sigma$-field for $X$, it follows that $P_{0}(A) \in\{0,1\}$. To improve this to the conclusion given, observe that $A \in \mathcal{F}_{1}^{\prime}$, so $1_{A}$ can be written as $1_{D} \circ \theta_{1}$. Applying the Markov property gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{x}(A) & =E_{x}\left(1_{D} \circ \theta_{1}\right)=E_{x}\left(E_{x}\left(1_{D} \circ \theta_{1} \mid \mathcal{F}_{1}\right)\right)=E_{x}\left(E_{B_{1}} 1_{D}\right) \\
& =\int(2 \pi)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-(y-x)^{2} / 2\right) P_{y}(D) d y
\end{aligned}
$$

Taking $x=0$, we see that if $P_{0}(A)=0$, then $P_{y}(D)=0$ for a.e. $y$ with respect to Lebesgue measure, and using the formula again shows $P_{x}(A)=0$ for all $x$. To handle the case $P_{0}(A)=1$, observe that $A^{c} \in \mathcal{T}$ and $P_{0}\left(A^{c}\right)=0$, so the last result implies $P_{x}\left(A^{c}\right)=0$ for all $x$.

The next result is a typical application of Theorem 7.2.7.
Theorem 7.2.8. Let $B_{t}$ be a one-dimensional Brownian motion starting at 0 then with probability 1 ,

$$
\limsup _{t \rightarrow \infty} B_{t} / \sqrt{t}=\infty \quad \liminf _{t \rightarrow \infty} B_{t} / \sqrt{t}=-\infty
$$

Proof. Let $K<\infty$. By Exercise 2.3.1 and scaling

$$
P_{0}\left(B_{n} / \sqrt{n} \geq K \text { i.o. }\right) \geq \limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{0}\left(B_{n} \geq K \sqrt{n}\right)=P_{0}\left(B_{1} \geq K\right)>0
$$

so the $0-1$ law in Theorem 7.2.7 implies the probability is 1 . Since $K$ is arbitrary, this proves the first result. The second one follows from symmetry.

From Theorem 7.2.8, translation invariance, and the continuity of Brownian paths it follows that we have:

Theorem 7.2.9. Let $B_{t}$ be a one-dimensional Brownian motion and let $A=\cap_{n}\left\{B_{t}=0\right.$ for some $\left.t \geq n\right\}$. Then $P_{x}(A)=1$ for all $x$.

In words, one-dimensional Brownian motion is recurrent. For any starting point $x$, it will return to 0 "infinitely often," i.e., there is a sequence of times $t_{n} \uparrow \infty$ so that $B_{t_{n}}=0$. We have to be careful with the interpretation of the phrase in quotes since starting from $0, B_{t}$ will hit 0 infinitely many times by time $\epsilon>0$.

Last rites. With our discussion of Blumenthal's 0-1 law complete, the distinction between $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+}$and $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{o}$ is no longer important, so we will make one final improvement in our $\sigma$-fields and remove the superscripts. Let

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathcal{N}_{x} & =\left\{A: A \subset D \text { with } P_{x}(D)=0\right\} \\
\mathcal{F}_{s}^{x} & =\sigma\left(\mathcal{F}_{s}^{+} \cup \mathcal{N}_{x}\right) \\
\mathcal{F}_{s} & =\cap_{x} \mathcal{F}_{s}^{x}
\end{aligned}
$$

$\mathcal{N}_{x}$ are the null sets and $\mathcal{F}_{s}^{x}$ are the completed $\sigma$-fields for $P_{x}$. Since we do not want the filtration to depend on the initial state, we take the intersection of all the $\sigma$-fields. The reader should note that it follows from the definition that the $\mathcal{F}_{s}$ are right-continuous.

Exercises
7.2.1. Let $T_{0}=\inf \left\{s>0: B_{s}=0\right\}$ and let $R=\inf \left\{t>1: B_{t}=0\right\} . R$ is for right or return. Use the Markov property at time 1 to get

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}(R>1+t)=\int p_{1}(x, y) P_{y}\left(T_{0}>t\right) d y \tag{7.2.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

7.2.2. Let $T_{0}=\inf \left\{s>0: B_{s}=0\right\}$ and let $L=\sup \left\{t \leq 1: B_{t}=0\right\} . L$ is for left or last. Use the Markov property at time $0<t<1$ to conclude

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}(L \leq t)=\int p_{t}(0, y) P_{y}\left(T_{0}>1-t\right) d y \tag{7.2.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

7.2.3. If $a<b$, then with probability one $a$ is the limit of local maximum of $B_{t}$ in $(a, b)$. So the set of local maxima of $B_{t}$ is almost surely a dense set. However, unlike the zero set it is countable.
7.2.4. (i) Suppose $f(t)>0$ for all $t>0$. Use Theorem 7.2.3 to conclude that $\limsup _{t \downarrow 0} B(t) / f(t)=c, P_{0}$ a.s., where $c \in[0, \infty]$ is a constant. (ii) Show that if $f(t)=\sqrt{t}$ then $c=\infty$, so with probability one Brownian paths are not Hölder continuous of order $1 / 2$ at 0 .

### 7.3 Stopping Times, Strong Markov Property

Generalizing the definition from discrete time, we call a random variable $S$ taking values in $[0, \infty]$ a stopping time if for all $t \geq 0,\{S<t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$. In the last definition, we have obviously made a choice between $\{S<t\}$ and $\{S \leq t\}$. This makes a big difference in discrete time but none in continuous time (for a right continuous filtration $\mathcal{F}_{t}$ ):

If $\{S \leq t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$ then $\{S<t\}=\cup_{n}\{S \leq t-1 / n\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$.
If $\{S<t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$ then $\{S \leq t\}=\cap_{n}\{S<t+1 / n\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$.
The first conclusion requires only that $t \rightarrow \mathcal{F}_{t}$ is increasing. The second relies on the fact that $t \rightarrow \mathcal{F}_{t}$ is right continuous. Theorem 7.3.2 and 7.3.3 below show that when checking something is a stopping time, it is nice to know that the two definitions are equivalent.

Theorem 7.3.1. If $G$ is an open set and $T=\inf \left\{t \geq 0: B_{t} \in G\right\}$ then $T$ is a stopping time.

Proof. Since $G$ is open and $t \rightarrow B_{t}$ is continuous, $\{T<t\}=\cup_{q<t}\left\{B_{q} \in\right. G\}$, where the union is over all rational $q$, so $\{T<t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$. Here we need to use the rationals to get a countable union, and hence a measurable set.

Theorem 7.3.2. If $T_{n}$ is a sequence of stopping times and $T_{n} \downarrow T$ then $T$ is a stopping time.

Proof. $\{T<t\}=\cup_{n}\left\{T_{n}<t\right\}$.
Theorem 7.3.3. If $T_{n}$ is a sequence of stopping times and $T_{n} \uparrow T$ then $T$ is a stopping time.

Proof. $\{T \leq t\}=\cap_{n}\left\{T_{n} \leq t\right\}$.
Theorem 7.3.4. If $K$ is a closed set and $T=\inf \left\{t \geq 0: B_{t} \in K\right\}$ then $T$ is a stopping time.

Proof. Let $B(x, r)=\{y:|y-x|<r\}$, let $G_{n}=\cup_{x \in K} B(x, 1 / n)$ and let $T_{n}=\inf \left\{t \geq 0: B_{t} \in G_{n}\right\}$. Since $G_{n}$ is open, it follows from Theorem 7.3.1 that $T_{n}$ is a stopping time. I claim that as $n \uparrow \infty, T_{n} \uparrow T$. To prove this, notice that $T \geq T_{n}$ for all $n$, so $\lim T_{n} \leq T$. To prove $T \leq \lim T_{n}$, we can suppose that $T_{n} \uparrow t<\infty$. Since $B\left(T_{n}\right) \in \bar{G}_{n}$ for all $n$ and $B\left(T_{n}\right) \rightarrow B(t)$, it follows that $B(t) \in K$ and $T \leq t$.

Theorems 7.3.1 and 7.3.4 will take care of all the hitting times we will consider. Using 7.3.2 with the second result one can show that the hitting time of a union of closed sets is a stopping time. It is known that the hitting time of a Borel set is a stopping time but proving that is very difficult.

We will need the next result in the proof of the strong Markov property

Theorem 7.3.5. Let $S$ be a stopping time and let $S_{n}=\left(\left[2^{n} S\right]+1\right) / 2^{n}$ where $[x]=$ the largest integer $\leq x$. That is,

$$
S_{n}=(m+1) 2^{-n} \text { if } m 2^{-n} \leq S<(m+1) 2^{-n}
$$

In words, we stop at the first time of the form $k 2^{-n}$ after $S$ (i.e., $>S$ ). From the verbal description, it should be clear that $S_{n}$ is a stopping time.

Proof. If $m / 2^{-n}<\leq(m+1) / 2^{-n}$ then $\left\{S_{n}<t\right\}=\left\{S \leq m / 2^{n}\right\} \in \mathcal{F}_{m / 2^{n}} \subset \mathcal{F}_{t}$. It is impossible to have $S_{n}=0$.

Our next goal is to state and prove the strong Markov property. To do this, we need to generalize two definitions from Section 4.1. Given a nonnegative random variable $S(\omega)$ we define the random shift $\theta_{S}$, which "cuts off the part of $\omega$ before $S(\omega)$ and then shifts the path so that time $S(\omega)$ becomes time 0 ." In symbols, we set

$$
\left(\theta_{S} \omega\right)(t)= \begin{cases}\omega(S(\omega)+t) & \text { on }\{S<\infty\} \\ \Delta & \text { on }\{S=\infty\}\end{cases}
$$

where $\Delta$ is an extra point we add to $C$. As in Section 6.3, we will usually explicitly restrict our attention to $\{S<\infty\}$, so the reader does not have to worry about the second half of the definition.

The second quantity $\mathcal{F}_{S}$, "the information known at time $S$," is a little more subtle. Imitating the discrete time definition from Section 4.1, we let

$$
\mathcal{F}_{S}=\left\{A: A \cap\{S \leq t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t} \text { for all } t \geq 0\right\}
$$

In words, this makes the reasonable demand that the part of $A$ that lies in $\{S \leq t\}$ should be measurable with respect to the information available at time $t$. Again we have made a choice between $\leq t$ and $<t$, but as in the case of stopping times, this makes no difference, and it is useful to know that the two definitions are equivalent.
Remark. When $\mathcal{F}_{t}$ is right continuous, the last definition is unchanged if we replace $\{S \leq t\}$ by $\{S<t\}$.

We will now derive some properties of $\mathcal{F}_{S}$. The first is intuitively obvious: at a later time we have more information.

Theorem 7.3.6. If $S \leq T$ are stopping times then $\mathcal{F}_{S} \subset \mathcal{F}_{T}$.
Proof. If $A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}$ then $A \cap\{T \leq t\}=(A \cap\{S \leq t\}) \cap\{T \leq t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$.
Theorem 7.3.7. If $T_{n} \downarrow T$ are stopping times then $\mathcal{F}_{T}=\cap \mathcal{F}\left(T_{n}\right)$.
Proof. Theorem 7.3.6 implies $\mathcal{F}\left(T_{n}\right) \supset \mathcal{F}_{T}$ for all $n$. To prove the other inclusion, let $A \in \cap \mathcal{F}\left(T_{n}\right)$. Since $A \cap\left\{T_{n}<t\right\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$ and $T_{n} \downarrow T$, it follows that $A \cap\{T<t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$.

Thenext result is obvious but takes a little work.
Theorem 7.3.8. $B_{S} \in \mathcal{F}_{S}$, i.e., the value of $B_{S}$ is measurable with respect to the information known at time $S$ !
Proof. To prove this, let $S_{n}=\left(\left[2^{n} S\right]+1\right) / 2^{n}$ be the stopping times defined in Theorem 7.3.5. If $A$ is a Borel set.

$$
\left\{B\left(S_{n}\right) \in A\right\}=\cup_{m=1}^{\infty}\left\{S_{n}=m / 2^{n}, B\left(m / 2^{n}\right) \in A\right\} \in \mathcal{F}_{S_{n}}
$$

Now let $n \rightarrow \infty$ and use Theorem 7.3.7.
We are now ready to state the strong Markov property, which says that the Markov property holds at stopping times. It is interesting that the notion of Brownian motion dates to the the very beginning of the 20th century, but the first proofs of the strong Markov property were given independently by Hunt (1956), and Dynkin and Yushkevich (1956). Hunt writes "Although mathematicians use this extended Markoff property, at least as a heuristic principle, I have nowhere found it discussed with rigor."
Theorem 7.3.9. Strong Markov property. Let $(s, \omega) \rightarrow Y_{s}(\omega)$ be bounded and $\mathcal{R} \times \mathcal{C}$ measurable. If $S$ is a stopping time, then for all $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$

$$
E_{x}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} \mid \mathcal{F}_{S}\right)=E_{B(S)} Y_{S} \text { on }\{S<\infty\}
$$

where the right-hand side is the function $\varphi(x, t)=E_{x} Y_{t}$ evaluated at $x=B(S), t=S$.

Remark. The only facts about Brownian motion used here are that (i) it is a Markov process, and (ii) if $f$ is bounded and continuous then $x \rightarrow E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)$ is continuous. In Markov process theory (ii) is called the Feller property. While Hunt's proof only applies to Brownian motion, and Dynkin and Yushkevich proved the result in this generality.

Proof. We first prove the result under the assumption that there is a sequence of times $t_{n} \uparrow \infty$, so that $P_{x}(S<\infty)=\sum P_{x}\left(S=t_{n}\right)$. In this case, the proof is basically the same as the proof of Theorem 5.2.5. We break things down according to the value of $S$, apply the Markov property, and put the pieces back together. If we let $Z_{n}=Y_{t_{n}}(\omega)$ and $A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}$, then

$$
E_{x}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} ; A \cap\{S<\infty\}\right)=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} E_{x}\left(Z_{n} \circ \theta_{t_{n}} ; A \cap\left\{S=t_{n}\right\}\right)
$$

Now if $A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}, A \cap\left\{S=t_{n}\right\}=\left(A \cap\left\{S \leq t_{n}\right\}\right)-\left(A \cap\left\{S \leq t_{n-1}\right\}\right) \in \mathcal{F}_{t_{n}}$, so it follows from the Markov property that the above sum is

$$
=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} E_{x}\left(E_{B\left(t_{n}\right)} Z_{n} ; A \cap\left\{S=t_{n}\right\}\right)=E_{x}\left(E_{B(S)} Y_{S} ; A \cap\{S<\infty\}\right)
$$

To prove the result in general, we let $S_{n}=\left(\left[2^{n} S\right]+1\right) / 2^{n}$ be the stopping time defined in Exercise 7.3.5. To be able to let $n \rightarrow \infty$, we restrict our attention to $Y$ 's of the form

$$
\begin{equation*}
Y_{s}(\omega)=f_{0}(s) \prod_{m=1}^{n} f_{m}\left(\omega\left(t_{m}\right)\right) \tag{7.3.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $0<t_{1}<\ldots<t_{n}$ and $f_{0}, \ldots, f_{n}$ are bounded and continuous. If $f$ is bounded and continuous then the dominated convergence theorem implies that

$$
x \rightarrow \int d y p_{t}(x, y) f(y)
$$

is continuous. From this and induction, it follows that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varphi(x, s)=E_{x} Y_{s}= & f_{0}(s) \int d y_{1} p_{t_{1}}\left(x, y_{1}\right) f_{1}\left(y_{1}\right) \\
& \ldots \int d y_{n} p_{t_{n-t_{n-1}}}\left(y_{n-1}, y_{n}\right) f_{n}\left(y_{n}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

is bounded and continuous.
Having assembled the necessary ingredients, we can now complete the proof. Let $A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}$. Since $S \leq S_{n}$, Theorem 7.3.6 implies $A \in \mathcal{F}\left(S_{n}\right)$. Applying the special case proved above to $S_{n}$ and observing that $\left\{S_{n}<\right. \infty\}=\{S<\infty\}$ gives

$$
E_{x}\left(Y_{S_{n}} \circ \theta_{S_{n}} ; A \cap\{S<\infty\}\right)=E_{x}\left(\varphi\left(B\left(S_{n}\right), S_{n}\right) ; A \cap\{S<\infty\}\right)
$$

Now, as $n \rightarrow \infty, S_{n} \downarrow S, B\left(S_{n}\right) \rightarrow B(S), \varphi\left(B\left(S_{n}\right), S_{n}\right) \rightarrow \varphi(B(S), S)$ and

$$
Y_{S_{n}} \circ \theta_{S_{n}} \rightarrow Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S}
$$

so the bounded convergence theorem implies that the result holds when $Y$ has the form given in (7.3.1).

To complete the proof now, we will apply the monotone class theorem. As in the proof of Theorem 7.2.1, we let $\mathcal{H}$ be the collection of $Y$ for which

$$
E_{x}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} ; A\right)=E_{x}\left(E_{B(S)} Y_{S} ; A\right) \quad \text { for all } A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}
$$

and it is easy to see that (ii) and (iii) hold. This time, however, we take $\mathcal{A}$ to be the sets of the form $A=G_{0} \times\left\{\omega: \omega\left(s_{j}\right) \in G_{j}, 1 \leq j \leq k\right\}$, where the $G_{j}$ are open sets. To verify (i), we note that if $K_{j}=G_{j}^{c}$ and $f_{j}^{n}(x)=1 \wedge n \rho\left(x, K_{j}\right)$, where $\rho(x, K)=\inf \{|x-y|: y \in K\}$ then $f_{j}^{n}$ are continuous functions with $f_{j}^{n} \uparrow 1_{G_{j}}$ as $n \uparrow \infty$. The facts that

$$
Y_{s}^{n}(\omega)=f_{0}^{n}(s) \prod_{j=1}^{k} f_{j}^{n}\left(\omega\left(s_{j}\right)\right) \in \mathcal{H}
$$

and (iii) holds for $\mathcal{H}$ imply that $1_{A} \in \mathcal{H}$. This verifies (i) in the monotone class theorem and completes the proof.

## Exercises

7.3.1. Let $A$ be an $F_{\sigma}$, that is, a countable union of closed sets. Show that $T_{A}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \in A\right\}$ is a stopping time.
7.3.2. If $S$ and $T$ are stopping times, then $S \wedge T=\min \{S, T\}, S \vee T= \max \{S, T\}$, and $S+T$ are also stopping times. In particular, if $t \geq 0$, then $S \wedge t, S \vee t$, and $S+t$ are stopping times.
7.3.3. Let $T_{n}$ be a sequence of stopping times. Show that

$$
\sup _{n} T_{n}, \quad \inf _{n} T_{n}, \quad \limsup _{n} T_{n}, \quad \liminf _{n} T_{n}
$$

are stopping times.
7.3.4 Let $S$ be a stopping time, let $A \in \mathcal{F}_{S}$, and let $R=S$ on $A$ and $R=\infty$ on $A^{c}$. Show that $R$ is a stopping time.
7.3.5. Let $S$ and $T$ be stopping times. $\{S<T\},\{S>T\}$, and $\{S=T\}$ are in $\mathcal{F}_{S}$ (and in $\mathcal{F}_{T}$ ).
7.3.6. Let $S$ and $T$ be stopping times. $\mathcal{F}_{S} \cap \mathcal{F}_{T}=\mathcal{F}_{S \wedge T}$.

### 7.4 Path Properties

In this section, we will use the strong Markov property to derive properties of the zero set $\left\{t: B_{t}=0\right\}$, the hitting times $T_{a}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t}=a\right\}$, and $\max _{0 \leq s \leq t} B_{s}$ for one dimensional Brownian motion.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/322d99c7-eeb2-406f-855e-59cc960bee81-012.jpg?height=504&width=757&top_left_y=1467&top_left_x=700)
Figure 7.2: Simulation one-dimensional Brownian motion.

### 7.4.1 Zeros of Brownian Motion

Let $R_{t}=\inf \left\{u>t: B_{u}=0\right\}$ and let $T_{0}=\inf \left\{u>0: B_{u}=0\right\}$. Now Theorem 7.2.9 implies $P_{x}\left(R_{t}<\infty\right)=1$, so $B\left(R_{t}\right)=0$ and the strong Markov property and Theorem 7.2.5 imply

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{0} \circ \theta_{R_{t}}>0 \mid \mathcal{F}_{R_{t}}\right)=P_{0}\left(T_{0}>0\right)=0
$$

Taking expected value of the last equation, we see that

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{0} \circ \theta_{R_{t}}>0 \text { for some rational } t\right)=0
$$

From this, it follows that if a point $u \in \mathcal{Z}(\omega) \equiv\left\{t: B_{t}(\omega)=0\right\}$ is isolated on the left (i.e., there is a rational $t<u$ so that $(t, u) \cap \mathcal{Z}(\omega)=\emptyset$ ), then it is, with probability one, a decreasing limit of points in $\mathcal{Z}(\omega)$. This shows that the closed set $\mathcal{Z}(\omega)$ has no isolated points and hence must be uncountable. For the last step, see Hewitt and Stromberg (1965), page 72.

If we let $|\mathcal{Z}(\omega)|$ denote the Lebesgue measure of $\mathcal{Z}(\omega)$ then Fubini's theorem implies

$$
E_{x}(|\mathcal{Z}(\omega)| \cap[0, T])=\int_{0}^{T} P_{x}\left(B_{t}=0\right) d t=0
$$

So $\mathcal{Z}(\omega)$ is a set of measure zero.
The last four observations show that $\mathcal{Z}$ is like the Cantor set that is obtained by removing $(1 / 3,2 / 3)$ from $[0,1]$ and then repeatedly removing the middle third from the intervals that remain. The Cantor set is bigger however. Its Hausdorff dimension is $\log 2 / \log 3$, while $\mathcal{Z}$ has dimension $1 / 2$.

### 7.4.2 Hitting times

Theorem 7.4.1. Under $P_{0},\left\{T_{a}, a \geq 0\right\}$ has stationary independent increments.

Proof. The first step is to notice that if $0<a<b$ then

$$
T_{b} \circ \theta_{T_{a}}=T_{b}-T_{a}
$$

so if $f$ is bounded and measurable, the strong Markov property, 7.3.9 and translation invariance imply

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{0}\left(f\left(T_{b}-T_{a}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{T_{a}}\right) & =E_{0}\left(f\left(T_{b}\right) \circ \theta_{T_{a}} \mid \mathcal{F}_{T_{a}}\right) \\
& =E_{a} f\left(T_{b}\right)=E_{0} f\left(T_{b-a}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

To show that the increments are independent, let $a_{0}<a_{1} \ldots<a_{n}$, let $f_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq n$ be bounded and measurable, and let $F_{i}=f_{i}\left(T_{a_{i}}-T_{a_{i-1}}\right)$. Conditioning on $\mathcal{F}_{T_{a n-1}}$ and using the preceding calculation we have

$$
E_{0}\left(\prod_{i=1}^{n} F_{i}\right)=E_{0}\left(\prod_{i=1}^{n-1} F_{i} \cdot E_{0}\left(F_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{T_{a_{n-1}}}\right)\right)=E_{0}\left(\prod_{i=1}^{n-1} F_{i}\right) E_{0} F_{n}
$$

By induction, it follows that $E_{0} \prod_{i=1}^{n} F_{i}=\prod_{i=1}^{n} E_{0} F_{i}$, which implies the desired conclusion.

The scaling relation (7.1.1) implies

$$
\begin{equation*}
T_{a} \stackrel{d}{=} a^{2} T_{1} \tag{7.4.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

Combining Theorem 7.4.1 and (7.4.1), we see that $t_{k}=T_{k}-T_{k-1}$ are i.i.d. and

$$
\frac{t_{1}+\cdots+t_{n}}{n^{2}} \rightarrow T_{1}
$$

so using Theorem 3.8.8, we see that $T_{a}$ has a stable law. Since we are dividng by $n^{2}$ and $T_{a} \geq 0$, the index $\alpha=1 / 2$ and the skewness parameter $\kappa=1$, see (3.8.11).

Without knowing the theory mentioned in the previous paragraph, it is easy to determine the Laplace transform

$$
\varphi_{a}(\lambda)=E_{0} \exp \left(-\lambda T_{a}\right) \quad \text { for } a \geq 0
$$

and reach the same conclusion. To do this, we start by observing that Theorem 7.4.1 implies

$$
\varphi_{x}(\lambda) \varphi_{y}(\lambda)=\varphi_{x+y}(\lambda)
$$

It follows easily from this that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi_{a}(\lambda)=\exp (-a c(\lambda)) \tag{7.4.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Let $c(\lambda)=-\log \varphi_{1}(\lambda)$ so (7.4.2) holds when $a=1$. Using the previous identity with $x=y=2^{-m}$ and induction gives the result for $a=2^{-m}, m \geq 1$. Then, letting $x=k 2^{-m}$ and $y=2^{-m}$ we get the result for $a=(k+1) 2^{-m}$ with $k \geq 1$. Finally, to extend to $a \in[0, \infty)$, note that $a \rightarrow \phi_{a}(\lambda)$ is decreasing.

To identify $c(\lambda)$, we observe that (7.4.1) implies

$$
E \exp \left(-T_{a}\right)=E \exp \left(-a^{2} T_{1}\right)
$$

so $a c(1)=c\left(a^{2}\right)$, i.e., $c(\lambda)=c(1) \sqrt{\lambda}$. Since all of our arguments also apply to $\sigma B_{t}$ we cannot hope to compute $c(1)$. Theorem 7.5.7 will show

$$
\begin{equation*}
E_{0}\left(\exp \left(-\lambda T_{a}\right)\right)=\exp (-a \sqrt{2 \lambda}) \tag{7.4.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Our next goal is to compute the distribution of the hitting times $T_{a}$. This application of the strong Markov property shows why we want to allow the function $Y$ that we apply to the shifted path to depend on the stopping time $S$.

Example 7.4.2. Reflection principle. Let $a>0$ and let $T_{a}=\inf \{t: \left.B_{t}=a\right\}$. Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t\right)=2 P_{0}\left(B_{t} \geq a\right) \tag{7.4.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

Intuitive proof. We observe that if $B_{s}$ hits $a$ at some time $s<t$, then the strong Markov property implies that $B_{t}-B\left(T_{a}\right)$ is independent of what happened before time $T_{a}$. The symmetry of the normal distribution and $P_{a}\left(B_{u}=a\right)=0$ for $u>0$ then imply

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t, B_{t}>a\right)=\frac{1}{2} P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t\right) \tag{7.4.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Rearranging the last equation and using $\left\{B_{t}>a\right\} \subset\left\{T_{a}<t\right\}$ gives

$$
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t\right)=2 P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t, B_{t}>a\right)=2 P_{0}\left(B_{t}>a\right)
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/322d99c7-eeb2-406f-855e-59cc960bee81-015.jpg?height=508&width=761&top_left_y=809&top_left_x=698)
Figure 7.3: Proof by picture of the reflection principle.

Proof. To make the intuitive proof rigorous, we only have to prove (7.4.5). To extract this from the strong Markov property, Theorem 7.3.9, we let

$$
Y_{s}(\omega)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } s<t, \omega(t-s)>a \\ 0 & \text { otherwise }\end{cases}
$$

We do this so that if we let $S=\inf \left\{s<t: B_{s}=a\right\}$ with $\inf \emptyset=\infty$, then

$$
Y_{S}\left(\theta_{S} \omega\right)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } S<t, B_{t}>a \\ 0 & \text { otherwise }\end{cases}
$$

and the strong Markov property implies

$$
E_{0}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} \mid \mathcal{F}_{S}\right)=\varphi\left(B_{S}, S\right) \quad \text { on }\{S<\infty\}=\left\{T_{a}<t\right\}
$$

where $\varphi(x, s)=E_{x} Y_{s} . B_{S}=a$ on $\{S<\infty\}$ and $\varphi(a, s)=1 / 2$ if $s<t$, so taking expected values gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t, B_{t} \geq a\right) & =E_{0}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} ; S<\infty\right) \\
& =E_{0}\left(E_{0}\left(Y_{S} \circ \theta_{S} \mid \mathcal{F}_{S}\right) ; S<\infty\right)=E_{0}\left(1 / 2 ; T_{a}<t\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

which proves (7.4.5).

Using (7.4.4), we can compute the probability density of $T_{a}$. We begin by noting that

$$
P\left(T_{a} \leq t\right)=2 P_{0}\left(B_{t} \geq a\right)=2 \int_{a}^{\infty}(2 \pi t)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2 t\right) d x
$$

then change variables $x=\left(t^{1 / 2} a\right) / s^{1 / 2}$ to get

$$
\begin{align*}
P_{0}\left(T_{a} \leq t\right) & =2 \int_{t}^{0}(2 \pi t)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-a^{2} / 2 s\right)\left(-t^{1 / 2} a / 2 s^{3 / 2}\right) d s \\
& =\int_{0}^{t}\left(2 \pi s^{3}\right)^{-1 / 2} a \exp \left(-a^{2} / 2 s\right) d s \tag{7.4.6}
\end{align*}
$$

Using the last formula, we can compute:
Example 7.4.3. The distribution of $L=\sup \left\{t \leq 1: B_{t}=0\right\}$. By (7.2.4),

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{0}(L \leq s) & =\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} p_{s}(0, x) P_{x}\left(T_{0}>1-s\right) d x \\
& =2 \int_{0}^{\infty}(2 \pi s)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2 s\right) \int_{1-s}^{\infty}\left(2 \pi r^{3}\right)^{-1 / 2} x \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2 r\right) d r d x \\
& =\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{1-s}^{\infty}\left(s r^{3}\right)^{-1 / 2} \int_{0}^{\infty} x \exp \left(-x^{2}(r+s) / 2 r s\right) d x d r \\
& =\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{1-s}^{\infty}\left(s r^{3}\right)^{-1 / 2} r s /(r+s) d r
\end{aligned}
$$

Our next step is to let $t=s /(r+s)$ to convert the integral over $r \in [1-s, \infty)$ into one over $t \in[0, s] . d t=-s /(r+s)^{2} d r$, so to make the calculations easier we first rewrite the integral as

$$
=\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{1-s}^{\infty}\left(\frac{(r+s)^{2}}{r s}\right)^{1 / 2} \frac{s}{(r+s)^{2}} d r
$$

and then change variables to get

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}(L \leq s)=\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{0}^{s}(t(1-t))^{-1 / 2} d t=\frac{2}{\pi} \arcsin (\sqrt{s}) \tag{7.4.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $L$ is the time of the last zero before time 1, it is remarkable that the density is symmetric about $1 / 2$ and blows up at 0 . The arcsin may remind the reader of the limit theorem for $L_{2 n}=\sup \left\{m \leq 2 n: S_{m}=0\right\}$ given in Theorem 4.9.5. We will see in Section 8.6 that our new result is a consequence of the old one.

## Exercises

7.4.1. Let $B_{t}=\left(B_{t}^{1}, B_{t}^{2}\right)$ be a two-dimensional Brownian motion starting from 0. Let $T_{a}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t}^{2}=a\right\}$. Show that (i) $B^{1}\left(T_{a}\right), a \geq 0$ has independent increments and $B^{1}\left(T_{a}\right)={ }_{d} a B^{1}\left(T_{1}\right)$. Use this to conclude that $B^{1}\left(T_{a}\right)$ has a Cauchy distirubtion.
7.4.2. Use (7.2.3) to show that $R=\inf \left\{t>1: B_{t}=0\right\}$ has probability density

$$
P_{0}(R=1+t)=1 /\left(\pi t^{1 / 2}(1+t)\right)
$$

7.4.3. (a) Generalize the proof of (7.4.5) to conclude that if $u<v \leq a$ then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t, u<B_{t}<v\right)=P_{0}\left(2 a-v<B_{t}<2 a-u\right) \tag{7.4.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

(b) Let ( $u, v$ ) shrink down to $x$ in (7.4.8) to conclude that for $a<x$

$$
P_{0}\left(T_{a}<t, B_{t}=x\right)=p_{t}(0,2 a-x)
$$

i.e., the (subprobability) density for $B_{t}$ on $\left\{T_{a}<t\right\}$. (c) Use the fact that $\left\{T_{a}<t\right\}=\left\{M_{t}>a\right\}$ and differentiate with respect to $a$ gives the joint density

$$
f_{\left(M_{t}, B_{t}\right)}(a, x)=\frac{2(2 a-x)}{\sqrt{2 \pi t^{3}}} e^{-(2 a-x)^{2} / 2 t}
$$

7.4.4. Let $A_{s, t}$ be the event Brownian motion has at least one zero in $[s, t]$. Show that $P_{0}\left(A_{s, t}\right)=(2 / \pi) \arccos (\sqrt{s / t})$. Hint: To get started note that

$$
P_{0}\left(A_{s, t}\right)=2 \int_{0}^{\infty}(2 \pi s)^{-1 / 2} e^{-x^{2} / 2} P_{x}\left(T_{0} \leq t-s\right) d x
$$

and use the formula for the density function of $T_{x}$. At the end you may need some trig to convert arctan into arccos.

Remark. Using the previous exercise it is easy to see that if $s \geq 1$ then $P\left(A_{s, s+h}\right) \leq C h^{1 / 2}$. Thus if we divide the interval [1,2] into $n$ equal pieces then expected number of intervals that contains zeros is $\leq C n^{-1 / 2}$. It follows that the Hausdorff dimension of the zero set is at most $1 / 2$.

### 7.5 Martingales

At the end of Section 5.7 we used martingales to study the hitting times of random walks. The same methods can be used on Brownian motion once we prove:

Theorem 7.5.1. Let $X_{t}$ be a right continuous martingale adapted to a right continuous filtration. If $T$ is a bounded stopping time, then $E X_{T}= E X_{0}$.

Proof. Let $n$ be an integer so that $P(T \leq n-1)=1$. As in the proof of the strong Markov property, let $T_{m}=\left(\left[2^{m} T\right]+1\right) / 2^{m} . Y_{k}^{m}=X\left(k 2^{-m}\right)$ is a martingale with respect to $\mathcal{F}_{k}^{m}=\mathcal{F}\left(k 2^{-m}\right)$ and $S_{m}=2^{m} T_{m}$ is a stopping time for $\left(Y_{k}^{m}, \mathcal{F}_{k}^{m}\right)$, so by Exercise 4.4.4

$$
X\left(T_{m}\right)=Y_{S_{m}}^{m}=E\left(Y_{n 2^{m}}^{m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{S_{m}}^{m}\right)=E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}\left(T_{m}\right)\right)
$$

As $m \uparrow \infty, X\left(T_{m}\right) \rightarrow X(T)$ by right continuity and $\mathcal{F}\left(T_{m}\right) \downarrow \mathcal{F}(T)$ by Theorem 7.3.7, so it follows from Theorem 4.7.3 that

$$
X(T)=E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}(T)\right)
$$

Taking expected values gives $E X(T)=E X_{n}=E X_{0}$, since $X_{n}$ is a martingale.

Theorem 7.5.2. $B_{t}$ is a martingale w.r.t. the $\sigma$-fields $\mathcal{F}_{t}$ defined in Section 8.2.

Note: We will use these $\sigma$-fields in all of the martingale results but will not mention them explicitly in the statements.

Proof. The Markov property implies that

$$
E_{x}\left(B_{t} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right)=E_{B_{s}}\left(B_{t-s}\right)=B_{s}
$$

since symmetry implies $E_{y} B_{u}=y$ for all $u \geq 0$.
From Theorem 7.5.2, it follows immediately that we have:
Theorem 7.5.3. If $a<x<b$ then $P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}\right)=(b-x) /(b-a)$.
Proof. Let $T=T_{a} \wedge T_{b}$. Theorem 7.2.8 implies that $T<\infty$ a.s. Using Theorems 7.5.1 and 7.5.2, it follows that $x=E_{x} B(T \wedge t)$. Letting $t \rightarrow \infty$ and using the bounded convergence theorem, it follows that

$$
x=a P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}\right)+b\left(1-P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}\right)\right)
$$

Solving for $P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}\right)$ now gives the desired result.
Theorem 7.5.4. $B_{t}^{2}-t$ is a martingale.
Proof. Writing $B_{t}^{2}=\left(B_{s}+B_{t}-B_{s}\right)^{2}$ we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x}\left(B_{t}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) & =E_{x}\left(B_{s}^{2}+2 B_{s}\left(B_{t}-B_{s}\right)+\left(B_{t}-B_{s}\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) \\
& =B_{s}^{2}+2 B_{s} E_{x}\left(B_{t}-B_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right)+E_{x}\left(\left(B_{t}-B_{s}\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) \\
& =B_{s}^{2}+0+(t-s)
\end{aligned}
$$

since $B_{t}-B_{s}$ is independent of $\mathcal{F}_{s}$ and has mean 0 and variance $t-s$.
Theorem 7.5.5. Let $T=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(a, b)\right\}$, where $a<0<b$.

$$
E_{0} T=-a b
$$

Proof Theorem 7.5.1 and 7.5.4 imply $\left.E_{0}\left(B^{2}(T \wedge t)\right)=E_{0}(T \wedge t)\right)$. Letting $t \rightarrow \infty$ and using the monotone convergence theorem gives $E_{0}(T \wedge t) \uparrow E_{0} T$. Using the bounded convergence theorem and Theorem 7.5.3, we have

$$
E_{0} B^{2}(T \wedge t) \rightarrow E_{0} B_{T}^{2}=a^{2} \frac{b}{b-a}+b^{2} \frac{-a}{b-a}=a b \frac{a-b}{b-a}=-a b
$$

Theorem 7.5.6. $\exp \left(\theta B_{t}-\left(\theta^{2} t / 2\right)\right)$ is a martingale.
Proof. Bringing $\exp \left(\theta B_{s}\right)$ outside

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x}\left(\exp \left(\theta B_{t}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) & =\exp \left(\theta B_{s}\right) E\left(\exp \left(\theta\left(B_{t}-B_{s}\right)\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) \\
& =\exp \left(\theta B_{s}\right) \exp \left(\theta^{2}(t-s) / 2\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

since $B_{t}-B_{s}$ is independent of $\mathcal{F}_{s}$ and has a normal distribution with mean 0 and variance $t-s$.

Theorem 7.5.7. If $T_{a}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t}=a\right\}$ then $E_{0} \exp \left(-\lambda T_{a}\right)=\exp (-a \sqrt{2 \lambda})$.
Proof. Theorem 7.5.1 and 7.5.6 imply that $1=E_{0} \exp \left(\theta B(T \wedge t)-\theta^{2}\left(T_{a} \wedge\right.\right. t) / 2$ ). Taking $\theta=\sqrt{2 \lambda}$, letting $t \rightarrow \infty$ and using the bounded convergence theorem gives $1=E_{0} \exp \left(a \sqrt{2 \lambda}-\lambda T_{a}\right)$.

Theorem 7.5.8. If $u(t, x)$ is a polynomial in $t$ and $x$ with

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{\partial u}{\partial t}+\frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial^{2} u}{\partial x^{2}}=0 \tag{7.5.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

then $u\left(t, B_{t}\right)$ is a martingale.
Proof. Let $p_{t}(x, y)=(2 \pi)^{-1 / 2} t^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-(y-x)^{2} / 2 t\right)$. The first step is to check that $p_{t}$ satisfies the heat equation: $\partial p_{t} / \partial t=(1 / 2) \partial^{2} p_{t} / \partial y^{2}$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\partial p}{\partial t} & =-\frac{1}{2} t^{-1} p_{t}+\frac{(y-x)^{2}}{2 t^{2}} p_{t} \\
\frac{\partial p}{\partial y} & =-\frac{y-x}{t} p_{t} \\
\frac{\partial^{2} p}{\partial y^{2}} & =-\frac{1}{t} p_{t}+\frac{(y-x)^{2}}{t^{2}} p_{t}
\end{aligned}
$$

Interchanging $\partial / \partial t$ and $\int$, and using the heat equation

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\partial}{\partial t} E_{x} u\left(t, B_{t}\right) & =\int \frac{\partial}{\partial t}\left(p_{t}(x, y) u(t, y)\right) d y \\
& =\int \frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial}{\partial y^{2}} p_{t}(x, y) u(t, y)+p_{t}(x, y) \frac{\partial}{\partial t} u(t, y) d y
\end{aligned}
$$

Integrating by parts twice the above

$$
=\int p_{t}(x, y)\left(\frac{\partial}{\partial t}+\frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial}{\partial y^{2}}\right) u(t, y) d y=0
$$

Since $u(t, y)$ is a polynomial there is no question about the convergence of integrals and there is no contribution from the boundary terms when we integrate by parts.

At this point we have shown that $t \rightarrow E_{x} u\left(t, B_{t}\right)$ is constant. To check the martingale property we need to show that

$$
E\left(u\left(t, B_{t}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right)=u\left(s, B_{s}\right)
$$

To do this we note that $v(r, x)=u(s+r, x)$ satisfies $\partial v / \partial r=(1 / 2) \partial v^{2} / \partial x^{2}$, so using the Markov property

$$
\begin{aligned}
E\left(u\left(t, B_{t}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) & =E\left(v\left(t-s, B_{t-s}\right) \circ \theta_{s} \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right) \\
& =E_{B(s)} v\left(t-s, B_{t-s}\right)=v\left(0, B_{s}\right)=u\left(s, B_{s}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

where in the next to last step we have used the previously proved constancy of the expected value.

Examples of polynomials that satisfy (7.5.1) are $x, x^{2}-t, x^{3}-3 t x$, $x^{4}-6 x^{2} t+3 t^{2} \ldots$ The result can also be extended to $\exp \left(\theta x-\theta^{2} t / 2\right)$. The only place we used the fact that $u(t, x)$ was a polynomial was in checking the convergence of the integral to justify integration by parts.

Theorem 7.5.9. If $T=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(-a, a)\right\}$ then $E T^{2}=5 a^{4} / 3$.
Proof. Theorem 7.5.1 implies

$$
E\left(B(T \wedge t)^{4}-6(T \wedge t) B(T \wedge t)^{2}\right)=-3 E(T \wedge t)^{2} .
$$

From Theorem 7.5.5, we know that $E T=a^{2}<\infty$. Letting $t \rightarrow \infty$, using the dominated convergence theorem on the left-hand side, and the monotone convergence theorem on the right gives

$$
a^{4}-6 a^{2} E T=-3 E\left(T^{2}\right)
$$

Plugging in $E T=a^{2}$ gives the desired result.

## Exercises

7.5.1. Let $T=\inf \left\{B_{t} \notin(-a, a)\right\}$. Show that

$$
E_{0} \exp (-\lambda T)=1 / \cosh (a \sqrt{2 \lambda})
$$

7.5.2. The point of this exercise is to get information about the amount of time it takes Brownian motion with drift $-b, X_{t} \equiv B_{t}-b t$ to hit level $a$. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t}=a+b t\right\}$, where $a>0$. (i) Use the martingale $\exp \left(\theta B_{t}-\theta^{2} t / 2\right)$ with $\theta=b+\left(b^{2}+2 \lambda\right)^{1 / 2}$ to show

$$
E_{0} \exp (-\lambda \tau)=\exp \left(-a\left\{b+\left(b^{2}+2 \lambda\right)^{1 / 2}\right\}\right)
$$

Letting $\lambda \rightarrow 0$ gives $P_{0}(\tau<\infty)=\exp (-2 a b)$.
7.5.3. Let $\sigma=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(a, b)\right\}$ and let $\lambda>0$. Use the strong Markov property to show

$$
E_{x} \exp \left(-\lambda T_{a}\right)=E_{x}\left(e^{-\lambda \sigma} ; T_{a}<T_{b}\right)+E_{x}\left(e^{-\lambda \sigma} ; T_{b}<T_{a}\right) E_{b} \exp \left(-\lambda T_{a}\right)
$$

(ii) Interchange the roles of a and $b$ to get a second equation, use Theorem 7.5.7, and solve to get

$$
\begin{aligned}
& E_{x}\left(e^{-\lambda \sigma} ; T_{a}<T_{b}\right)=\sinh (\sqrt{2 \lambda}(b-x)) / \sinh (\sqrt{2 \lambda}(b-a)) \\
& E_{x}\left(e^{-\lambda \sigma} ; T_{b}<T_{a}\right)=\sinh (\sqrt{2 \lambda}(x-a)) / \sinh (\sqrt{2 \lambda}(b-a))
\end{aligned}
$$

7.5.4. If $T=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(a, b)\right\}$, where $a<0<b$ and $a \neq-b$, then $T$ and $B_{T}^{2}$ are not independent, so we cannot calculate $E T^{2}$ as we did in the proof of Theorem 7.5.9. Use the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality to estimate $E\left(T B_{T}^{2}\right)$ and conclude (i) $E T^{2} \leq 4 E\left(B_{T}^{4}\right)$ and (ii) $E B_{T}^{4} \leq 36 E T^{2}$
7.5.5. Find a martingale of the form $B_{t}^{6}-c_{1} t B_{t}^{4}+c_{2} t^{2} B_{t}^{2}-c_{3} t^{3}$ and use it to compute the third moment of $T=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(-a, a)\right\}$.
7.5.6. Show that $(1+t)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(B_{t}^{2} / 2(1+t)\right)$ is a martingale and use this to conclude that $\limsup _{t \rightarrow \infty} B_{t} /((1+t) \log (1+t))^{1 / 2} \leq 1$ a.s.

### 7.6 Itô's formula*

Our goal in this section is to prove three versions of Itô's formula. The first one, for one dimensional Brownian motion is

Theorem 7.6.1. Suppose that $f \in C^{2}$, i.e., it has two continuous derivatives. The with probability one, for all $t \geq 0$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
f\left(B_{t}\right)-f\left(B_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime \prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d s \tag{7.6.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

Of course, as part of the derivation we have to explain the meaning of the first term on the right-hand side. In contrast if $A_{s}$ is continuous and has bounded variation on each finite interval, and $f \in C^{1}$ :

$$
f\left(A_{t}\right)-f\left(A_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(A_{s}\right) d A_{s}
$$

where the Riemann-Steiltjes integral on the right-hand side is

$$
\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \sum_{i=1}^{k(n)} f^{\prime}\left(A_{t_{i-1}^{n}}\right)\left[A\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)-A\left(t_{i-1}^{n}\right)\right]
$$

over a sequence of partitions $0=t_{0}^{n}<t_{1}^{n}<\ldots<t_{k(n)}^{n}=t$ with mesh $\max _{i} t_{i}^{n}-t_{i-1}^{n} \rightarrow 0$.

Proof. To derive (7.6.1) we let $t_{i}^{n}=t i / 2^{n}$ for $0 \leq i \leq k(n)=2^{n}$. From calculus we know that for any $a$ and $b$, there is a $c(a, b)$ in between $a$ and $b$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
f(b)-f(a)=(b-a) f^{\prime}(a)+\frac{1}{2}(b-a)^{2} f^{\prime \prime}(c(a, b)) \tag{7.6.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

Using the calculus fact, it follows that

$$
\begin{align*}
f\left(B_{t}\right)-f\left(B_{0}\right) & =\sum_{i} f\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}\right)-f\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right) \\
& =\sum_{i} f^{\prime}\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)  \tag{7.6.3}\\
& +\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i} g_{i}^{n}(\omega)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)^{2}
\end{align*}
$$

where $g_{i}^{n}(\omega)=f^{\prime \prime}\left(c\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}, B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}\right)\right)$. Comparing (7.6.3) with (7.6.1), it becomes clear that we want to show

$$
\begin{align*}
\sum_{i} f^{\prime}\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right) & \rightarrow \int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}  \tag{7.6.4}\\
\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i} g_{i}(\omega)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)^{2} & \rightarrow \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime \prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d s \tag{7.6.5}
\end{align*}
$$

in probability as $n \rightarrow \infty$.
We will prove the result first under the assumption that $\left|f^{\prime}(x)\right|,\left|f^{\prime \prime}(x)\right| \leq K$. Let

$$
I_{m}^{1} \equiv \sum_{i} f^{\prime}\left(B_{t_{i}^{m}}\right)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{m}}-B_{t_{i}^{m}}\right)
$$

In order to compare $I_{m}^{1}$ and $I_{n}^{1}$ with $n \geq m$, we want to further subdivide the intervals of length $t 2^{-m}$ to be of length $t 2^{-n}$. To leave the sum unchanged we have to redefine things. Let $i(n, m, j)=\left[2^{m} j / 2^{n}\right] / 2^{m}$ and $H_{j}^{m}=f^{\prime}\left(B_{i(n, m, j)}\right)$. If we let $K_{j}^{n}=f^{\prime}\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)$ then we have

$$
I_{m}^{1}-I_{n}^{1}=\sum_{j}\left(H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right)\left(B_{t_{j+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{j}^{n}}\right)
$$

Squaring we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(I_{m}^{1}-I_{n}^{1}\right)^{2} & =\sum_{j}\left(H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right)^{2}\left(B_{t_{j+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{j}^{n}}\right)^{2} \\
& +2 \sum_{j<k}\left(H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right)\left(H_{k}^{m}-K_{k}^{n}\right)\left(B_{t_{j+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{j}^{n}}\right)\left(B_{t_{k+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{k}^{n}}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

The next step is to take expected value. The expected value of the second sum vanishes because if we take expected value of the $j, k$ term with respect to $\mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}$ the first three terms can be taken outside the expected value

$$
\left(H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right)\left(H_{k}^{m}-K_{k}^{n}\right)\left(B_{t_{j+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{j}^{n}}\right) E\left(B_{t_{k+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{k}^{n}} \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right)=0
$$

Conditioning on $\mathcal{F}_{t_{j}^{n}}$ in the first sum we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left(I_{m}^{1}-I_{n}^{1}\right)^{2}=\sum_{j} E\left(H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right)^{2} \cdot 2^{-n} t \tag{7.6.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $\left|i(n, m, j) / 2^{m}-j / 2^{n}\right| \leq 1 / 2^{m}$ and $\left|f^{\prime \prime}(x)\right| \leq K$, we have

$$
\sup _{j \leq 2^{n}}\left|H_{j}^{m}-K_{j}^{n}\right| \leq K \sup \left\{\left|B_{s}-B_{r}\right|: 0 \leq r \leq s \leq t,|s-r| \leq 2^{-m}\right\}
$$

Since Brownian motion is uniformly continuous on $[0, t]$ and $E \sup _{s \leq t}\left|B_{s}\right|^{2}< \infty$ it follows from the dominated convergence theorem that

$$
\lim _{m, n \rightarrow \infty} E\left(I_{m}^{1}-I_{n}^{1}\right)^{2}=0
$$

This shows that $I_{m}^{1}$ is a Cauchy sequence in $L^{2}$ so there is an $I_{\infty}^{1}$ so that $I_{m}^{1} \rightarrow I_{\infty}^{1}$ in $L^{2}$. The limit defines $\int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}$ as a limit of approximating Riemann sums.

To prove (7.6.5), we let $G_{s}^{n}=g_{i}^{n}(\omega)=f^{\prime \prime}\left(c\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}, B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}\right)\right)$ when $s \in \left(t_{i}^{n}, t_{i+1}^{n}\right]$ and let

$$
A_{s}^{n}=\sum_{t_{i+1}^{n} \leq s}\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)^{2}
$$

so that

$$
\sum_{i} g_{i}^{n}(\omega)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)^{2}=\int_{0}^{t} G_{s}^{n} d A_{s}^{n}
$$

and what we want to show is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{0}^{t} G_{s}^{n} d A_{s}^{n} \rightarrow \int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime \prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d s \tag{7.6.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

To do this we begin by observing that continuity of $f^{\prime \prime}$ implies that if $s_{n} \rightarrow s$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$ then $G_{s_{n}}^{n} \rightarrow f^{\prime \prime}\left(B_{s}\right)$, while Exercise 7.1.6 implies that $A_{s}^{n}$ converges almost surely to $s$. At this point, we can fix $\omega$ and deduce (7.6.7) from the following simple result:

Lemma 7.6.2. If (i) measures $\mu_{n}$ on $[0, t]$ converge weakly to $\mu_{\infty}$, a finite measure, and (ii) $g_{n}$ is a sequence of functions with $\left|g_{n}\right| \leq K$ that have the property that whenever $s_{n} \in[0, t] \rightarrow s$ we have $g_{n}\left(s_{n}\right) \rightarrow g(s)$, then as $n \rightarrow \infty$

$$
\int g_{n} d \mu_{n} \rightarrow \int g d \mu_{\infty}
$$

Proof. By letting $\mu_{n}^{\prime}(A)=\mu_{n}(A) / \mu_{n}([0, t])$, we can assume that all the $\mu_{n}$ are probability measures. A standard construction (see Theorem 3.2.8) shows that there is a sequence of random variables $X_{n}$ with distribution $\mu_{n}$ so that $X_{n} \rightarrow X_{\infty}$ a.s. as $n \rightarrow \infty$ The convergence of $g_{n}$ to $g$ implies $g_{n}\left(X_{n}\right) \rightarrow g\left(X_{\infty}\right)$, so the result follows from the bounded convergence theorem.

Lemma 7.6.2 is the last piece in the proof of Theorem under the additional assumptions that $\left|f^{\prime}(x)\right|,\left|f^{\prime \prime}(x)\right| \leq M$. Tracing back through the proof, we see that Lemma 7.6.2 implies (7.6.7), which in turn completes the proof of (7.6.5). So adding (7.6.4) and using (7.6.3) gives that

$$
f\left(B_{t}\right)-f\left(B_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime \prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d s \text { a.s. }
$$

To remove the assumed bounds on the derivatives, we let $M$ be large and define $f_{M}$ with $f_{M}=f$ on $[-M, M]$ and $\left|f_{M}^{\prime}\right|,\left|f_{M}^{\prime \prime}\right| \leq M$. The formula holds for $f_{M}$ but on $\left\{\sup _{s \leq t}\left|B_{s}\right| \leq M\right\}$ there is no difference in the formula between using $f_{M}$ and $f$.

Example 7.6.3. Taking $f=x^{2}$ in (7.6.1) we have

$$
B_{t}^{2}-B_{0}^{2}=\int_{0}^{t} 2 B_{s} d B_{s}+t
$$

so if $B_{0}=0$, we have

$$
\int_{0}^{t} 2 B_{s} d B_{s}=B_{t}^{2}-t
$$

In contrast to the calculus formula $\int_{0}^{a} 2 x d x=a^{2}$.
Direct proof. Using the subdivision we introduced previous we can write

$$
\begin{aligned}
B_{t}^{2} & =\left(\sum _ { i = 0 } ^ { 2 ^ { n } - 1 } \left(B\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right)^{2}\right.\right. \\
& +\sum_{i=0}^{2^{n}-1} \sum_{j=0}^{2^{n}-1}\left(B \left(t_{i+1}^{n}-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\left(B \left(t_{j+1}^{n}-B\left(t_{j}^{n}\right)\right.\right.\right.\right.
\end{aligned}
$$

If we sum over $j<i$ this becomes

$$
\sum_{i=0}^{2^{n}-1} B\left(t^{n}-i\right)\left(B\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right) \rightarrow \int_{0}^{t} B_{s} d B_{s}\right.
$$

We get the same limit for the sum over $i<j$. When we sum over $i=j$

$$
\sum_{i=0}^{2^{n}-1}\left(B \left(t_{i+1}^{n}-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)^{2} \rightarrow t\right.\right.
$$

To give one reason why the answer cannot be $B_{t}^{2} / 2$, we note that
Theorem 7.6.4. If $g$ is continuous and $E \int_{0}^{t}\left|g\left(B_{s}\right)\right|^{2} d s<\infty$ then $\int_{0}^{t} g\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}$ is a continuous martingale.

If $g$ grows too fast at $\infty$ then the integrability condition might not hold. Too deal with this we introduce a weaker concept: $M_{t}$ is a local martingale if there is an increasing sequence of stopping times $T_{n} \uparrow \infty$ so that $M\left(t \wedge T_{n}\right)$ is a martingale. If $g$ is continuous then the stochastic integral is always a local martingale.

Proof. We first prove the result assuming $|g| \leq K$. Let $t_{i}^{n}=t i / 2^{n}$ for $0 \leq i \leq 2^{n}$, let $J=\max \left\{i: t_{i+1}^{n} \leq s\right\}$ and let

$$
I_{n}^{1}(s)=\sum_{i=0}^{J} g\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)\left(B_{t_{i+1}^{n}}-B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)+g\left(B_{t_{J+1}^{n}}\right)\left(B_{s}-B_{t_{J+1}^{n}}\right)
$$

It is easy to check that $I_{n}^{1}(s), s \leq t$ is a continuous martingale with

$$
E I_{n}^{1}(t)^{2}=\sum_{0 \leq i<2^{n}} E\left[g\left(B_{t_{i}^{n}}\right)^{2}\right] \cdot t 2^{-n}
$$

The $L^{2}$ maximal inequality implies that

$$
E\left(\sup _{s \leq t}\left|I_{m}^{1}(s)-I_{n}^{1}(s)\right|^{2}\right) \leq 4 E\left(I_{m}^{1}-I_{n}^{1}\right)^{2}
$$

The right-hand side $\rightarrow 0$ by the previous proof, so $I_{m}^{1}(s) \rightarrow I_{\infty}^{1}(s)$ uniformly on $[0, t]$. Let $Y \in \mathcal{F}_{r}$ with $E Y^{2}<\infty$. Since $I_{n}^{1}(s)$ is a martingale, if $r<s<t$ we have

$$
E\left[Y\left\{I_{n}^{1}(s)-I_{n}^{1}(r)\right\}\right]=0
$$

From this and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality we conclude that

$$
E\left[Y\left\{I_{\infty}^{1}(s)-I_{\infty}^{1}(r)\right\}\right]=0
$$

Since this holds for $Y=1_{A}$ with $A \in \mathcal{F}_{r}$, we have proved the desired result under the assumption $|g| \leq K$.

Approximating $g$ by $g_{M}=g$ on $[-M, M]$ and $g_{M}$ bounded as before and stopping at time $T_{M}=\inf \left\{t:\left|B_{t}\right| \geq M\right\}$ we can conclude that if $g$ is continuous then

$$
\int_{0}^{s \wedge T_{M}} g\left(B_{r}\right) d B_{r}
$$

is a continuous martingale with

$$
E\left(\int_{0}^{s \wedge T_{M}} f^{\prime}\left(B_{r}\right) d B_{r}\right)^{2}=E \int_{0}^{s \wedge T_{M}} f^{\prime}\left(B_{r}\right)^{2} d r
$$

Letting $M \rightarrow \infty$ it follows that if $E \int_{0}^{t}\left|f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right)\right|^{2} d s<\infty$ then $\int_{0}^{t} f^{\prime}\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}$ is a continuous martingale.

Our second version of Itô's formula allows $f$ to depend on $t$ as well
Theorem 7.6.5. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$, i.e., it has continuous partial derivatives up to order two. Then with probability 1 , for all $t \geq 0$

$$
\begin{align*}
f\left(t, B_{t}\right)-f\left(0, B_{0}\right) & =\int_{0}^{t} \frac{\partial f}{\partial t}\left(s, B_{s}\right) d s+\int_{0}^{t} \frac{\partial f}{\partial x}\left(s, B_{s}\right) d B_{s} \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{t} \frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial x^{2}}\left(s, B_{s}\right) d s \tag{7.6.8}
\end{align*}
$$

Proof. Perhaps the simplest way to give a completely rigorous proof of this result (and the next one) is to prove the result when $f$ is a polynomial in $t$ and $x$ and then note that for any $f \in C^{2}$ and $M<\infty$, we can find polynomials $\phi_{n}$ so that $\phi_{n}$ and all of its partial derivatives up to order two converge to those of $f$ uniformly on $[0, t] \times[-M, M]$. Here, we will content ourselves to explain why this result is true.

Expanding to second order and being a little less precise about the error term

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(t, b) & -f(s, a) \approx \frac{\partial f}{\partial t}(s, a)(t-s)+\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}(s, a)(b-a) \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial x^{2}}(s, a)(b-a)^{2}+\frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial t \partial x}(s, a)(t-s)(b-a)+\frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial t^{2}}(s, a)(t-s)^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

Let $t$ be a fixed positive number and let $t_{k}^{n}=t k / 2^{n}$. As before, we first prove the result under the assumption that all of the first and second order partial derivatives are bounded by $K$. The details of the convergence of the first three terms to their limits is similar to the previous proof. To see that the fifth term vanishes in the limit, use the fact that $t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}=2^{-n}$ to conclude that

$$
\sum_{i}\left|\frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial x^{2}}\left(t_{i}^{n}, B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\right|\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}\right)^{2} \leq K t 2^{-n} \rightarrow 0
$$

To treat the fourth term, we first consider

$$
V_{n}^{1,1}(t)=\sum_{i}\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}\right)\left|B\left(t_{i+1}^{n}\right)-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right|
$$

$E V_{n}^{1,1}(t)=\sum_{i} C_{1}\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}\right)^{3 / 2}$ where $C_{1}=E \chi^{1 / 2}$ and $\chi$ is a standard normal. Arguing as before,

$$
E \sum_{i}\left|\frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial x^{2}}\left(t_{i}^{n}, B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\right|\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}\right)\left|B\left(t_{i+1}^{n}\right)-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right| \leq K C_{1} t \cdot 2^{-n / 2} \rightarrow 0
$$

Using Markov's inequality now we see that if $\epsilon>0$

$$
P\left(\sum_{i}\left|\frac{\partial^{2} f}{\partial x^{2}}\left(t_{i}^{n}, B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\right|\left(t_{i+1}^{n}-t_{i}^{n}\right)\left|B\left(t_{i+1}^{n}\right)-B\left(t_{i}^{n}\right)\right|>\epsilon\right) \leq K C_{1} t 2^{-n / 2} / \epsilon \rightarrow 0
$$

and hence the sum converges to 0 in probability.
To remove the assumption that the partial derivatives are bounded, let $T_{M}=\min \left\{t:\left|B_{t}\right|>M\right\}$ and use the previous calculation to conclude that the formula holds up to time $T_{M} \wedge t$.

Example 7.6.6. Using (7.6.8) we can prove Theorem 7.5.8: if $u(t, x)$ is a polynomial in $t$ and $x$ with $\partial u / \partial t+(1 / 2) \partial^{2} u / \partial x^{2}=0$ then Itô's formula implies

$$
u\left(t, B_{t}\right)-u\left(0, B_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{t} \frac{\partial u}{\partial x}\left(s, B_{s}\right) d B_{s}
$$

Since $\partial u / \partial x$ is a polynomial it satisfies the integrability condition in Theorem 7.6.4, $u\left(t, B_{t}\right)$ is a martingale. To get a new conclusion, let $u(t, x)=\exp (\mu t+\sigma x)$. Exponential Brownian motion $X_{t}=u\left(t, B_{t}\right)$ is often used as a model of the price of a stock. Since

$$
\frac{\partial u}{\partial t}=\mu u \quad \frac{\partial u}{\partial x}=\sigma u \quad \frac{\partial^{2} u}{\partial x^{2}}=\sigma^{2} u
$$

Itô's formula gives

$$
X_{t}-X_{0}=\int_{0}^{t}\left(\mu-\frac{\sigma^{2}}{2}\right) X_{s} d s+\int_{0}^{t} \sigma X_{s} d B_{s}
$$

From this we see that if $\mu=\sigma^{2} / 2$ then $X_{t}$ is a martingale, but this formula gives more information about how $X_{t}$ behaves.

Our third and final version of Itô's formula is for multi-dimensional Brownian motion. To simplify notation we write $D_{i} f=\partial f / \partial x_{i}$ and $D_{i j}=\partial^{2} f / \partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}$

Theorem 7.6.7. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$. Then with probability 1, for all $t \geq 0$

$$
\begin{align*}
f\left(t, B_{t}\right)-f\left(0, B_{0}\right) & =\int_{0}^{t} \frac{\partial f}{\partial t}\left(s, B_{s}\right) d s+\sum_{i=1}^{d} \int_{0}^{t} D_{i} f\left(B_{s}\right) d B_{s}^{i} \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \int_{0}^{t} D_{i i} f\left(B_{s}\right) d s \tag{7.6.9}
\end{align*}
$$

Proof. Let $x_{0}=t$ and write $D_{0} f=\partial f / \partial x_{0}$. Expanding to second order and again not worrying about the error term

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(y)-f(x) & \approx \sum_{i} D_{i} f(x)\left(y_{i}-x_{i}\right) \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i} D_{i i} f(x)\left(y_{i}-x_{i}\right)^{2}+\sum_{i<j} D_{i j} f(x)\left(y_{i}-x_{i}\right)\left(y_{j}-x_{j}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Let $t$ be a fixed positive number and let $t_{i}^{n} t i / 2^{n}$. The details of the convergence of the first two terms to their limits are similar to the previous two proofs. To handle the third term, we let

$$
F_{i, j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)=D_{i j} f\left(B\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)
$$

and note that independence of coordinates implies

$$
\begin{aligned}
& E\left(F_{i, j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\left(B^{i}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right) \cdot\left(B^{j}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right) \\
& \quad=F_{i, j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right) E\left(\left(B^{i}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right) \cdot\left(B^{j}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right)=0
\end{aligned}
$$

Fix $i<j$ and let $G_{k}=F_{i, j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\left(B^{i}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right) \cdot\left(B^{j}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)$ The last calculation shows that

$$
E\left(\sum_{k} G_{k}\right)=0
$$

To compute the variance of the sum we note that

$$
E\left(\sum_{k} G_{k}\right)^{2}=E \sum_{k} G_{k}^{2}+2 E \sum_{k<\ell} G_{k} G_{\ell}
$$

Again we first comlete the proof under the assumption that $\left|\partial^{2} f / \partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}\right| \leq C$. In this case, conditioning on $\mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}$ in order to bring $F_{i, j}^{2}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)$ outside the first term,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& E\left(F_{i, j}^{2}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\left(B^{i}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)^{2} \cdot\left(B^{j}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right) \\
& \quad=F_{i, j}^{2}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right) E\left(\left(B^{i}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)^{2} \cdot\left(B^{j}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right)=F_{i, j}^{2}\left(t_{k}^{n}\right)\left(t_{k+1}^{n}-t_{k}^{n}\right)^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

and it follows that

$$
E \sum_{k} G_{k}^{2} \leq K^{2} \sum_{k}\left(t_{k+1}^{n}-t_{k}^{n}\right)^{2} \leq K^{2} t 2^{-n} \rightarrow 0
$$

To handle the cross terms, we condition on $\mathcal{F}_{t_{\ell}^{n}}$ to get
$E\left(G_{k} G_{\ell} \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{k}^{n}}\right)=G_{k} F_{i, j}\left(t_{\ell}^{n}\right) E\left(\left(B^{i}\left(t_{\ell+1}^{n}\right)-B^{i}\left(t_{\ell}^{n}\right)\right)\left(B^{j}\left(t_{\ell+1}^{n}\right)-B^{j}\left(t_{\ell}^{n}\right)\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{t_{\ell}^{n}}\right)=0$
At this point we have shown $E\left(\sum_{k} G_{k}\right)^{2} \rightarrow 0$ and the desired result follows from Markov's inequality. The assumption that the second derivatives are bounded can be removed as before.

## Exercises

7.6.1. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$. Show that $f\left(B_{t}\right)$ is a martingale if and only if $f(x)-a+b x$.
7.6.2. Show that $B_{t}^{3}-3 t B_{t}$ and $B_{t}^{3}-\int_{0}^{t} 3 B_{s} d s$ are a martingale.
7.6.3. Let $\beta_{2 k}(t)=E_{0} B_{t}^{2 k}$. Use Itô's formula to relate $\beta_{2 k}(t)$ to $\beta_{2 k-2}(t)$ and use this relationship to derive a formula for $\beta_{2 k}(t)$
7.6.4. Apply Itô's formula to $\left|B_{t}\right|$.
7.6.5. Apply Itô's formula to $\left|B_{t}\right|^{2}$. Use this to conclude that $E_{0}\left|B_{t}\right|^{2}=$ td.

## Chapter 8

## Applications to Random Walk

The key to the developments in the section is that we can embed sums of mean zero independent random variables (see Section 8.1) and martingales (see Section 8.2) into Brownian motion and derive central limit theorems. These results lead in Section 8.3 to CLTs for stationary sequences and in Section 8.4 to the convergence of rescaled empirical distributions to Brownian Bridge. Finally in Section 8.5 we use the embedding to prove a law of the iterated logarithm.

### 8.1 Donsker's Theorem

In this section we will prove Donsker's (1951) theorem. The key to its proof is:
Theorem 8.1.1. Skorokhod's representation theorem. If $E X=0$ and $E X^{2}<\infty$ then there is a stopping time $T$ for Brownian motion so that $B_{T}={ }_{d} X$ and $E T=E X^{2}$.

Remark. The Brownian motion in the statement and all the Brownian motions in this section have $B_{0}=0$.
Proof. Suppose first that $X$ is supported on $\{a, b\}$, where $a<0<b$. Since $E X=0$, we must have

$$
P(X=a)=\frac{b}{b-a} \quad P(X=b)=\frac{-a}{b-a}
$$

If we let $T=T_{a, b}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(a, b)\right\}$ then Theorem 7.5.3 implies $B_{T}={ }_{d} X$ and Theorem 7.5.5 tells us that

$$
E T=-a b=E B_{T}^{2}
$$

To treat the general case, we will write $F(x)=P(X \leq x)$ as a mixture of two point distributions with mean 0 . Let

$$
c=\int_{-\infty}^{0}(-u) d F(u)=\int_{0}^{\infty} v d F(v)
$$

If $\varphi$ is bounded and $\varphi(0)=0$, then using the two formulas for $c$

$$
\begin{aligned}
c \int \varphi(x) d F(x) & =\left(\int_{0}^{\infty} \varphi(v) d F(v)\right) \int_{-\infty}^{0}(-u) d F(u) \\
& +\left(\int_{-\infty}^{0} \varphi(u) d F(u)\right) \int_{0}^{\infty} v d F(v) \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} d F(v) \int_{-\infty}^{0} d F(u)(v \varphi(u)-u \varphi(v))
\end{aligned}
$$

So we have

$$
\int \varphi(x) d F(x)=c^{-1} \int_{0}^{\infty} d F(v) \int_{-\infty}^{0} d F(u)(v-u)\left\{\frac{v}{v-u} \varphi(u)+\frac{-u}{v-u} \varphi(v)\right\}
$$

The last equation gives the desired mixture. If we let $(U, V) \in \mathbf{R}^{2}$ have

$$
\begin{align*}
& P\{(U, V)=(0,0)\}=F(\{0\}) \\
& P((U, V) \in A)=c^{-1} \iint_{(u, v) \in A} d F(u) d F(v)(v-u) \tag{8.1.1}
\end{align*}
$$

for $A \subset(-\infty, 0) \times(0, \infty)$ and define probability measures by $\mu_{0,0}(\{0\})=$ 1 and

$$
\mu_{u, v}(\{u\})=\frac{v}{v-u} \quad \mu_{u, v}(\{v\})=\frac{-u}{v-u} \quad \text { for } u<0<v
$$

then

$$
\int \varphi(x) d F(x)=E\left(\int \varphi(x) \mu_{U, V}(d x)\right)
$$

We proved the last formula when $\varphi(0)=0$, but it is easy to see that it is true in general. Letting $\varphi \equiv 1$ in the last equation shows that the measure defined in (8.1.1) has total mass 1 .

From the calculations above it follows that if we have ( $U, V$ ) with distribution given in (8.1.1) and an independent Brownian motion defined on the same space then $B\left(T_{U, V}\right)={ }_{d} X$. Sticklers for detail will notice that $T_{U, V}$ is not a stopping time for $B_{t}$ since ( $U, V$ ) is independent of the Brownian motion. This is not a serious problem since if we condition on $U=u$ and $V=v$, then $T_{u, v}$ is a stopping time, and this is good enough for all the calculations below. For instance, to compute $E\left(T_{U, V}\right)$ we observe

$$
E\left(T_{U, V}\right)=E\left\{E\left(T_{U, V} \mid(U, V)\right)\right\}=E(-U V)
$$

by Theorem 7.5.5. (8.1.1) implies

$$
\begin{aligned}
E(-U V) & =\int_{-\infty}^{0} d F(u)(-u) \int_{0}^{\infty} d F(v) v(v-u) c^{-1} \\
& =\int_{-\infty}^{0} d F(u)(-u)\left\{-u+\int_{0}^{\infty} d F(v) c^{-1} v^{2}\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

since

$$
c=\int_{0}^{\infty} v d F(v)=\int_{-\infty}^{0}(-u) d F(u)
$$

Using the second expression for $c$ now gives

$$
E\left(T_{U, V}\right)=E(-U V)=\int_{-\infty}^{0} u^{2} d F(u)+\int_{0}^{\infty} v^{2} d F(v)=E X^{2}
$$

From Theorem 8.1.1, it is only a small step to:
Theorem 8.1.2. Let $X_{1}, X_{2} \ldots$ be i.i.d. with a distribution $F$, which has mean 0 and variance 1, and let $S_{n}=X_{1}+\ldots+X_{n}$. There is a sequence of stopping times $T_{0}=0, T_{1}, T_{2} \ldots$ such that $S_{n}={ }_{d} B\left(T_{n}\right)$ and $T_{n}-T_{n-1}$ are independent and identically distributed.

Proof. Let $\left(U_{1}, V_{1}\right),\left(U_{2}, V_{2}\right), \ldots$ be i.i.d. and have distribution given in (8.1.1) and let $B_{t}$ be an independent Brownian motion. Let $T_{0}=0$, and for $n \geq 1$, let

$$
T_{n}=\inf \left\{t \geq T_{n-1}: B_{t}-B\left(T_{n-1}\right) \notin\left(U_{n}, V_{n}\right)\right\}
$$

As a corollary of Theorem 8.1.2, we get:
Theorem 8.1.3. Central limit theorem. Under the hypotheses of Theorem 8.1.2, $S_{n} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \chi$, where $\chi$ has the standard normal distribution.

Proof. If we let $W_{n}(t)=B(n t) / \sqrt{n}={ }_{d} B_{t}$ by Brownian scaling, then

$$
S_{n} / \sqrt{n} \stackrel{d}{=} B\left(T_{n}\right) / \sqrt{n}=W_{n}\left(T_{n} / n\right)
$$

The weak law of large numbers implies that $T_{n} / n \rightarrow 1$ in probability. It should be clear from this that $S_{n} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow B_{1}$. To fill in the details, let $\epsilon>0$, pick $\delta$ so that

$$
P\left(\left|B_{t}-B_{1}\right|>\epsilon \text { for some } t \in(1-\delta, 1+\delta)\right)<\epsilon / 2
$$

then pick $N$ large enough so that for $n \geq N, P\left(\left|T_{n} / n-1\right|>\delta\right)<\epsilon / 2$. The last two estimates imply that for $n \geq N$

$$
P\left(\left|W_{n}\left(T_{n} / n\right)-W_{n}(1)\right|>\epsilon\right)<\epsilon
$$

Since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary, it follows that $W_{n}\left(T_{n} / n\right)-W_{n}(1) \rightarrow 0$ in probability. Applying the converging together lemma, Lemma 3.2.13, with $X_{n}= W_{n}(1)$ and $Z_{n}=W_{n}\left(T_{n} / n\right)$, the desired result follows.

Let $\mathbf{N}$ be the nonnegative integers and let

$$
S(u)=\left\{\begin{array}{cl}
S_{k} & \text { if } u=k \in \mathbf{N} \\
\text { linear on }[k, k+1] & \text { for } k \in \mathbf{N}
\end{array}\right.
$$

Later in this section, we will prove:

Theorem 8.1.4. Donsker's theorem. Under the hypotheses of Theorem 8.1.2,

$$
S(n \cdot) / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow B(\cdot),
$$

i.e., the associated measures on $C[0,1]$ converge weakly.
To motivate ourselves for the proof we will begin by extracting several corollaries. The key to each one is a consequence of the following result which follows from Theorem 3.10.1.

Theorem 8.1.5. If $\psi: C[0,1] \rightarrow \mathbf{R}$ has the property that it is continuous $P_{0}$-a.s. then

$$
\psi(S(n \cdot) / \sqrt{n}) \Rightarrow \psi(B(\cdot))
$$

Example 8.1.6. Maxima. Let $\psi(\omega)=\max \{\omega(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\}$. Again, $\psi: C[0,1] \rightarrow \mathbf{R}$ is continuous. This time Theorem 8.1.5 implies

$$
\max _{0 \leq m \leq n} S_{m} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow M_{1} \equiv \max _{0 \leq t \leq 1} B_{t}
$$

To complete the picture, we observe that by (7.4.4) the distribution of the right-hand side is

$$
P_{0}\left(M_{1} \geq a\right)=P_{0}\left(T_{a} \leq 1\right)=2 P_{0}\left(B_{1} \geq a\right)
$$

Example 8.1.7. Last 0 before time $n$. Let $\psi(\omega)=\sup \{t \leq 1: \omega(t)= 0\}$. This time, $\psi$ is not continuous, for if $\omega_{\epsilon}$ with $\omega_{\epsilon}(0)=0$ is piecewise linear with slope 1 on $[0,1 / 3+\epsilon],-1$ on $[1 / 3+\epsilon, 2 / 3]$, and slope 1 on $[2 / 3,1]$, then $\psi\left(\omega_{0}\right)=2 / 3$ but $\psi\left(\omega_{\epsilon}\right)=0$ for $\epsilon>0$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/322d99c7-eeb2-406f-855e-59cc960bee81-034.jpg?height=192&width=680&top_left_y=1641&top_left_x=758)

It is easy to see that if $\psi(\omega)<1$ and $\omega(t)$ has positive and negative values in each interval $(\psi(\omega)-\delta, \psi(\omega)+\delta)$, then $\psi$ is continuous at $\omega$. By arguments in Subection 8.4.1, the last set has $P_{0}$ measure 1. (If the zero at $\psi(\omega)$ was isolated on the left, it would not be isolated on the right.) It follows that

$$
\sup \left\{m \leq n: S_{m-1} \cdot S_{m} \leq 0\right\} / n \Rightarrow L=\sup \left\{t \leq 1: B_{t}=0\right\}
$$

The distribution of $L$ is given in (7.4.7). The last result shows that the arcsine law, Theorem 4.9.5, proved for simple random walks holds when the mean is 0 and variance is finite.

Example 8.1.8. Occupation times of half-lines. Let

$$
\psi(\omega)=|\{t \in[0,1]: \omega(t)>a\}| .
$$

The point $\omega \equiv a$ shows that $\psi$ is not continuous, but it is easy to see that $\psi$ is continuous at paths $\omega$ with $|\{t \in[0,1]: \omega(t)=a\}|=0$. Fubini's theorem implies that

$$
E_{0}\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}=a\right\}\right|=\int_{0}^{1} P_{0}\left(B_{t}=a\right) d t=0
$$

so $\psi$ is continuous $P_{0}$-a.s. Using Theorem 8.1.5, we now get that

$$
|\{u \leq n: S(u)>a \sqrt{n}\}| / n \Rightarrow\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}>a\right\}\right|
$$

As we will now show, with a little work, one can convert this into the more natural result

$$
\left|\left\{m \leq n: S_{m}>a \sqrt{n}\right\}\right| / n \Rightarrow\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}>a\right\}\right|
$$

Proof. Application of Theorem 8.1.5 gives that for any $a$,

$$
