|\{t \in[0,1]: S(n t)>a \sqrt{n}\}| \Rightarrow\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}>a\right\}\right|
$$

To convert this into a result about $\left|\left\{m \leq n: S_{m}>a \sqrt{n}\right\}\right|$, we note that on $\left\{\max _{m \leq n}\left|X_{m}\right| \leq \epsilon \sqrt{n}\right\}$, which by Chebyshev's inequality has a probability $\rightarrow 1$, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
|\{t \in[0,1]: S(n t)>(a+\epsilon) \sqrt{n}\}| & \leq \frac{1}{n}\left|\left\{m \leq n: S_{m}>a \sqrt{n}\right\}\right| \\
& \leq|\{t \in[0,1]: S(n t)>(a-\epsilon) \sqrt{n}\}|
\end{aligned}
$$

Combining this with the first conclusion of the proof and using the fact that $b \rightarrow\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}>b\right\}\right|$ is continuous at $b=a$ with probability one, one arrives easily at the desired conclusion.

To compute the distribution of $\left|\left\{t \in[0,1]: B_{t}>0\right\}\right|$, observe that we proved in Theorem 4.9.6 that if $S_{n}$ is symmetric simple random walk, then the left-hand side converges to the arcsine law, so the right-hand side has that distribution and is the limit for any random walk with mean 0 and finite variance. The last argument uses an idea called the "invariance principle" that originated with Erdös and Kac (1946, 1947): The asymptotic behavior of functionals of $S_{n}$ should be the same as long as the central limit theorem applies.

Proof of Theorem 8.1.4. To simplify the proof and prepare for generalizations in the next section, let $X_{n, m}, 1 \leq m \leq n$, be a triangular array of
random variables, $S_{n, m}=X_{n, 1}+\cdots+X_{n, m}$ and suppose $S_{n, m}=B\left(\tau_{m}^{n}\right)$. Let

$$
S_{n,(u)}=\left\{\begin{array}{cl}
S_{n, m} & \text { if } u=m \in\{0,1, \ldots, n\} \\
\text { linear for } u \in[m-1, m] & \text { when } m \in\{1, \ldots, n\}
\end{array}\right.
$$

Lemma 8.1.9. If $\tau_{[n s]}^{n} \rightarrow s$ in probability for each $s \in[0,1]$ then

$$
\left\|S_{n,(n \cdot)}-B(\cdot)\right\| \rightarrow 0 \quad \text { in probability }
$$

To make the connection with the original problem, let $X_{n, m}=X_{m} / \sqrt{n}$ and define $\tau_{1}^{n}, \ldots, \tau_{n}^{n}$ so that $\left(S_{n, 1}, \ldots, S_{n, n}\right)={ }_{d}\left(B\left(\tau_{1}^{n}\right), \ldots, B\left(\tau_{n}^{n}\right)\right)$. If $T_{1}, T_{2}, \ldots$ are the stopping times defined in the proof of Theorem 8.1.3, Brownian scaling implies $\tau_{m}^{n}={ }_{d} T_{m} / n$, so the hypothesis of Lemma 8.1.9 is satisfied.

Proof. The fact that $B$ has continuous paths (and hence uniformly continuous on $[0,1]$ ) implies that if $\epsilon>0$ then there is a $\delta>0$ so that $1 / \delta$ is an integer and

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\left|B_{t}-B_{s}\right|<\epsilon \text { for all } 0 \leq s \leq 1,|t-s|<2 \delta\right)>1-\epsilon \tag{a}
\end{equation*}
$$

The hypothesis of Lemma 8.1.9 implies that if $n \geq N_{\delta}$ then

$$
P\left(\left|\tau_{[n k \delta]}^{n}-k \delta\right|<\delta \quad \text { for } k=1,2, \ldots, 1 / \delta\right) \geq 1-\epsilon
$$

Since $m \rightarrow \tau_{m}^{n}$ is increasing, it follows that if $s \in((k-1) \delta, k \delta)$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \tau_{[n s]}^{n}-s \geq \tau_{[n(k-1) \delta]}^{n}-k \delta \\
& \tau_{[n s]}^{n}-s \leq \tau_{[n k \delta]}^{n}-(k+1) \delta
\end{aligned}
$$

so if $n \geq N_{\delta}$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\sup _{0 \leq s \leq 1}\left|\tau_{[n s]}^{n}-s\right|<2 \delta\right) \geq 1-\epsilon \tag{b}
\end{equation*}
$$

When the events in (a) and (b) occur

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|S_{n, m}-B_{m / n}\right|<\epsilon \text { for all } m \leq n \tag{c}
\end{equation*}
$$

To deal with $t=(m+\theta) / n$ with $0<\theta<1$, we observe that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|S_{n,(n t)}-B_{t}\right| & \leq(1-\theta)\left|S_{n, m}-B_{m / n}\right|+\theta\left|S_{n, m+1}-B_{(m+1) / n}\right| \\
& +(1-\theta)\left|B_{m / n}-B_{t}\right|+\theta\left|B_{(m+1) / n}-B_{t}\right|
\end{aligned}
$$

Using (c) on the first two terms and (a) on the last two, we see that if $n \geq N_{\delta}$ and $1 / n<2 \delta$, then $\left\|S_{n,(n \cdot)}-B(\cdot)\right\|<2 \epsilon$ with probability $\geq 1-2 \epsilon$. Since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary, the proof of Lemma 8.1.9 is complete.

To get Theorem 8.1.4 now, we have to show:
Lemma 8.1.10. If $\varphi$ is bounded and continuous then $E \varphi\left(S_{n,(n \cdot)}\right) \rightarrow E \varphi(B(\cdot))$.

Proof. For fixed $\epsilon>0$, let $G_{\delta}=\left\{\omega\right.$ : if $\left\|\omega-\omega^{\prime}\right\|<\delta$ then $\mid \varphi(\omega)- \left.\varphi\left(\omega^{\prime}\right) \mid<\epsilon\right\}$. Since $\varphi$ is continuous, $G_{\delta} \uparrow C[0,1]$ as $\delta \downarrow 0$. Let $\Delta= \left\|S_{n,(n \cdot)}-B(\cdot)\right\|$. The desired result now follows from Lemma 8.1.9 and the trivial inequality

$$
\left|E \varphi\left(S_{n,(n \cdot)}\right)-E \varphi(B(\cdot))\right| \leq \epsilon+(2 \sup |\varphi(\omega)|)\left\{P\left(G_{\delta}^{c}\right)+P(\Delta \geq \delta)\right\}
$$

To accommodate our final example, we need a trivial generalization of Theorem 8.1.4. Let $C[0, \infty)=\{$ continuous $\omega:[0, \infty) \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\}$ and let $\mathcal{C}[0, \infty)$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by the finite dimensional sets. Given a probability measure $\mu$ on $C[0, \infty)$, there is a corresponding measure $\pi_{M} \mu$ on $C[0, M]=\{$ continuous $\omega:[0, M] \rightarrow \mathbf{R}\}$ (with $\mathcal{C}[0, M]$ the $\sigma$-field generated by the finite dimensional sets) obtained by "cutting off the paths at time $M$." Let $\left(\psi_{M} \omega\right)(t)=\omega(t)$ for $t \in[0, M]$ and let $\pi_{M} \mu=\mu \circ \psi_{M}^{-1}$. We say that a sequence of probability measures $\mu_{n}$ on $C[0, \infty)$ converges weakly to $\mu$ if for all $M, \pi_{M} \mu_{n}$ converges weakly to $\pi_{M} \mu$ on $C[0, M]$, the last concept being defined by a trivial extension of the definitions for $M=1$. With these definitions, it is easy to conclude:

Theorem 8.1.11. $S(n \cdot) / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow B(\cdot)$, i.e., the associated measures on $C[0, \infty)$ converge weakly.

Proof. By definition, all we have to show is that weak convergence occurs on $C[0, M]$ for all $M<\infty$. The proof of Theorem 8.1.4 works in the same way when 1 is replaced by $M$.

Example 8.1.12. Let $N_{n}=\inf \left\{m: S_{m} \geq \sqrt{n}\right\}$ and $T_{1}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \geq\right. 1\}$. Since $\psi(\omega)=T_{1}(\omega) \wedge 1$ is continuous $P_{0}$ a.s. on $C[0,1]$ and the distribution of $T_{1}$ is continuous, it follows from Theorem 8.1.5 that for $0<t<1$

$$
P\left(N_{n} \leq n t\right) \rightarrow P\left(T_{1} \leq t\right)
$$

Repeating the last argument with 1 replaced by $M$ and using Theorem 8.1.11 shows that the last conclusion holds for all $t$.

## Exercises

8.1.1. Use Exercise 7.5.4 to conclude that $E\left(T_{U, V}^{2}\right) \leq 4 E X^{4}$.
8.1.2. Suppose $S_{n}$ is one-dimensional simple random walk and let

$$
R_{n}=1+\max _{m \leq n} S_{m}-\min _{m \leq n} S_{m}
$$

be the number of points visited by time $n$. Show that $R_{n} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow a$ limit.

### 8.2 CLT's for Martingales

In this section, we will prove central limit theorems for martingales. The key is an embedding theorem due to Strassen (1967). See his Theorem 4.3.

Theorem 8.2.1. If $S_{n}$ is a square integrable martingale with $S_{0}=0$, and $B_{t}$ is a Brownian motion, then there is a sequence of stopping times $0=T_{0} \leq T_{1} \leq T_{2} \ldots$ for the Brownian motion so that

$$
\left(S_{0}, S_{1}, \ldots, S_{k}\right) \stackrel{d}{=}\left(B\left(T_{0}\right), B\left(T_{1}\right), \ldots, B\left(T_{k}\right)\right) \quad \text { for all } k \geq 0
$$

Proof. We include $S_{0}=0=B\left(T_{0}\right)$ only for the sake of starting the induction argument. Suppose we have $\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1}\right)={ }_{d}\left(B\left(T_{0}\right), \ldots, B\left(T_{k-1}\right)\right)$ for some $k \geq 1$. The strong Markov property implies that $\left\{B\left(T_{k-1}+t\right)-\right. \left.B\left(T_{k-1}\right): t \geq 0\right\}$ is a Brownian motion that is independent of $\mathcal{F}\left(T_{k-1}\right)$. Let $\mu_{k}\left(s_{0}, \ldots, s_{k-1} ; \cdot\right)$ be a regular conditional distribution of $S_{k}-S_{k-1}$, given $S_{j}=s_{j}, 0 \leq j \leq k-1$, that is, for each Borel set $A$

$$
P\left(S_{k}-S_{k-1} \in A \mid S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1}\right)=\mu_{k}\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1} ; A\right)
$$

By Exercises 4.1.18 and 4.1.16, this exists, and we have

$$
0=E\left(S_{k}-S_{k-1} \mid S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1}\right)=\int x \mu_{k}\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1} ; d x\right)
$$

so the mean of the conditional distribution is 0 almost surely. Using Theorem 8.1.1 now we see that for almost every $\hat{S} \equiv\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1}\right)$, there is a stopping time $\tau_{\hat{S}}$ (for $\left\{B\left(T_{k-1}+t\right)-B\left(T_{k-1}\right): t \geq 0\right\}$ ) so that

$$
B\left(T_{k-1}+\tau_{\hat{S}}\right)-B\left(T_{k-1}\right) \stackrel{d}{=} \mu_{k}\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1} ; \cdot\right)
$$

If we let $T_{k}=T_{k-1}+\tau_{\hat{S}}$ then $\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k}\right) \stackrel{d}{=}\left(B\left(T_{0}\right), \ldots, B\left(T_{k}\right)\right)$ and the result follows by induction.

Remark. While the details of the proof are fresh in the reader's mind, we would like to observe that if $E\left(S_{k}-S_{k-1}\right)^{2}<\infty$, then

$$
E\left(\tau_{\hat{S}} \mid S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1}\right)=\int x^{2} \mu_{k}\left(S_{0}, \ldots, S_{k-1} ; d x\right)
$$

since $B_{t}^{2}-t$ is a martingale and $\tau_{\hat{S}}$ is the exit time from a randomly chosen interval ( $S_{k-1}+U_{k}, S_{k-1}+V_{k}$ ).

Before getting involved in the details of the central limit theorem, we show that the existence of the embedding allows us to give an easy proof of Theorem 4.5.2.

Theorem 8.2.2. Let $\mathcal{F}_{m}=\sigma\left(S_{0}, S_{1}, \ldots S_{m}\right) . \lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} S_{n}$ exists and is finite on $\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} E\left(\left(S_{m}-S_{m-1}\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{m-1}\right)<\infty$.

Proof. Let $\mathcal{B}_{t}$ be the filtration generated by Brownian motion, and let $t_{m}=T_{m}-T_{m-1}$. By construction we have

$$
E\left(\left(S_{m}-S_{m-1}\right)^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{m-1}\right)=E\left(t_{m} \mid \mathcal{B}\left(T_{m-1}\right)\right)
$$

Let $M=\inf \left\{n: \sum_{m=1}^{n+1} E\left(t_{m} \mid \mathcal{B}\left(T_{m-1}\right)\right)>A\right\} . M$ is a stopping time and by its definition has $\sum_{m=1}^{M} E\left(t_{m} \mid \mathcal{B}\left(T_{m-1}\right)\right) \leq A$. Now $\{M \geq m\} \in \mathcal{F}_{m-1}$ so

$$
\begin{aligned}
E \sum_{m=1}^{M} t_{m} & =\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} E\left(t_{m} ; M \geq m\right) \\
& =\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} E\left(E\left(t_{M} \mid \mathcal{B}\left(T_{m-1}\right) ; M \geq m\right)=E \sum_{m=1}^{M} E\left(t_{m} \mid \mathcal{B}\left(T_{m-1}\right)\right) \leq A\right.
\end{aligned}
$$

From this we see that $\sum_{m=1}^{M} t_{m}<\infty$. As $A \rightarrow \infty, P(M<\infty) \downarrow 0$ so $T_{\infty}=\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} t_{m}<\infty$. Since $B\left(T_{n}\right) \rightarrow B\left(T_{\infty}\right)$ as $n \rightarrow \infty$ the desired result follows.

Our first step toward the promised central limit theorem is to prove a result of Freedman (1971a). We say that $X_{n, m}, \mathcal{F}_{n, m}, 1 \leq m \leq n$, is a martingale difference array if $X_{n, m} \in \mathcal{F}_{n, m}$ and $E\left(X_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=0$ for $1 \leq m \leq n$, where $\mathcal{F}_{n, 0}=\{\emptyset, \Omega\}$. Let $S_{n, m}=X_{n, 1}+\ldots+X_{n, m}$. Let $N=\{0,1,2, \ldots\}$. Throughout the section, $S_{n,(n \cdot)}$ denotes the linear interpolation of $S_{n, m}$ defined by

$$
S_{n,(u)}= \begin{cases}S_{k} & \text { if } u=k \in N \\ \text { linear for } u \in[k, k+1] & \text { when } k \in N\end{cases}
$$

and $B(\cdot)$ is a Brownian motion with $B_{0}=0$. Let

$$
V_{n, k}=\sum_{1 \leq m \leq k} E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
$$

Theorem 8.2.3. Suppose $\left\{X_{n, m}, \mathcal{F}_{n, m}\right\}$ is a martingale difference array.
If (i) for each $t, V_{n,[n t]} \rightarrow t$ in probability,
(i) $\left|X_{n, m}\right| \leq \epsilon_{n}$ for all $m$ with $\epsilon_{n} \rightarrow 0$, and
then $S_{n,(n \cdot)} \Rightarrow B(\cdot)$.
Proof. (i) implies $V_{n, n} \rightarrow 1$ in probability. By stopping each sequence at the first time $V_{n, k}>2$ and setting the later $X_{n, m}=0$, we can suppose without loss of generality that $V_{n, n} \leq 2+\epsilon_{n}^{2}$ for all $n$. By Theorem 8.2.1, we can find stopping times $T_{n, 1}, \ldots, T_{n, n}$ so that $\left(S_{n, 1}, \ldots, S_{n, n}\right)={ }_{d} \left(B\left(T_{n, 1}\right), \ldots, B\left(T_{n, n}\right)\right)$. By Lemma 8.1.9, it suffices to show that $T_{n,[n t]} \rightarrow$
$t$ in probability for each $t \in[0,1]$. To do this we let $t_{n, m}=T_{n, m}- T_{n, m-1}$ (with $T_{n, 0}=0$ ) observe that by the remark after the proof of Theorem 8.2.1, $E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)$. The last observation and hypothesis (i) imply

$$
\sum_{m=1}^{[n t]} E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow t \quad \text { in probability }
$$

To get from this to $T_{n,[n t]} \rightarrow t$ in probability, we observe

$$
E\left(\sum_{m=1}^{[n t]} t_{n, m}-E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right)^{2}=E \sum_{m=1}^{[n t]}\left\{t_{n, m}-E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right\}^{2}
$$

by the orthogonality of martingale increments, Theorem 4.4.7. Now

$$
\begin{aligned}
E\left(\left\{t_{n, m}\right.\right. & \left.\left.-E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right\}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \leq E\left(t_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \\
& \leq C E\left(X_{n, m}^{4} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \leq C \epsilon_{n}^{2} E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

by Exercise 7.5.4 and assumption (ii). Summing over $n$, taking expected values, and recalling we have assumed $V_{n, n} \leq 2+\epsilon_{n}^{2}$, it follows that

$$
E\left(\sum_{m=1}^{[n t]} t_{n, m}-E\left(t_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right)^{2} \rightarrow C \epsilon_{n}^{2} E V_{n, n} \rightarrow 0
$$

Unscrambling the definitions, we have shown $E\left(T_{n,[n t]}-V_{n,[n t]}\right)^{2} \rightarrow 0$, so Chebyshev's inequality implies $P\left(\left|T_{n,[n t]}-V_{n,[n t]}\right|>\epsilon\right) \rightarrow 0$, and using (ii) now completes the proof.

With Theorem 8.2.3 established, a truncation argument gives us:
Theorem 8.2.4. Lindeberg-Feller theorem for martingales. Suppose $X_{n, m}, \mathcal{F}_{n, m}, 1 \leq m \leq n$ is a martingale difference array.
If (i) $V_{n,[n t]} \rightarrow t$ in probability for all $t \in[0,1]$ and
(ii) for all $\epsilon>0, \sum_{m \leq n} E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon\right)} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow 0$ in probability, then $S_{n,(n \cdot)} \Rightarrow B(\cdot)$.

Remark 8.2.1. If $X_{n, m}$ on each row are independent this reduces to the martingale central limit theorem.

Proof. The first step is to truncate so that we can apply Theorem 8.2.3. Let

$$
\hat{V}_{n}(\epsilon)=\sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n}\right)} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
$$

Lemma 8.2.5. If $\epsilon_{n} \rightarrow 0$ slowly enough then $\epsilon_{n}^{-2} \hat{V}_{n}\left(\epsilon_{n}\right) \rightarrow 0$ in probability.

Remark. The $\epsilon_{n}^{-2}$ in front is so that we can conclude

$$
\sum_{m \leq n} P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow 0 \text { in probability }
$$

Proof. Let $N_{m}$ be chosen so that $P\left(m^{2} \hat{V}_{n}(1 / m)>1 / m\right) \leq 1 / m$ for $n \geq N_{m}$. Let $\epsilon_{n}=1 / m$ for $n \in\left[N_{m}, N_{m+1}\right)$ and $\epsilon_{n}=1$ if $n<N_{1}$. If $\delta>0$ and $1 / m<\delta$ then for $n \in\left[N_{m}, N_{m+1}\right)$

$$
P\left(\epsilon_{n}^{-2} \hat{V}_{n}\left(\epsilon_{n}\right)>\delta\right) \leq P\left(m^{2} \hat{V}_{n}(1 / m)>1 / m\right) \leq 1 / m
$$

Let $\hat{X}_{n, m}=X_{n, m} 1_{\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n}\right)}, \bar{X}_{n, m}=X_{n, m} 1_{\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right| \leq \epsilon_{n}\right)}$ and

$$
\tilde{X}_{n, m}=\bar{X}_{n, m}-E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
$$

Our next step is to show:
Lemma 8.2.6. If we define $\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)}$ in the obvious way then Theorem 8.2.3 implies $\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)} \Rightarrow B(\cdot)$.

Proof. Since $\left|\tilde{X}_{n, m}\right| \leq 2 \epsilon_{n}$, we only have to check (ii) in Theorem 8.2.3. To do this, we observe that the conditional variance formula, Theorem 4.4.8, implies

$$
E\left(\tilde{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)-E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)^{2}
$$

For the first term, we observe

$$
E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)-E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
$$

For the second, we observe that $E\left(X_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=0$ implies

$$
E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)^{2}=E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)^{2} \leq E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)
$$

by Jensen's inequality, so it follows from (a) and (i) that

$$
\sum_{m=1}^{[n t]} E\left(\tilde{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow t \quad \text { for all } t \in[0,1]
$$

Having proved Lemma 8.2.6, it remains to estimate the difference between $S_{n,(n \cdot)}$ and $\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)}$. On $\left\{\left|X_{n, m}\right| \leq \epsilon_{n}\right.$ for all $\left.1 \leq m \leq n\right\}$, we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left\|S_{n,(n \cdot)}-\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)}\right\| \leq \sum_{m=1}^{n}\left|E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right| \tag{8.2.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

To handle the right-hand side, we observe

$$
\begin{align*}
\sum_{m=1}^{n}\left|E\left(\bar{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right| & =\sum_{m=1}^{n}\left|E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)\right| \\
& \leq \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(\left|\hat{X}_{n, m}\right| \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)  \tag{8.2.2}\\
& \leq \epsilon_{n}^{-1} \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow 0
\end{align*}
$$

in probability by Lemma 8.2.5. To complete the proof now, it suffices to show

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n} \text { for some } m \leq n\right) \rightarrow 0 \tag{8.2.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

for with (8.2.2) and (8.2.1) this implies $\left\|S_{n,(n \cdot)}-\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)}\right\| \rightarrow 0$ in probability. The proof of Theorem 8.2.3 constructs a Brownian motion with $\left\|\tilde{S}_{n,(n \cdot)}-B(\cdot)\right\| \rightarrow 0$, so the desired result follows from the triangle inequality and Lemma 8.1.10.

To prove (8.2.3), we will use Lemma 3.5 of Dvoretsky (1972).
Lemma 8.2.7. If $A_{n}$ is adapted to $\mathcal{G}_{n}$ then for any nonnegative $\delta \in \mathcal{G}_{0}$,

$$
P\left(\cup_{m=1}^{n} A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq \delta+P\left(\sum_{m=1}^{n} P\left(A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)>\delta \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

Proof. We proceed by induction. When $n=1$, the conclusion says

$$
P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq \delta+P\left(P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)>\delta \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

This is obviously true on $\Omega_{-} \equiv\left\{P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq \delta\right\}$ and also on $\Omega_{+} \equiv \left\{P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)>\delta\right\} \in \mathcal{G}_{0}$ since on $\Omega_{+}$

$$
P\left(P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)>\delta \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)=1 \geq P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

To prove the result for $n$ sets, observe that by the last argument the inequality is trivial on $\Omega_{+}$. Let $B_{m}=A_{m} \cap \Omega_{-}$. Since $\Omega_{-} \in \mathcal{G}_{0} \subset \mathcal{G}_{m-1}$, $P\left(B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)=P\left(A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)$ on $\Omega_{-}$. (See Exercise 4.1.2.) Applying the result for $n-1$ sets with $\gamma=\delta-P\left(B_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \geq 0$,

$$
P\left(\cup_{m=2}^{n} B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{1}\right) \leq \gamma+P\left(\sum_{m=2}^{n} P\left(B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)>\gamma \mid \mathcal{G}_{1}\right)
$$

Taking conditional expectation w.r.t. $\mathcal{G}_{0}$ and noting $\gamma \in \mathcal{G}_{0}$,

$$
P\left(\cup_{m=2}^{n} B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq \gamma+P\left(\sum_{m=1}^{n} P\left(B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)>\delta \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

$\cup_{2 \leq m \leq n} B_{m}=\left(\cup_{2 \leq m \leq n} A_{m}\right) \cap \Omega_{-}$and another use of Exercise 4.1.2 shows

$$
\sum_{1 \leq m \leq n} P\left(B_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)=\sum_{1 \leq m \leq n} P\left(A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)
$$

on $\Omega_{-}$. So, on $\Omega_{-}$,

$$
P\left(\cup_{m=2}^{n} A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq \delta-P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)+P\left(\sum_{m=1}^{n} P\left(A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{m-1}\right)>\delta \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

The result now follows from

$$
P\left(\cup_{m=1}^{n} A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right) \leq P\left(A_{1} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)+P\left(\cup_{m=2}^{n} A_{m} \mid \mathcal{G}_{0}\right)
$$

To see this, let $C=\cup_{2 \leq m \leq n} A_{m}$, observe that $1_{A_{1} \cup C} \leq 1_{A_{1}}+1_{C}$, and use the monotonicity of conditional expectations.

Proof of (8.2.3). Let $A_{m}=\left\{\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n}\right\}, \mathcal{G}_{m}=\mathcal{F}_{n, m}$, and let $\delta$ be a positive number. Lemma 8.2.7 implies
$P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n}\right.$ for some $\left.m \leq n\right) \leq \delta+P\left(\sum_{m=1}^{n} P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)>\delta\right)$
To estimate the right-hand side, we observe that "Chebyshev's inequality" (Exercise 1.3 in Chapter 4) implies

$$
\sum_{m=1}^{n} P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \leq \epsilon_{n}^{-2} \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(\hat{X}_{n, m}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right) \rightarrow 0
$$

so $\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} P\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon_{n}\right.$ for some $\left.m \leq n\right) \leq \delta$. Since $\delta$ is arbitrary, the proof of (e) and hence of Theorem 8.2.4 is complete.

For applications, it is useful to have a result for a single sequence.
Theorem 8.2.8. Martingale central limit theorem. Suppose $X_{n}$, $\mathcal{F}_{n}, n \geq 1$, is a martingale difference sequence and let $V_{k}=\sum_{1 \leq n \leq k} E\left(X_{n}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n-1}\right)$. If (i) $V_{k} / k \rightarrow \sigma^{2}>0$ in probability and
(ii) $n^{-1} \sum_{m \leq n} E\left(X_{m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{m}\right|>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right)}\right) \rightarrow 0$
then $S_{(n \cdot)} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot)$.
Proof. Let $X_{n, m}=X_{m} / \sigma \sqrt{n}, \mathcal{F}_{n, m}=\mathcal{F}_{m}$. Changing notation and letting $k=n t$, our first assumption becomes (i) of Theorem 8.2.4. To check (ii), observe that

$$
E \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(X_{n, m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{n, m}\right|>\epsilon\right)} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n, m-1}\right)=\sigma^{-2} n^{-1} \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(X_{m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{m}\right|>\epsilon \sigma \sqrt{n}\right)}\right) \rightarrow 0
$$

### 8.3 CLTs for Stationary Sequences

We begin by considering the martingale case.
Theorem 8.3.1. Suppose $X_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$, is an ergodic stationary sequence of square integrable martingale differences, i.e., $\sigma^{2}=E X_{n}^{2}<\infty$ and $E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n-1}\right)=0$, where $\mathcal{F}_{n}=\sigma\left(X_{m}, m \leq n\right)$. Let $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$. Then

$$
S_{(n \cdot)} / n^{1 / 2} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot)
$$

Remark 8.3.1. This result was discovered independently by Billingsley (1961) and Ibragimov (1963).

Proof. $u_{n} \equiv E\left(X_{n}^{2} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n-1}\right)$ can be written as $\varphi\left(X_{n-1}, X_{n-2}, \ldots\right)$, so (1.3) in Chapter 6 implies $u_{n}$ is stationary and ergodic, and the ergodic theorem implies

$$
n^{-1} \sum_{m=1}^{n} u_{m} \rightarrow E u_{0}=E X_{0}^{2} \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

The last conclusion shows that (i) of Theorem 8.2.8 holds. To verify (ii), we observe

$$
n^{-1} \sum_{m=1}^{n} E\left(X_{m}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{m}\right|>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right)}\right)=E\left(X_{0}^{2} 1_{\left(\left|X_{0}\right|>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right)}\right) \rightarrow 0
$$

by the dominated convergence theorem.
We prove our first central limit theorem for stationary sequences by reducing to the martingale case Our proof is based on Scott (1973), but the key is an idea of Gordin (1969). In some applications of this result (see e.g., Exercise 8.3.1), it is convenient to use $\sigma$-fields richer than $\mathcal{F}_{m}= \sigma\left(X_{n}, n \leq m\right)$. So we adopt abstract conditions that satisfy the demands of martingale theory and ergodic theory:
(i) $n \rightarrow \mathcal{F}_{n}$ is increasing, and $X_{n} \in \mathcal{F}_{n}$
(ii) $\theta^{-1} \mathcal{F}_{m}=\mathcal{F}_{m+1}$

Theorem 8.3.2. Suppose $X_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$ is an ergodic stationary sequence with $E X_{n}=0$. Let $\mathcal{F}_{n}$ satisfy (i), (ii), and

$$
\sum_{n \geq 1}\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2}<\infty
$$

where $\|Y\|_{2}=\left(E Y^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}$. Let $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$. Then $S_{(n \cdot)} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot)$ where

$$
\sigma^{2}=E X_{0}^{2}+2 \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} E X_{0} X_{n}
$$

and the series in the definition converges absolutely.

Proof. Suppose $X_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$ is defined on sequence space ( $\mathbf{R}^{\mathbf{Z}}, \mathcal{R}^{\mathbf{Z}}, P$ ) with $X_{n}(\omega)=\omega_{n}$ and let $\left(\theta^{n} \omega\right)(m)=\omega(m+n)$. Let

$$
\begin{aligned}
& H_{n}=\left\{Y \in \mathcal{F}_{n} \text { with } E Y^{2}<\infty\right\} \\
& K_{n}=\left\{Y \in H_{n} \text { with } E(Y Z)=0 \text { for all } Z \in H_{n-1}\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

Geometrically, $H_{0} \supset H_{-1} \supset H_{-2} \ldots$ is a sequence of subspaces of $L^{2}$ and $K_{n}$ is the orthogonal complement of $H_{n-1}$ in $H_{n}$. If $Y$ is a random variable, let $\left(\theta^{n} Y\right)(\omega)=Y\left(\theta^{n} \omega\right)$. Generalizing from the example $Y= f\left(X_{-j}, \ldots, X_{k}\right)$, which has $\theta^{n} Y=f\left(X_{n-j}, \ldots, X_{n+k}\right)$, it is easy to see that if $Y \in H_{k}$, then $\theta^{n} Y \in H_{k+n}$, and hence if $Y \in K_{j}$ then $\theta^{n} Y \in K_{n+j}$.

If $X_{0}$ happened to be in $K_{0}$ then we would be happy since then $X_{n}=\theta^{n} X_{0} \in K_{n}$ for all $n$, and taking $Z=1_{A} \in H_{n-1}$ we would have $E\left(X_{n} 1_{A}\right)=0$ for all $A \in \mathcal{F}_{n-1}$ and hence $E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{n-1}\right)=0$. The next best thing to having $X_{0} \in K_{0}$ is to have

$$
\begin{equation*}
X_{0}=Y_{0}+Z_{0}-\theta Z_{0} \tag{8.3.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

with $Y_{0} \in K_{0}$ and $Z_{0} \in L^{2}$, for then if we let

$$
S_{n}=\sum_{m=1}^{n} X_{m}=\sum_{m=1}^{n} \theta^{m} X_{0} \quad \text { and } \quad T_{n}=\sum_{m=1}^{n} \theta^{m} Y_{0}
$$

then $S_{n}=T_{n}+\theta Z_{0}-\theta^{n+1} Z_{0}$. The $\theta^{m} Y_{0}$ are a stationary ergodic martingale difference sequence (ergodicity follows from (1.3) in Chapter 6), so Theorem 8.3.1 implies

$$
T_{(n \cdot)} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot) \quad \text { where } \sigma^{2}=E Y_{0}^{2}
$$

To get rid of the other term, we observe $\theta Z_{0} / \sqrt{n} \rightarrow 0$ a.s. and

$$
P\left(\sup _{1 \leq m \leq n} \theta^{m+1} Z_{0}>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right) \leq n P\left(Z_{0}>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right) \leq \epsilon^{-2} E\left(Z_{0}^{2} ; Z_{0}>\epsilon \sqrt{n}\right) \rightarrow 0
$$

by dominated convergence. To solve (8.3.1) formally, we let

$$
\begin{aligned}
Z_{0} & =\sum_{j=0}^{\infty} E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-1}\right) \\
\theta Z_{0} & =\sum_{j=0}^{\infty} E\left(X_{j+1} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right) \\
Y_{0} & =\sum_{j=0}^{\infty}\left\{E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)-E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-1}\right)\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

and check that

$$
Y_{0}+Z_{0}-\theta Z_{0}=E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)=X_{0}
$$

To justify the last calculation, we need to know that the series in the definitions of $Z_{0}$ and $Y_{0}$ converge. Our assumption and shift invariance imply

$$
\sum_{j=0}^{\infty}\left\|E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-1}\right)\right\|_{2}<\infty
$$

so the triangle inequality implies that the series for $Z_{0}$ converges in $L^{2}$. Since

$$
\left\|E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)-E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-1}\right)\right\|_{2} \leq\left\|E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)\right\|_{2}
$$

(the left-hand side is the projection onto $K_{0} \subset H_{0}$ ), the series for $Y_{0}$ also converges in $L^{2}$. Putting the pieces together, we have shown Theorem 8.3.2 with $\sigma^{2}=E Y_{0}^{2}$. To get the indicated formula for $\sigma^{2}$, observe that conditioning and using Cauchy-Schwarz

$$
\left|E X_{0} X_{n}\right|=\left|E\left(X_{0} E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)\right)\right| \leq\left\|X_{0}\right\|_{2}\left\|E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)\right\|_{2}
$$

Shift invariance implies $\left\|E\left(X_{n} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)\right\|_{2}=\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2}$, so the series converges absolutely.

$$
E S_{n}^{2}=\sum_{j=1}^{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} E X_{j} X_{k}=n E X_{0}^{2}+2 \sum_{m=1}^{n-1}(n-m) E X_{0} X_{m}
$$

From this, it follows easily that

$$
n^{-1} E S_{n}^{2} \rightarrow E X_{0}^{2}+2 \sum_{m=1}^{\infty} E X_{0} X_{m}
$$

To finish the proof, let $T_{n}=\sum_{m=1}^{n} \theta^{m} Y_{0}$, observe $\sigma^{2}=E Y_{0}^{2}$ and

$$
n^{-1} E\left(S_{n}-T_{n}\right)^{2}=n^{-1} E\left(\theta Z_{0}-\theta^{n+1} Z_{0}\right)^{2} \leq 4 E Z_{0}^{2} / n \rightarrow 0
$$

since $(a-b)^{2} \leq(2 a)^{2}+(2 b)^{2}$.
We turn now to examples. In the first one, it is trivial to check the hypothesis of Theorem 8.3.2.

Example 8.3.3. M-dependent sequences. Let $X_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$, be a stationary sequence with $E X_{n}=0, E X_{n}^{2}<\infty$, and suppose $\left\{X_{j}, j \leq 0\right\}$ and $\left\{X_{k}, k>M\right\}$ are independent. In this case, $E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)=0$ for $n>M$, so Theorem 8.3.2 implies $S_{n,(n \cdot)} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot)$, where

$$
\sigma^{2}=E X_{0}^{2}+2 \sum_{m=1}^{M} E X_{0} X_{m}
$$

Remark. For a bare-hands approach to this problem, see Theorem 7.3.1 in Chung (1974).

Exercise 8.3.1. Consider the special case of Example 8.3.3 in which $\xi_{i}, i \in \mathbf{Z}$, are i.i.d. and take values $H$ and $T$ with equal probability. Let $\eta_{n}=f\left(\xi_{n}, \xi_{n+1}\right)$, where $f(H, T)=1$ and $f(i, j)=0$ otherwise. $S_{n}=\eta_{1}+\ldots+\eta_{n}$ counts the number of head runs in $\xi_{1}, \ldots, \xi_{n+1}$. Apply Theorem 8.3.2 with $\mathcal{F}_{n}=\sigma\left(X_{m}: m \leq n+1\right)$ to show that there are constants $\mu$ and $\sigma$ so that $\left(S_{n}-n \mu\right) / \sigma n^{1 / 2} \Rightarrow \chi$. What is the random variable $Y_{0}$ constructed in the proof of Theorem 8.3.2 in this case?

Example 8.3.4. Markov chains. Let $\zeta_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$ be an irreducible Markov chain on a countable state space $S$ in which each $\zeta_{n}$ has the stationary distribution $\pi$. Let $X_{n}=f\left(\zeta_{n}\right)$, where $\sum f(x) \pi(x)=0$ and $\sum f(x)^{2} \pi(x)<\infty$. Results in Chapter 6 imply that $X_{n}$ is an ergodic stationary sequence. If we let $\mathcal{F}_{-n}=\sigma\left(\zeta_{m}, m \leq-n\right)$, then

$$
E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)=\sum_{y} p^{n}\left(\zeta_{-n}, y\right) f(y)
$$

where $p^{n}(x, y)$ is the $n$ step transition probability, so

$$
\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2}^{2}=\sum_{x} \pi(x)\left(\sum_{y} p^{n}(x, y) f(y)\right)^{2}
$$

When $f$ is bounded, we can use $\sum f(x) \pi(x)=0$ to get the following bound,

$$
\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2}^{2} \leq\|f\|_{\infty} \sum_{x} \pi(x)\left\|p^{n}(x, \cdot)-\pi(\cdot)\right\|^{2}
$$

where $\|f\|_{\infty}=\sup |f(x)|$ and $\|\cdot\|$ is the total variation norm.
When $S$ is finite, all $f$ are bounded. If the chain is aperiodic, Exercise 5.6 in Chapter 5 implies that

$$
\sup _{x}\left\|p^{n}(x, \cdot)-\pi(\cdot)\right\| \leq C e^{-\epsilon n}
$$

and the hypothesis of Theorem 8.3.2 is satisfied. To see that the limiting variance $\sigma^{2}$ may be 0 in this case, consider the modification of Exercise 7.1 in which $X_{n}=\left(\xi_{n}, \xi_{n+1}\right), f(H, T)=1, f(T, H)=-1$ and $f(H, H)= f(T, T)=0$. In this case, $\sum_{m=1}^{n} f\left(X_{m}\right) \in\{-1,0,1\}$ so there is no central limit theorem.

Our last example is simple to treat directly, but will help us evaluate the strength of the conditions in Theorem 8.3.2 and in later theorems.

Example 8.3.5. Moving average process. Suppose

$$
X_{m}=\sum_{k \geq 0} c_{k} \xi_{m-k} \quad \text { where } \quad \sum_{k \geq 0} c_{k}^{2}<\infty
$$

and the $\xi_{i}, i \in \mathbf{Z}$, are i.i.d. with $E \xi_{i}=0$ and $E \xi_{i}^{2}=1$. If $\mathcal{F}_{-n}=\sigma\left(\xi_{m}\right.$; $m \leq-n)$ then

$$
\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2}=\left\|\sum_{k \geq n} c_{k} \xi_{-k}\right\|_{2}=\left(\sum_{k \geq n} c_{k}^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}
$$

If, for example, $c_{k}=(1+k)^{-p},\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2} \sim n^{(1 / 2)-p}$, and Theorem 8.3.5 applies if $p>3 / 2$.

Remark 8.3.2. Theorem 5.3 in Hall and Heyde (1980) shows that

$$
\sum_{j \geq 0}\left\|E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{0}\right)-E\left(X_{j} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-1}\right)\right\|_{2}<\infty
$$

is sufficient for a central limit theorem. Using this result shows that $\sum\left|c_{k}\right|<\infty$ is sufficient for the central limit theorem in Example 7.3.

The condition in the improved result is close to the best possible. Suppose the $\xi_{i}$ take values 1 and -1 with equal probability and let $c_{k}= (1+k)^{-p}$ where $1 / 2<p<1$. The Lindeberg-Feller theorem can be used to show that $S_{n} / n^{3 / 2-p} \Rightarrow \sigma \chi$. To check the normalization note that

$$
\sum_{m=1}^{n} X_{m}=\sum_{j \leq n} a_{n, j} \xi_{j}
$$

If $j \geq 0$ then $a_{n, j}=\sum_{i=0}^{n-j} c_{k} \approx(n-j)^{1-p}$, so using $0 \leq j \leq n / 2$ the variance is at least $n^{3-2 p}$. Further details are left to the reader.

### 8.3.1 Mixing Properties

The last three examples show that in many cases it is easy to verify the hypothesis of Theorem 8.3.2 directly. To connect Theorem 8.3.2 with other results in the literature, we will introduce sufficient conditions phrased in terms of quantitative measures of dependence between $\sigma$-fields. Our first is

$$
\alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})=\sup \{|P(A \cap B)-P(A) P(B)|: A \in \mathcal{G}, B \in \mathcal{H}\}
$$

If $\alpha=0, \mathcal{G}$ and $\mathcal{H}$ are independent so $\alpha$ measures the dependence between the $\sigma$-fields.

Lemma 8.3.6. Let $p, q, r \in(1, \infty]$ with $1 / p+1 / q+1 / r=1$, and suppose $X \in \mathcal{G}, Y \in \mathcal{H}$ have $E|X|^{p}, E|Y|^{q}<\infty$. Then

$$
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 8\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{q}(\alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H}))^{1 / r}
$$

Here, we interpret $x^{0}=1$ for $x>0$ and $0^{0}=0$.

Proof. If $\alpha=0, X$ and $Y$ are independent and the result is true, so we can suppose $\alpha>0$. We build up to the result in three steps, starting with the case $r=\infty$.

$$
\begin{equation*}
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 2\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{q} \tag{8.3.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Hölder's inequality implies $|E X Y| \leq\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{q}$, and Jensen's inequality implies

$$
\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{q} \geq|E| X|E| Y|\| \geq|E X E Y|
$$

so the result follows from the triangle inequality.

$$
\begin{equation*}
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 4\|X\|_{\infty}\|Y\|_{\infty} \alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H}) \tag{8.3.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Let $\eta=\operatorname{sgn}\{E(Y \mid \mathcal{G})-E Y\} \in \mathcal{G} . E X Y=E(X E(Y \mid \mathcal{G}))$, so

$$
\begin{aligned}
|E X Y-E X E Y| & =|E(X\{E(Y \mid \mathcal{G})-E Y\})| \\
& \leq\|X\|_{\infty} E|E(Y \mid \mathcal{G})-E Y| \\
& =\|X\|_{\infty} E(\eta\{E(Y \mid \mathcal{G})-E Y\}) \\
& =\|X\|_{\infty}\{E(\eta Y)-E \eta E Y\}
\end{aligned}
$$

Applying the last result with $X=Y$ and $Y=\eta$ gives

$$
|E(Y \eta)-E Y E \eta| \leq\|Y\|_{\infty}|E(\zeta \eta)-E \zeta E \eta|
$$

where $\zeta=\operatorname{sgn}\{E(\eta \mid \mathcal{H})-E \eta\}$. Now $\eta=1_{A}-1_{B}$ and $\zeta=1_{C}-1_{D}$, so

$$
\begin{aligned}
|E(\zeta \eta)-E \zeta E \eta| & =\mid P(A \cap C)-P(B \cap C)-P(A \cap D)+P(B \cap D) \\
& -P(A) P(C)+P(B) P(C)+P(A) P(D)-P(B) P(D) \mid \\
& \leq 4 \alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})
\end{aligned}
$$

Combining the last three displays gives the desired result.

$$
\begin{equation*}
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 6\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{\infty} \alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})^{1-1 / p} \tag{8.3.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Let $C=\alpha^{-1 / p}\|X\|_{p}, X_{1}=X 1_{(|X| \leq C)}$ and $X_{2}=X-X_{1}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
|E X Y-E X E Y| & \leq\left|E X_{1} Y-E X_{1} E Y\right|+\left|E X_{2} Y-E X_{2} E Y\right| \\
& \leq 4 \alpha C\|Y\|_{\infty}+2\|Y\|_{\infty} E\left|X_{2}\right|
\end{aligned}
$$

by (8.3.3) and (8.3.2). Now

$$
E\left|X_{2}\right| \leq C^{-(p-1)} E\left(|X|^{p} 1_{(|X|>C)}\right) \leq C^{-p+1} E|X|^{p}
$$

Combining the last two inequalities and using the definition of $C$ gives

$$
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 4 \alpha^{1-1 / p}\|X\|_{p}\|Y\|_{\infty}+2\|Y\|_{\infty} \alpha^{1-1 / p}\|X\|_{p}^{-p+1+p}
$$

which proves (8.3.4)

Finally, to prove Lemma 8.3.6, let $C=\alpha^{-1 / q}\|Y\|_{q}, Y_{1}=Y 1_{(|Y| \leq C)}$, and $Y_{2}=Y-Y_{1}$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
|E X Y-E X E Y| & \leq\left|E X Y_{1}-E X E Y_{1}\right|+\left|E X Y_{2}-E X E Y_{2}\right| \\
& \leq 6 C\|X\|_{p} \alpha^{1-1 / p}+2\|X\|_{p}\left\|Y_{2}\right\|_{\theta}
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\theta=(1-1 / p)^{-1}$ by (8.3.4) and (8.3.2). Now

$$
E\left|Y_{2}\right|^{\theta} \leq C^{-q+\theta} E\left(|Y|^{q} 1_{(|Y|>C)}\right) \leq C^{-q+\theta} E|Y|^{q}
$$

Taking the $1 / \theta$ root of each side and recalling the definition of $C$

$$
\left\|Y_{2}\right\|_{\theta} \leq C^{-(q-\theta) / \theta}\|Y\|_{q}^{q / \theta} \leq \alpha^{(q-\theta) / \theta q}\|Y\|_{q}
$$

so we have

$$
|E X Y-E X E Y| \leq 6 \alpha^{-1 / q}\|Y\|_{q}\|X\|_{p} \alpha^{1-1 / p}+2\|X\|_{p} \alpha^{1 / \theta-1 / q}\|Y\|_{q}^{1 / \theta+1 / q}
$$

proving the desired result.
Remark 8.3.3. The last proof is from Appendix III of Hall and Heyde (1980). They attribute (b) to Ibragimov (1962) and (c) and Lemma 8.3.6 to Davydov (1968).

Theorem 8.3.7. Suppose $X_{n}, n \in \mathbf{Z}$ is an ergodic stationary sequence with $E X_{n}=0, E\left|X_{0}\right|^{2+\delta}<\infty$. Let $\alpha(n)=\alpha\left(\mathcal{F}_{-n}, \sigma\left(X_{0}\right)\right)$, where $\mathcal{F}_{-n}= \sigma\left(X_{m}: m \leq-n\right)$, and suppose

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \alpha(n)^{\delta / 2(2+\delta)}<\infty
$$

If $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$, then $S_{(n \cdot)} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma B(\cdot)$, where

$$
\sigma^{2}=E X_{0}^{2}+2 \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} E X_{0} X_{n}
$$

Remark. If one strengthens the mixing condition one can weaken the summability condition. Let $\bar{\alpha}(n)=\alpha\left(\mathcal{F}_{-n}, \mathcal{F}_{0}^{\prime}\right)$, where $\mathcal{F}_{0}^{\prime}=\sigma\left(X_{k}, k \geq\right.$ 0 ). When $\bar{\alpha}(n) \downarrow 0$, the sequence is called strong mixing. Rosenblatt (1956) introduced the concept as a condition under which the central limit theorem for stationary sequences could be obtained. Ibragimov (1962) proved $S_{n} / \sqrt{n} \Rightarrow \sigma \chi$ where $\sigma^{2}=\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} E S_{n}^{2} / n$ under the assumption

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \bar{\alpha}(n)^{\delta /(2+\delta)}<\infty
$$

See Ibragimov and Linnik (1971), Theorem 18.5.3, or Hall and Heyde (1980), Corollary 5.1, for a proof.

Proof. To use Lemma 8.3.6 to estimate the quantity in Theorem 8.3.2 we begin with

$$
\begin{equation*}
\|E(X \mid \mathcal{F})\|_{2}=\sup \left\{E(X Y): Y \in \mathcal{F},\|Y\|_{2}=1\right\} \tag{8.3.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof of (8.3.5). If $Y \in \mathcal{F}$ with $\|Y\|_{2}=1$, then using a by now familiar property of conditional expectation and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality

$$
E X Y=E(E(X Y \mid \mathcal{F}))=E(Y E(X \mid \mathcal{F})) \leq\|E(X \mid \mathcal{F})\|_{2}\|Y\|_{2}
$$

Equality holds when $Y=E(X \mid \mathcal{F}) /\|E(X \mid \mathcal{F})\|_{2}$.
Letting $p=2+\delta$ and $q=2$ in Lemma 8.3.6, noticing

$$
\frac{1}{r}=1-\frac{1}{p}-\frac{1}{q}=1-\frac{1}{2+\delta}-\frac{1}{2}=\frac{4+2 \delta-2-(2+\delta)}{(2+\delta) 2}=\frac{\delta}{(2+\delta) 2}
$$

and recalling $E X_{0}=0$, shows that if $Y \in \mathcal{F}_{-n}$

$$
\left|E X_{0} Y\right| \leq 8\left\|X_{0}\right\|_{2+\delta}\|Y\|_{2} \alpha(n)^{\delta / 2(2+\delta)}
$$

Combining this with (8.3.5) gives

$$
\left\|E\left(X_{0} \mid \mathcal{F}_{-n}\right)\right\|_{2} \leq 8\left\|X_{0}\right\|_{2+\delta} \alpha(n)^{\delta / 2(2+\delta)}
$$

and it follows that the hypotheses of Theorem 8.3.2 are satisfied.
In the $M$ dependent case (Example 8.3.3), $\alpha(n)=0$ for $n>M$, so Theorem 8.3.7 applies. As for Markov chains (Example 8.3.4), in this case

$$
\begin{aligned}
\alpha(n) & =\sup _{A, B}\left|P\left(X_{-n} \in A, X_{0} \in B\right)-\pi(A) \pi(B)\right| \\
& =\sup _{A, B}\left|\sum_{x \in A, y \in B} \pi(x) p^{n}(x, y)-\pi(x) \pi(y)\right| \\
& \leq \sum_{x} \pi(x) 2\left\|p^{n}(x, \cdot)-\pi(\cdot)\right\|
\end{aligned}
$$

so the hypothesis of Theorem 8.3.7 can be checked if we know enough about the rate of convergence to equilibrium.

Finally, to see how good the conditions in Theorem 8.3.7 are, we consider the special case of the moving average process (Example 8.3.5) in which the $\xi_{i}$ are i.i.d. standard normals. Let

$$
\rho(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})=\sup \{\operatorname{corr}(X, Y): X \in \mathcal{G}, Y \in \mathcal{H}\}
$$

where

$$
\operatorname{corr}(X, Y)=\frac{E X Y-E X E Y}{\|X-E X\|_{2}\|Y-E Y\|_{2}}
$$

Clearly, $\alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H}) \leq \rho(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})$. Kolmogorov and Rozanov (1960) have shown that when $\mathcal{G}$ and $\mathcal{H}$ are generated by Gaussian random variables, then $\rho(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H}) \leq 2 \pi \alpha(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})$. They proved this by showing that $\rho(\mathcal{G}, \mathcal{H})$ is the angle between $L^{2}(\mathcal{G})$ and $L^{2}(\mathcal{H})$. Using the geometric interpretation, we see that if $\left|c_{k}\right|$ is decreasing, then $\alpha(n) \leq \bar{\alpha}(n) \leq\left|c_{n}\right|$, so Theorem 8.3.7 requires $\sum\left|c_{n}\right|^{(1 / 2)-\epsilon}<\infty$, but Ibragimov's result applies if $\sum\left|c_{n}\right|^{1-\epsilon}< \infty$. However as remarked earlier, the central limit theorem is valid if $\sum\left|c_{n}\right|<\infty$.

### 8.4 Empirical Distributions, Brownian Bridge

Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be i.i.d. with distribution distribution $F$. Let

$$
\hat{F}_{n}(x)=\frac{1}{n}\left|\left\{m \leq n: X_{m} \leq x\right\}\right|
$$

be the empirical distribution. In Theorem 2.4.7 add ref we showed
Theorem 8.4.1. The Glivenko-Cantelli theorem. As $n \rightarrow \infty$,

$$
\sup _{x}\left|F_{n}(x)-F(x)\right| \rightarrow 0 \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

In this section, we will investigate the rate of convergence when $F$ is continuous. We impose this restriction so we can reduce to the case of a uniform distribution on ( 0,1 ) by setting $Y_{n}=F\left(X_{n}\right)$. Since $x \rightarrow F(x)$ is nondecreasing and continuous and no observations land in intervals of constancy of $F$, it is easy to see that if we let

$$
\hat{G}_{n}(y)=\frac{1}{n}\left|\left\{m \leq n: Y_{m} \leq y\right\}\right|
$$

then

$$
\sup _{x}\left|\hat{F}_{n}(x)-F(x)\right|=\sup _{0<y<1}\left|\hat{G}_{n}(y)-y\right|
$$

For the rest of the section then, we will assume $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ is i.i.d. uniform on $(0,1)$. To be able to apply Donsker's theorem, we will transform the problem. Put the observations $Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}$ in increasing order: $U_{1}^{n}<U_{2}^{n}< \ldots<U_{n}^{n}$. I claim that

$$
\begin{align*}
\sup _{0<y<1} \hat{G}_{n}(y)-y & =\sup _{1 \leq m \leq n} \frac{m}{n}-U_{m}^{n} \\
\inf _{0<y<1} \hat{G}_{n}(y)-y & =\inf _{1 \leq m \leq n} \frac{m-1}{n}-U_{m}^{n} \tag{8.4.1}
\end{align*}
$$

since the sup occurs at a jump of $\hat{G}_{n}$ and the inf right before a jump. For a picture, see Figure 8.1. We will show that

$$
D_{n} \equiv n^{1 / 2} \sup _{0<y<1}\left|\hat{G}_{n}(y)-y\right|
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/322d99c7-eeb2-406f-855e-59cc960bee81-053.jpg?height=437&width=499&top_left_y=348&top_left_x=829)
Figure 8.1: Picture proof of formulas in (8.4.1).

has a limit, so the extra $-1 / n$ in the inf does not make any difference.
Our third and final maneuver is to give a special construction of the order statistics $U_{1}^{n}<U_{2}^{n} \ldots<U_{n}^{n}$. Let $W_{1}, W_{2}, \ldots$ be i.i.d. with $P\left(W_{i}>\right. t)=e^{-t}$ and let $Z_{n}=W_{1}+\cdots+W_{n}$.

Lemma 8.4.2. $\left\{U_{k}^{n}: 1 \leq k \leq n\right\} \stackrel{d}{=}\left\{Z_{k} / Z_{n+1}: 1 \leq k \leq n\right\}$
Proof. We change variables $v=r(t)$, where $v_{i}=t_{i} / t_{n+1}$ for $i \leq n$, $v_{n+1}=t_{n+1}$. The inverse function is

$$
s(v)=\left(v_{1} v_{n+1}, \ldots, v_{n} v_{n+1}, v_{n+1}\right)
$$

which has matrix of partial derivatives $\partial s_{i} / \partial v_{j}$ given by

$$
\left(\begin{array}{ccccc}
v_{n+1} & 0 & \ldots & 0 & v_{1} \\
0 & v_{n+1} & \ldots & 0 & v_{2} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots \\
0 & 0 & \ldots & v_{n+1} & v_{n} \\
0 & 0 & \ldots & 0 & 1
\end{array}\right)
$$

The determinant of this matrix is $v_{n+1}^{n}$, so if we let $W=\left(V_{1}, \ldots, V_{n+1}\right)= r\left(Z_{1}, \ldots, Z_{n+1}\right)$, the change of variables formula implies $W$ has joint density

$$
f_{W}\left(v_{1}, \ldots, v_{n}, v_{n+1}\right)=\left(\prod_{m=1}^{n} \lambda e^{-\lambda v_{n+1}\left(v_{m}-v_{m-1}\right)}\right) \lambda e^{-\lambda v_{n+1}\left(1-v_{n}\right)} v_{n+1}^{n}
$$

To find the joint density of $V=\left(V_{1}, \ldots, V_{n}\right)$, we simplify the preceding formula and integrate out the last coordinate to get

$$
f_{V}\left(v_{1}, \ldots, v_{n}\right)=\int_{0}^{\infty} \lambda^{n+1} v_{n+1}^{n} e^{-\lambda v_{n+1}} d v_{n+1}=n!
$$

for $0<v_{1}<v_{2} \ldots<v_{n}<1$, which is the desired joint density.

We turn now to the limit law for $D_{n}$. As argued above, it suffices to consider

$$
\begin{align*}
D_{n}^{\prime} & =n^{1 / 2} \max _{1 \leq m \leq n}\left|\frac{Z_{m}}{Z_{n+1}}-\frac{m}{n}\right| \\
& =\frac{n}{Z_{n+1}} \max _{1 \leq m \leq n}\left|\frac{Z_{m}}{n^{1 / 2}}-\frac{m}{n} \cdot \frac{Z_{n+1}}{n^{1 / 2}}\right| \\
& =\frac{n}{Z_{n+1}} \max _{1 \leq m \leq n}\left|\frac{Z_{m}-m}{n^{1 / 2}}-\frac{m}{n} \cdot \frac{Z_{n+1}-n}{n^{1 / 2}}\right| \tag{8.4.2}
\end{align*}
$$

If we let

$$
B_{n}(t)= \begin{cases}\left(Z_{m}-m\right) / n^{1 / 2} & \text { if } t=m / n \text { with } m \in\{0,1, \ldots, n\} \\ \text { linear } & \text { on }[(m-1) / n, m / n]\end{cases}
$$

then

$$
D_{n}^{\prime}=\frac{n}{Z_{n+1}} \max _{0 \leq t \leq 1}\left|B_{n}(t)-t\left\{B_{n}(1)+\frac{Z_{n+1}-Z_{n}}{n^{1 / 2}}\right\}\right|
$$

The strong law of large numbers implies $Z_{n+1} / n \rightarrow 1$ a.s., so the first factor will disappear in the limit. To find the limit of the second, we observe that Donsker's theorem, Theorem 8.1.4, implies $B_{n}(\cdot) \Rightarrow B(\cdot)$, a Brownian motion, and computing second moments shows

$$
\left(Z_{n+1}-Z_{n}\right) / n^{1 / 2} \rightarrow 0 \text { in probability }
$$

$\psi(\omega)=\max _{0 \leq t \leq 1}|\omega(t)-t \omega(1)|$ is a continuous function from $C[0,1]$ to $\mathbf{R}$, so it follows from Donsker's theorem that:
Theorem 8.4.3. $D_{n} \Rightarrow \max _{0 \leq t \leq 1}\left|B_{t}-t B_{1}\right|$, where $B_{t}$ is a Brownian motion starting at 0 .
Remark. Doob (1949) suggested this approach to deriving results of Kolmogorov and Smirnov, which was later justified by Donsker (1952). Our proof follows Breiman (1968).

To identify the distribution of the limit in Theorem 8.4.3, we will first prove

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left\{B_{t}-t B_{1}, 0 \leq t \leq 1\right\} \stackrel{d}{=}\left\{B_{t}, 0 \leq t \leq 1 \mid B_{1}=0\right\} \tag{8.4.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

a process we will denote by $B_{t}^{0}$ and call the Brownian bridge. The event $B_{1}=0$ has probability 0 , but it is easy to see what the conditional probability should mean. If $0=t_{0}<t_{1}<\ldots<t_{n}<t_{n+1}=1, x_{0}=0$, $x_{n+1}=0$, and $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n} \in \mathbf{R}$, then

$$
\begin{align*}
& P\left(B\left(t_{1}\right)=x_{1}, \ldots, B\left(t_{n}\right)\right.\left.=x_{n} \mid B(1)=0\right) \\
& \quad=\frac{1}{p_{1}(0,0)} \prod_{m=1}^{n+1} p_{t_{m}-t_{m-1}}\left(x_{m-1}, x_{m}\right) \tag{8.4.4}
\end{align*}
$$

where $p_{t}(x, y)=(2 \pi t)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-(y-x)^{2} / 2 t\right)$.
Proof of (8.4.3). Formula (8.4.4) shows that the f.d.d.'s of $B_{t}^{0}$ are multivariate normal and have mean 0 . Since $B_{t}-t B_{1}$ also has this property, it suffices to show that the covariances are equal. We begin with the easier computation. If $s<t$ then

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left(\left(B_{s}-s B_{1}\right)\left(B_{t}-t B_{1}\right)\right)=s-s t-s t+s t=s(1-t) \tag{8.4.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

For the other process, $P\left(B_{s}^{0}=x, B_{t}^{0}=y\right)$ is

$$
\begin{align*}
& \frac{\exp \left(-x^{2} / 2 s\right)}{(2 \pi s)^{1 / 2}} \cdot \frac{\exp \left(-(y-x)^{2} / 2(t-s)\right)}{(2 \pi(t-s))^{1 / 2}} \cdot \frac{\exp \left(-y^{2} / 2(1-t)\right)}{(2 \pi(1-t))^{1 / 2}} \cdot(2 \pi)^{1 / 2} \\
& \quad=(2 \pi)^{-1}(s(t-s)(1-t))^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-\left(a x^{2}+2 b x y+c y^{2}\right) / 2\right) \tag{8.4.6}
\end{align*}
$$

where

$$
\begin{aligned}
& a=\frac{1}{s}+\frac{1}{t-s}=\frac{t}{s(t-s)} \quad b=-\frac{1}{t-s} \\
& c=\frac{1}{t-s}+\frac{1}{1-t}=\frac{1-s}{(t-s)(1-t)}
\end{aligned}
$$

Recalling the discussion at the end of Section 3.9 and noticing

$$
\left(\begin{array}{cc}
\frac{t}{s(t-s)} & \frac{-1}{(t-s)} \\
\frac{-1}{(t-s)} & \frac{1-s}{(t-s)(1-t)}
\end{array}\right)^{-1}=\left(\begin{array}{cc}
s(1-s) & s(1-t) \\
s(1-t) & t(1-t)
\end{array}\right)
$$

(multiply the matrices!) shows (8.4.3) holds.
Remark. To do computations with the joint density it is useful to know that if ( $X, Y$ ) have a bivariate normal distribution with mean 0 $E X^{2}=\sigma_{X}^{2}, E Y^{2}=\sigma_{Y}$, and correlation $\rho$ then the joint density is given by

$$
\frac{1}{2 \pi \sigma_{X} \sigma_{Y} \sqrt{1-\rho^{2}}} \exp \left[-\frac{1}{2\left(1-\rho^{2}\right)}\left(\frac{x^{2}}{\sigma_{X}^{2}}-\frac{2 x y}{\sigma_{X} \sigma_{Y}}+\frac{y^{2}}{\sigma_{Y}^{2}}\right)\right]
$$

In (8.4.6) if $X=B_{s}^{0}$ and $Y=B_{s}^{0}$ then $\sigma_{X}=\sqrt{s(1-s)}, \sigma_{Y}=\sqrt{t(1-t)}$ and

$$
\rho=\frac{s(1-t)}{\sqrt{s(1-s) t(1-t)}}=\sqrt{\frac{s(1-t)}{(1-s) t}}
$$

so we have

$$
1-\rho^{2}-\frac{(1-s) t-s(1-t)}{(1-s) t}=\frac{t-s}{(1-s) t}
$$

We leave it to the reader to check the well-known fact that the conditional distribution

$$
(Y \mid X=x)=\operatorname{normal}\left(x \rho \sigma_{Y} / \sigma_{X},\left(1-\rho^{2}\right) \sigma_{Y}^{2}\right)
$$

which in our special case implies

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left.\left(B_{t}^{0} \mid B_{s}^{0}=x\right)=\operatorname{normal}(x(1-t) /(1-s),(1-t)(1-s) / 1-s)\right) \tag{8.4.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

Our final step in investigating the limit distribution of $D_{n}$ is to compute the distribution of $\max _{0 \leq t \leq 1}\left|B_{t}^{0}\right|$. To do this, we first prove

Theorem 8.4.4. The density function of $B_{t}$ on $\left\{T_{a} \wedge T_{b}>t\right\}$ is

$$
\begin{align*}
& P_{x}\left(T_{a} \wedge T_{b}>t, B_{t}=y\right)=\sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty} P_{x}\left(B_{t}=y+2 n(b-a)\right)  \tag{8.4.8}\\
& -P_{x}\left(B_{t}=2 b-y+2 n(b-a)\right)
\end{align*}
$$

Figure 8.2: Picture of the infinite series in (8.4.8). Note that the array of + and - is anti-symmetric when seen from $a$ or $b$.

Proof. We begin by observing that if $A \subset(a, b)$

$$
\begin{align*}
P_{x}\left(T_{a} \wedge T_{b}>t, B_{t} \in A\right) & =P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in A\right)-P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, T_{a}<t, B_{t} \in A\right) \\
& -P_{x}\left(T_{b}<T_{a}, T_{b}<t, B_{t} \in A\right) \tag{8.4.9}
\end{align*}
$$

If we let $\rho_{a}(y)=2 a-y$ be reflection through $a$ and observe that $\left\{T_{a}<T_{b}\right\}$ is $\mathcal{F}\left(T_{a}\right)$ measurable, then it follows from the proof of (7.4.5) that

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, T_{a}<t, B_{t} \in A\right)=P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right)
$$

where $\rho_{a} A=\left\{\rho_{a}(y): y \in A\right\}$. To get rid of the $T_{a}<T_{b}$, we observe that

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right)=P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right)-P_{x}\left(T_{b}<T_{a}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right)
$$

Noticing that $B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A$ and $T_{b}<T_{a}$ imply $T_{b}<t$ and using the reflection principle again gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{x}\left(T_{b}<T_{a}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right) & =P_{x}\left(T_{b}<T_{a}, B_{t} \in \rho_{b} \rho_{a} A\right) \\
& =P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{b} \rho_{a} A\right)-P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in \rho_{b} \rho_{a} A\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Repeating the last two calculations $n$ more times gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right) & =\sum_{m=0}^{n} P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{a}\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{m} A\right)-P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{m+1} A\right) \\
& +P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{n+1} A\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Each pair of reflections pushes $A$ further away from 0 , so letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ shows

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{a}<T_{b}, B_{t} \in \rho_{a} A\right)=\sum_{m=0}^{\infty} P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{a}\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{m} A\right)-P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{m+1} A\right)
$$

Interchanging the roles of a and $b$ gives

$$
P_{x}\left(T_{b}<T_{a}, B_{t} \in \rho_{b} A\right)=\sum_{m=0}^{\infty} P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{b}\left(\rho_{a} \rho_{b}\right)^{m} A\right)-P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in\left(\rho_{a} \rho_{b}\right)^{m+1} A\right)
$$

Combining the last two expressions with (8.4.9) and using $\rho_{c}^{-1}=\rho_{c}$, $\left(\rho_{a} \rho_{b}\right)^{-1}=\rho_{b}^{-1} \rho_{a}^{-1}$ gives
$P_{x}\left(T_{a} \wedge T_{b}>t, B_{t} \in A\right)=\sum_{m=-\infty}^{\infty} P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{n} A\right)-P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in \rho_{a}\left(\rho_{b} \rho_{a}\right)^{n} A\right)$
To prepare for applications, let $A=(u, v)$ where $a<u<v<b$, notice that $\rho_{b} \rho_{a}(y)=y+2(b-a)$, and change variables in the second sum to get

$$
\begin{align*}
& P_{x}\left(T_{a} \wedge T_{b}>t, u<B_{t}<v\right)= \\
& \quad \sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty}\left\{P_{x}\left(u+2 n(b-a)<B_{t}<v+2 n(b-a)\right)\right.  \tag{8.4.10}\\
& \left.\quad-P_{x}\left(2 b-v+2 n(b-a)<B_{t}<2 b-u+2 n(b-a)\right)\right\}
\end{align*}
$$

Letting $u=y-\epsilon, v=y+\epsilon$, dividing both sides by $2 \epsilon$, and letting $\epsilon \rightarrow 0$ (leaving it to the reader to check that the dominated convergence theorem applies) gives the desired result.

Setting $x=y=0, t=1$, and dividing by $(2 \pi)^{-1 / 2}=P_{0}\left(B_{1}=0\right)$, we get a result for the Brownian bridge $B_{t}^{0}$ :

$$
\begin{align*}
P_{0}(a & \left.<\min _{0 \leq t \leq 1} B_{t}^{0}<\max _{0 \leq t \leq 1} B_{t}^{0}<b\right)  \tag{8.4.11}\\
& =\sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-(2 n(b-a))^{2} / 2}-e^{-(2 b+2 n(b-a))^{2} / 2}
\end{align*}
$$

Taking $a=-b$, we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}\left(\max _{0 \leq t \leq 1}\left|B_{t}^{0}\right|<b\right)=\sum_{m=-\infty}^{\infty}(-1)^{m} e^{-2 m^{2} b^{2}} \tag{8.4.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

This formula gives the distribution of the Kolmogorv-Smirnov statistic, which can be used to test if an i.i.d. sequence $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ has distribution $F$. To do this, we transform the data to $F\left(X_{n}\right)$ and look at the maximum discrepancy between the empirical distribution and the uniform. (8.4.12) tells us the distribution of the error when the $X_{i}$ have distribution $F$.

Exercises
8.4.1. Use Exercise 7.4.8 and the reasoning that led to (8.4.11) to conclude

$$
P\left(\max _{0 \leq t \leq 1} B_{t}^{0}>b\right)=\exp \left(-2 b^{2}\right)
$$

8.4.2. Let $B_{t}$ be a Brownian motion starting at 0 . Show that $X_{t}= (1-t) B(t / 1-t)$ is a Brownian bridge.
8.4.3. Show that Brownian bridge is a Markov process by checking that if $s_{1}<\ldots s_{n}<s<t<1$
$P\left(B(t)=y \mid B(s)=x, B\left(s_{n}\right)=x_{n}, \ldots B\left(s_{1}\right)=x_{1}\right)=P(B(t)=y \mid B(s)=x)$
8.4.4. Let $B_{t}^{0}$ be Brownian bridge. Show that $X_{t}=B_{t}^{0} /(1-t)$ is a martingale but is not $L^{2}$ bounded.

### 8.5 Laws of the Iterated Logarithm

Our first goal is to show:

## Theorem 8.5.1. LIL for Brownian motion.

$$
\limsup _{t \rightarrow \infty} B_{t} /(2 t \log \log t)^{1 / 2}=1 \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

Here LIL is short for "law of the iterated logarithm," a name that refers to the $\log \log t$ in the denominator. Once Theorem 8.5.1 is established, we can use the Skorokhod representation to prove the analogous result for random walks with mean 0 and finite variance.

Proof. The key to the proof is (7.4.4).

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{0}\left(\max _{0 \leq s \leq 1} B_{s}>a\right)=P_{0}\left(T_{a} \leq 1\right)=2 P_{0}\left(B_{1} \geq a\right) \tag{8.5.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

To bound the right-hand side, we the result in use Lemma 1.2.6. For $x>0$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left(x^{-1}-x^{-3}\right) \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2\right) \leq \int_{x}^{\infty} \exp \left(-y^{2} / 2\right) d y \leq x^{-1} \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2\right) \tag{8.5.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

and hence

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{x}^{\infty} \exp \left(-y^{2} / 2\right) d y \sim \frac{1}{x} \exp \left(-x^{2} / 2\right) \quad \text { as } x \rightarrow \infty \tag{8.5.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

(8.5.3) and Brownian scaling imply that

$$
P_{0}\left(B_{t}>(t f(t))^{1 / 2}\right) \sim \kappa f(t)^{-1 / 2} \exp (-f(t) / 2)
$$

where $\kappa=(2 \pi)^{-1 / 2}$ is a constant that we will try to ignore below. The last result implies that if $\epsilon>0$, then

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P_{0}\left(B_{n}>(n f(n))^{1 / 2}\right) \begin{cases}<\infty & \text { when } f(n)=(2+\epsilon) \log n \\ =\infty & \text { when } f(n)=(2-\epsilon) \log n\end{cases}
$$

and hence by the Borel-Cantelli lemma that

$$
\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} B_{n} /(2 n \log n)^{1 / 2} \leq 1 \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

To replace $\log n$ by $\log \log n$, we have to look along exponentially growing sequences. Let $t_{n}=\alpha^{n}$, where $\alpha>1$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{0}\left(\max _{t_{n} \leq s \leq t_{n+1}} B_{s}>\left(t_{n} f\left(t_{n}\right)\right)^{1 / 2}\right) & \leq P_{0}\left(\max _{0 \leq s \leq t_{n+1}} B_{s} / t_{n+1}^{1 / 2}>\left(\frac{f\left(t_{n}\right)}{\alpha}\right)^{1 / 2}\right) \\
& \leq 2 \kappa\left(f\left(t_{n}\right) / \alpha\right)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-f\left(t_{n}\right) / 2 \alpha\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

by (8.5.1) and (8.5.2). If $f(t)=2 \alpha^{2} \log \log t$, then

$$
\log \log t_{n}=\log (n \log \alpha)=\log n+\log \log \alpha
$$

so $\exp \left(-f\left(t_{n}\right) / 2 \alpha\right) \leq C_{\alpha} n^{-\alpha}$, where $C_{\alpha}$ is a constant that depends only on $\alpha$, and hence

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P_{0}\left(\max _{t_{n} \leq s \leq t_{n+1}} B_{s}>\left(t_{n} f\left(t_{n}\right)\right)^{1 / 2}\right)<\infty
$$

Since $t \rightarrow(t f(t))^{1 / 2}$ is increasing and $\alpha>1$ is arbitrary, it follows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\limsup B_{t} /(2 t \log \log t)^{1 / 2} \leq 1 \tag{8.5.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

To prove the other half of Theorem 8.5.1, again let $t_{n}=\alpha^{n}$, but this time $\alpha$ will be large, since to get independent events, we will we look at

$$
P_{0}\left(B\left(t_{n+1}\right)-B\left(t_{n}\right)>\left(t_{n+1} f\left(t_{n+1}\right)\right)^{1 / 2}\right)=P_{0}\left(B_{1}>\left(\beta f\left(t_{n+1}\right)\right)^{1 / 2}\right)
$$

where $\beta=t_{n+1} /\left(t_{n+1}-t_{n}\right)=\alpha /(\alpha-1)>1$. The last quantity is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\geq \frac{\kappa}{2}\left(\beta f\left(t_{n+1}\right)\right)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-\beta f\left(t_{n+1}\right) / 2\right. \tag{2}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $n$ is large by (8.5.3). If $f(t)=\left(2 / \beta^{2}\right) \log \log t$, then $\log \log t_{n}=\log n+ \log \log \alpha$ so

$$
\exp \left(-\beta f\left(t_{n+1}\right) / 2\right) \geq C_{\alpha} n^{-1 / \beta}
$$

where $C_{\alpha}$ is a constant that depends only on $\alpha$, and hence

$$
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P_{0}\left(B\left(t_{n+1}\right)-B\left(t_{n}\right)>\left(t_{n+1} f\left(t_{n+1}\right)\right)^{1 / 2}\right)=\infty
$$

Since the events in question are independent, it follows from the second Borel-Cantelli lemma that

$$
\begin{equation*}
B\left(t_{n+1}\right)-B\left(t_{n}\right)>\left(\left(2 / \beta^{2}\right) t_{n+1} \log \log t_{n+1}\right)^{1 / 2} \quad \text { i.o. } \tag{8.5.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

From (8.5.4), we get

$$
\begin{equation*}
\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} B\left(t_{n}\right) /\left(2 t_{n} \log \log t_{n}\right)^{1 / 2} \leq 1 \tag{8.5.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $t_{n}=t_{n+1} / \alpha$ and $t \rightarrow \log \log t$ is increasing, combining (8.5.5) and (8.5.6), and recalling $\beta=\alpha /(\alpha-1)$ gives

$$
\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} B\left(t_{n+1}\right) /\left(2 t_{n+1} \log \log t_{n+1}\right)^{1 / 2} \geq \frac{\alpha-1}{\alpha}-\frac{1}{\alpha^{1 / 2}}
$$

Letting $\alpha \rightarrow \infty$ now gives the desired lower bound, and the proof of Theorem 8.5.1 is complete.

Turning to random walk, we will prove a result due to Hartman and Wintner (1941):

Theorem 8.5.2. If $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are i.i.d. with $E X_{i}=0$ and $E X_{i}^{2}=1$ then

$$
\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty} S_{n} /(2 n \log \log n)^{1 / 2}=1
$$

Proof. By Theorem 8.1.2, we can write $S_{n}=B\left(T_{n}\right)$ with $T_{n} / n \rightarrow 1$ a.s. As in the proof of Donsker's theorem, this is all we will use in the argument below. Theorem 8.5.2 will follow from Theorem 8.5.1 once we show

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left(S_{[t]}-B_{t}\right) /(t \log \log t)^{1 / 2} \rightarrow 0 \quad \text { a.s. } \tag{8.5.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

To do this, we begin by observing that if $\epsilon>0$ and $t \geq t_{o}(\omega)$

$$
\begin{equation*}
T_{[t]} \in[t /(1+\epsilon), t(1+\epsilon)] \tag{8.5.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

To estimate $S_{[t]}-B_{t}$, we let $M(t)=\sup \{|B(s)-B(t)|: t /(1+\epsilon) \leq s \leq t(1+\epsilon)\}$. To control the last quantity, we let $t_{k}=(1+\epsilon)^{k}$ and notice that if $t_{k} \leq t \leq t_{k+1}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
M(t) & \leq \sup \left\{|B(s)-B(t)|: t_{k-1} \leq s, t \leq t_{k+2}\right\} \\
& \leq 2 \sup \left\{\left|B(s)-B\left(t_{k-1}\right)\right|: t_{k-1} \leq s \leq t_{k+2}\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

Noticing $t_{k+2}-t_{k-1}=\delta t_{k-1}$, where $\delta=(1+\epsilon)^{3}-1$, scaling implies

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left(\max _{t_{k-1} \leq s \leq t_{k+2}}|B(s)-B(t)|>\left(3 \delta t_{k-1} \log \log t_{k-1}\right)^{1 / 2}\right) \\
& \quad=P\left(\max _{0 \leq r \leq 1}|B(r)|>\left(3 \log \log t_{k-1}\right)^{1 / 2}\right) \\
& \quad \leq 2 \kappa\left(3 \log \log t_{k-1}\right)^{-1 / 2} \exp \left(-3 \log \log t_{k-1} / 2\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

by a now familiar application of (8.5.1) and (8.5.2). Summing over $k$ and using (b) gives

$$
\limsup _{t \rightarrow \infty}\left(S_{[t]}-B_{t}\right) /(t \log \log t)^{1 / 2} \leq(3 \delta)^{1 / 2}
$$

If we recall $\delta=(1+\epsilon)^{3}-1$ and let $\epsilon \downarrow 0$, (a) follows and the proof is complete.

Strassen (1965) has shown an exact converse. If Theorem 8.5.2 holds then $E X_{i}=0$ and $E X_{i}^{2}=1$. Another one of his contributions to this subject is
Theorem 8.5.3. Strassen's (1964) invariance principle. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be i.i.d. with $E X_{i}=0$ and $E X_{i}^{2}=1$, let $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$, and let $S_{(n .)}$ be the usual linear interpolation. The limit set (i.e., the collection of limits of convergent subsequences) of

$$
Z_{n}(\cdot)=(2 n \log \log n)^{-1 / 2} S(n \cdot) \quad \text { for } n \geq 3
$$

is $\mathcal{K}=\left\{f: f(x)=\int_{0}^{x} g(y) d y\right.$ with $\left.\int_{0}^{1} g(y)^{2} d y \leq 1\right\}$.
Jensen's inequality implies $f(1)^{2} \leq \int_{0}^{1} g(y)^{2} d y \leq 1$ with equality if and only if $f(t)=t$, so Theorem 8.5.3 contains Theorem 8.5.2 as a special case and provides some information about how the large value of $S_{n}$ came about.

## Exercises

8.5.1. Show that if $E\left|X_{i}\right|^{\alpha}=\infty$ for some $\alpha<2$ then

$$
\limsup _{n \rightarrow \infty}\left|X_{n}\right| / n^{1 / \alpha}=\infty \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

so the law of the iterated logarithm fails.
8.5.2. Give a direct proof that, under the hypotheses of Theorem 8.5.3, the limit set of $\left\{S_{n} /(2 n \log \log n)^{1 / 2}\right\}$ is $[-1,1]$.

## Chapter 9

## Multidimensional Brownian Motion

The point of this chapter is to establish the connection between Brownian motion and various parabolic and elliptic partial differential equations. The first section uses martingales to do computations for multidimensional Brownian motion.

### 9.1 Martingales

Let $\Delta f=\sum_{i=1}^{d} \partial^{2} f / \partial x_{i}^{2}$ be the Laplacian of $f$. Combining Ito's formula (7.6.9) with Theorem 7.6.4

Theorem 9.1.1. Suppose $v \in C^{2}$, i.e., all first and second order partial derivatives exist and are continuous, and $E \int_{0}^{t}\left|\nabla v\left(B_{s}\right)\right|^{2} d s<\infty$ for all $t$. Then

$$
v\left(B_{t}\right)-\int_{0}^{t} \frac{1}{2} \Delta v\left(B_{s}\right) d s \quad \text { is a continuous martingale. }
$$

Let $S_{r}=\inf \left\{t:\left|B_{t}\right|=r\right\}, r<R$, and $\tau=S_{r} \wedge S_{R}$. The first detail is to note that if $|x|<R$ then $P_{x}\left(S_{R}<\infty\right)=1$. Once we know this we can conclude

Theorem 9.1.2. If $|x|<R$ then $E_{x} S_{R}=\left(R^{2}-|x|^{2}\right) / d$.
Proof. It follows from Theorem 7.5.4 that $\left|B_{t}\right|^{2}-d t=\sum_{i=1}^{d}\left(B_{t}^{i}\right)^{2}-t$ is a martingale. Theorem 7.5.1 implies $|x|^{2}=E\left|B_{S_{R} \wedge t}\right|^{2}-d E\left(S_{R} \wedge t\right)$. Letting $t \rightarrow \infty$ gives the desired result. $\square$

To further investigate Brownian motion we introduce two new functions:

$$
\varphi(x)= \begin{cases}\log |x| & d=2 \\ |x|^{2-d} & d \geq 3\end{cases}
$$

We leave it to the reader to check that in each case $\Delta \varphi=0$.
Lemma 9.1.3. $\varphi(x)=E_{x} \varphi\left(B_{\tau}\right)$
Proof. Define $\psi(x)=g(|x|)$ to be $C^{2}$ and have compact support, and have $\psi(x)=\phi(x)$ when $r<|x|<R$. Theorem 9.1.1 implies that $\psi(x)= E_{x} \psi\left(B_{t \wedge \tau}\right)$. Letting $t \rightarrow \infty$ now gives the desired result.

Lemma 9.1.3 implies that

$$
\varphi(x)=\varphi(r) P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)+\varphi(R)\left(1-P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)\right)
$$

where $\varphi(r)$ is short for the value of $\varphi(x)$ on $\{x:|x|=r\}$. Solving now gives

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)=\frac{\varphi(R)-\varphi(x)}{\varphi(R)-\varphi(r)} \tag{9.1.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

In $d=2$, the last formula says

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)=\frac{\log R-\log |x|}{\log R-\log r} \tag{9.1.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

If we fix $r$ and let $R \rightarrow \infty$ in (9.1.2), the right-hand side goes to 1 . So

$$
P_{x}\left(S_{r}<\infty\right)=1 \quad \text { for any } x \text { and any } r>0
$$

It follows that two-dimensional Brownian motion is recurrent in the sense that if $G$ is any open set, then $P_{x}\left(B_{t} \in G\right.$ i.o. $) \equiv 1$.

If we fix $R$, let $r \rightarrow 0$ in (9.1.2), and let $S_{0}=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t}=0\right\}$, then for $x \neq 0$

$$
P_{x}\left(S_{0}<S_{R}\right) \leq \lim _{r \rightarrow 0} P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)=0
$$

Since this holds for all $R$ and since the continuity of Brownian paths implies $S_{R} \uparrow \infty$ as $R \uparrow \infty$, we have $P_{x}\left(S_{0}<\infty\right)=0$ for all $x \neq 0$. To extend the last result to $x=0$ we note that the Markov property implies

$$
P_{0}\left(B_{t}=0 \text { for some } t \geq \epsilon\right)=E_{0}\left[P_{B_{\epsilon}}\left(T_{0}<\infty\right)\right]=0
$$

for all $\epsilon>0$, so $P_{0}\left(B_{t}=0\right.$ for some $\left.t>0\right)=0$, and thanks to our definition of $S_{0}=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t}=0\right\}$, we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}\left(S_{0}<\infty\right)=0 \quad \text { for all } x \tag{9.1.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus, in $d \geq 2$ Brownian motion will not hit 0 at a positive time even if it starts there. As a corollary we see that Brownian motion in $d=3$ will not hit a straight line.

For $d \geq 3$, formula (9.1.1) says

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}\left(S_{r}<S_{R}\right)=\frac{R^{2-d}-|x|^{2-d}}{R^{2-d}-r^{2-d}} \tag{9.1.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

There is no point in fixing $R$ and letting $r \rightarrow 0$, here. The fact that two dimensional Brownian motion does not hit 0 implies that three dimensional Brownian motion does not hit 0 and indeed will not hit the line $\left\{x: x_{1}=x_{2}=0\right\}$. If we fix $r$ and let $R \rightarrow \infty$ in (9.1.4) we get

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}\left(S_{r}<\infty\right)=(r /|x|)^{d-2}<1 \quad \text { if }|x|>r \tag{9.1.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

From the last result it follows easily that for $d \geq 3$, Brownian motion is transient, i.e., it does not return infinitely often to any bounded set.

Theorem 9.1.4. As $t \rightarrow \infty,\left|B_{t}\right| \rightarrow \infty$ a.s.
Proof. Let $A_{n}=\left\{\left|B_{t}\right|>n^{1-\epsilon}\right.$ for all $\left.t \geq S_{n}\right\}$. The strong Markov property implies

$$
P_{x}\left(A_{n}^{c}\right)=E_{x}\left(P_{B\left(S_{n}\right)}\left(S_{n^{1-\epsilon}}<\infty\right)\right)=\left(n^{1-\epsilon} / n\right)^{d-2} \rightarrow 0
$$

as $n \rightarrow \infty$. Now $\limsup A_{n}=\cap_{N=1}^{\infty} \cup_{n=N}^{\infty} A_{n}$ has

$$
P\left(\limsup A_{n}\right) \geq \limsup P\left(A_{n}\right)=1
$$

So infinitely often the Brownian path never returns to $\left\{x:|x| \leq n^{1-\epsilon}\right\}$ after time $S_{n}$ and this implies the desired result.

The scaling relation (7.1.1) implies that $S_{n}={ }_{d} n^{2} S_{1}$, so the proof of Theorem 9.1.4 suggests that

$$
\left|B_{t}\right| / t^{(1-\epsilon) / 2} \rightarrow \infty
$$

Dvoretsky and Erdös (1951) have proved the following result about how fast Brownian motion goes to $\infty$ in $d \geq 3$.

Theorem 9.1.5. Suppose $g(t)$ is positive and decreasing. Then

$$
P_{0}\left(\left|B_{t}\right| \leq g(t) \sqrt{t} \text { i.o. as } t \uparrow \infty\right)=1 \text { or } 0
$$

according as $\int^{\infty} g(t)^{d-2} / t d t=\infty$ or $<\infty$.
Here the absence of the lower limit implies that we are only concerned with the behavior of the integral "near $\infty$." A little calculus shows that

$$
\int^{\infty} t^{-1} \log ^{-\alpha} t d t=\infty \text { or }<\infty
$$

according as $\alpha \leq 1$ or $\alpha>1$, so $B_{t}$ goes to $\infty$ faster than

$$
t^{1 / 2}(\log t)^{-\alpha /(d-2)} \quad \text { for any } \alpha>1
$$

## Exercises

9.1.1. Combine scaling with the $0-1$ law to show that

$$
\liminf _{t \rightarrow \infty}\left|B_{t}\right| / t^{1 / 2}=0 \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

9.1.2. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$ is nonnegative and superharmonic. Show that in dimensions 1 and 2, $f$ must be constant. Give an example of the form $f(x)=g(|x|)$ in $d \geq 3$.
9.1.3. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$ is bounded and harmonic. Then $f$ must be constant (in any dimension).

### 9.2 Heat Equation

In this section, we will consider the following equation:
(a) $u \in C^{1,2}$ and $u_{t}=\frac{1}{2} \Delta u$ in $(0, \infty) \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$.
(b) $u$ is continuous at each point of $\{0\} \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ and $u(0, x)=f(x)$, where $f$ is bounded and continuous.
This equation derives its name from the fact that if the units of measurement are chosen suitably then the solution $u(t, x)$ gives the temperature at the point $x \in R^{d}$ at time $t$ when the temperature profile at time 0 is given by $f(x)$.

Obviously (b) cannot hold unless $f$ is continuous. The results below can be generalized to unbounded $f$. We assume $f$ is bounded to keep things simple. In this and the next five sections the assumptions stated at the beginning are in force throughout the section.

The first step in solving this equation, as it will be six times below, is to find a local martingale $M_{s}, s \in[0, t]$, i.e., a process so that there is an increasing sequence of stopping times so that $M\left(s \wedge T_{n}\right)$ is a martingale and $P\left(T_{n}<t\right) \rightarrow 0$.
Theorem 9.2.1. If $u$ satisfies ( $a$ ), $M_{s}=u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)$ is a local martingale on $[0, t]$.

Proof. Applying Itô's formula, (7.6.9) gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
& u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)-u\left(t, B_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{s}-u_{t}\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) d r \\
& +\int_{0}^{s} \nabla u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \cdot d B_{r}+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{s} \Delta u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) d r
\end{aligned}
$$

The result follows since $-u_{t}+\frac{1}{2} \Delta u=0$ and the second term on the right-hand side is a local martingale by Theorem 7.6.4. Here we need the stopping times to make sure the integrability condition holds.

Our next step is to prove a uniqueness theorem.

Theorem 9.2.2. If there is a solution that is bounded then it must be

$$
v(t, x) \equiv E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)
$$

Here $\equiv$ means that the last equation defines $v$. We will always use $u$ for a generic solution of the equation and $v$ for our special solution.

Proof. If we now assume that $u$ is bounded, then $M_{s}, 0 \leq s<t$, is a bounded martingale. The martingale convergence theorem implies that

$$
M_{t} \equiv \lim _{s \uparrow t} M_{s} \quad \text { exists a.s. }
$$

If $u$ satisfies (b), this limit must be $f\left(B_{t}\right)$. Since $M_{s}$ is uniformly integrable, it follows that $u(t, x)=E_{x} M_{0}=E_{x} M_{t}=v(t, x)$.

Now that Theorem 9.2.2 has told us what the solution must be, the next logical step is to find conditions under which $v$ is a solution. Here and in the other five examples it is easy to show that if $v$ is smooth enough then it is a solution.
Theorem 9.2.3. If $v \in C^{1,2}$ then it satisfies (a).
Proof. If we think of running space time Brownian motion ( $t-s, B_{s}$ ) to construct the solution then we see

$$
v(t+h, x)=E_{x} v\left(t, B_{h}\right)
$$

Subtracting $v(t, x)$ from each side and using Taylor series

$$
\begin{aligned}
v(t+h, x)-v(t, x)= & E_{x}\left(\sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(t, x)\left(B_{i}(h)-x_{i}\right)\right. \\
& \left.+\frac{1}{2} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(t, x)\left(B_{i}(h)-x_{i}\right)\left(B_{j}(h)-x_{j}\right)\right)+o(h)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, B_{i}(t), B_{i}^{2}(t)-t$, and $B_{i}(t) B_{j}(t)$ are martingales

$$
v(t+h, x)-v(t, x)=\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i}^{2}}(t, x) h+o(h)
$$

Dividing by $h$ and letting $h \rightarrow 0$ gives the desired result.
Theorem 9.2.4. v satisfies (b).
Proof. $\left(B_{t}-B_{0}\right) \stackrel{d}{=} t^{1 / 2} \chi$, where $\chi$ has a normal distribution with mean 0 and variance 1 , so if $t_{n} \rightarrow 0$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow x$, the bounded convergence theorem implies that

$$
v\left(t_{n}, x_{n}\right)=E f\left(x_{n}+t_{n}^{1 / 2} \chi\right) \rightarrow f(x)
$$

which proves the desired result.

The final step in showing that $v$ is a solution is to find conditions that guarantee that it is smooth.
Theorem 9.2.5. $v \in C^{1,2}$ and hence satisfies (a).
Proof. Our solution is

$$
v(t, x)=E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)=\int(2 \pi t)^{-d / 2} e^{-|x-y|^{2} / 2 t} f(y) d y
$$

Using calculus and some basic facts from analysis one can prove the desired result. The details are not exciting and are left to the reader.

### 9.3 Inhomogeneous Heat Equation

In this section, we will consider what happens when we add a bounded continuous function $g(x)$ to the heat equation. That is, we will study
(a) $u \in C^{1,2}$ and $u_{t}=\frac{1}{2} \Delta u+g$ in $(0, \infty) \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$
(b) $u$ is continuous at each point of $\{0\} \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ and $u(0, x)=f(x)$ is bounded and continuous.
We observed in the previous section that (b) cannot hold unless $f$ is continuous. Here $g=u_{t}-(1 / 2) \Delta u$ so the equation in (a) cannot hold with $u \in C^{1,2}$ unless $g$ is continuous.

Our first step in treating the new equation is to observe that if $u_{1}$ is a solution of the equation with $f=f_{0}$ and $g=0$ which we studied in the last section, and $u_{2}$ is a solution of the equation with $f=0$ and $g=g_{0}$ then $u_{1}+u_{2}$ is a solution of the equation with $f=f_{0}$ and $g=g_{0}$ so we can restrict our attention to the case $f \equiv 0$.

Having made this simplification, we will now study the equation above by following the procedure used in the last section.

Theorem 9.3.1. If $u$ satisfies (a), then

$$
M_{s}=u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)+\int_{0}^{s} g\left(B_{r}\right) d r
$$

is a local martingale on $[0, t]$.
Proof. Applying Itô's formula as in the proof of Theorem 9.2.1 gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)-u\left(t, B_{0}\right) & =\int_{0}^{s}\left(-u_{t}+\frac{1}{2} \Delta u\right)\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) d r \\
& +\int_{0}^{s} \nabla u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \cdot d B_{r}
\end{aligned}
$$

which proves the result, since $-u_{t}+\frac{1}{2} \Delta u=-g$ and the second term on the right-hand side is a local martingale by Theorem 7.6.4.

Again the next step is a uniqueness result.
Theorem 9.3.2. If there is a solution that is bounded on $[0, T] \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ for any $T<\infty$, it must be

$$
v(t, x) \equiv E_{x}\left(\int_{0}^{t} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right)
$$

Proof. Under the assumptions on $g$ and $u, M_{s}, 0 \leq s<t$, defined in Theorem 9.3.1 is a bounded martingale and $u(0, x) \equiv 0$ so

$$
M_{t} \equiv \lim _{s \uparrow t} M_{s}=\int_{0}^{t} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

Since $M_{s}$ is uniformly integrable, $u(t, x)=E_{x} M_{0}=E_{x} M_{t}=v(t, x)$.
Again, it is easy to show that if $v$ is smooth enough it is a solution.
Theorem 9.3.3. If $v \in C^{1,2}$, then it satisfies (a) in ( $0, \infty$ ) $\times \mathbf{R}^{d}$.
Proof. If we think of running space time Brownian motion $\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)$ to construct the solution then we see

$$
v(t+h, x)=E_{x}\left(v\left(t, B_{h}\right)+\int_{0}^{h} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right)
$$

Subtracting $v(t, x)$ from each side and using Taylor series

$$
\begin{aligned}
v(t+h, x) & -v(t, x)=E_{x}\left(\sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(t, x)\left(B_{i}(h)-x_{i}\right)\right. \\
