& +\frac{1}{2} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(t, x)\left(B_{i}(h)-x_{i}\right)\left(B_{j}(h)-x_{j}\right) \\
& \left.+\int_{0}^{h} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right)+o(h)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, B_{i}(t), B_{i}^{2}(t)-t$, and $B_{i}(t) B_{j}(t)$ are martingales

$$
v(t+h, x)-v(t, x)=\frac{1}{2} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i}^{2}}(t, x) h+E_{x} \int_{0}^{h} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s+o(h)
$$

Dividing by $h$ and letting $h \rightarrow 0$ gives $v_{t}=\frac{1}{2} \Delta V+g(x)$ which is the desired result.

Theorem 9.3.4. $v$ satisfies (b).
Proof. If $|g| \leq M$, then as $t \rightarrow 0$

$$
|v(t, x)| \leq E_{x} \int_{0}^{t}\left|g\left(B_{s}\right)\right| d s \leq M t \rightarrow 0
$$

which proves the desired result.

The final step in showing that $v$ is a solution is to check that $v \in C^{1,2}$. The calculations are messy so we just state the result. Here and in what follows when we say a function is Hölder continuous, that means $|g(x)-g(y)| \leq C|x-y|^{\alpha}$ for some $\alpha>0$.
Theorem 9.3.5. If $g$ is Hölder continuous then $v \in C^{1,2}$.
The problem is that $v(t, x)=\int G_{t}(x, y) g(y) d y$ where

$$
G_{t}(x, y)=\int_{0}^{t}(2 \pi s)^{-d / 2} e^{-|y-x|^{2} / 2 s} d s
$$

Looking at the $y$ with $|y-x|^{2} \leq s$ leads to a lower bound

$$
G_{t}(x, y) \geq C \int_{|y-x|^{2}}^{t} s^{-d / 2} d s=O\left(|y-x|^{2-d}\right)
$$

From this it follows that first derivative blows up like $\delta^{1-d}$ and the second like is $\delta^{-d}$, which is not integrable near 0 . However if $g$ is Hölder continuity of $g$ its smoothness can save the day. For details, see pages 12-13 of Friedman (1964).

### 9.4 Feynman-Kac Formula

In this section, we will consider what happens when we add $c u$ to the right-hand side of the heat equation where $c(x)$ is bounded and continuous. That is, we will study
(a) $u \in C^{1,2}$ and $u_{t}=\frac{1}{2} \Delta u+c u$ in $(0, \infty) \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$.
(b) $u$ is continuous at each point of $\{0\} \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ and $u(0, x)=f(x)$, where $f$ is bounded and continuous.
If $c(x) \leq 0$, then this equation describes heat flow with cooling. That is, $u(t, x)$ gives the temperature at the point $x \in \mathbf{R}^{d}$ at time $t$, when the heat at $x$ at time $t$ dissipates at the rate $-c(x)$.

The first step, as usual, is to find a local martingale.
Theorem 9.4.1. Let $c_{s}=\int_{0}^{s} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r$. If $u$ satisfies (a), then

$$
M_{s}=u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{s}\right)
$$

is a local martingale on $[0, t]$
Proof. The first step in doing this is to generalize our third version of Itô's formula

$$
\begin{align*}
& u\left(t-s, B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{s}\right)-u\left(t, B_{0}\right)  \tag{9.4.1}\\
& \quad=\int_{0}^{s}-u_{t}\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \exp \left(c_{r}\right) d r+\int_{0}^{s} \exp \left(c_{r}\right) \nabla u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \cdot d B_{r} \\
& \quad+\int_{0}^{s} u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) c\left(B_{r}\right) \exp \left(c_{r}\right) d r+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{s} \Delta u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \exp \left(c_{r}\right) d r
\end{align*}
$$

Proof of (9.4.1). As in the three previous proofs of Itô's formula, we subdivide the interval $[0, s]$ with $s_{i}^{n}=s i / 2^{n}$. We write the difference as

$$
\begin{aligned}
& {\left[u\left(t-s_{i+1}^{n}, B\left(s_{i+1}^{n}\right)\right)-u\left(t-s_{i}^{n}, B\left(s_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\right] \exp \left(c\left(s_{i+1}^{n}\right)\right)} \\
& \quad+u\left(t-s_{i}^{n}, B\left(s_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\left[\exp \left(c\left(s_{i+1}^{n}\right)\right)-\exp \left(c\left(s_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\right.
\end{aligned}
$$

The limit of the first line gives the first, second, and fourth terms. Using

$$
\exp \left(c\left(s_{i+1}^{n}\right)\right)-\exp \left(c\left(s_{i}^{n}\right)\right)=\exp \left(c\left(s_{i}^{n}\right)\right)\left\{\exp \left(\int_{s_{i}^{n}}^{s_{i+1}^{n}} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r\right)-1\right\}
$$

we see that the limit of the second line gives the third term.
Rearranging, the right-hand side of (9.4.1) is

$$
\begin{gathered}
=\int_{0}^{s}\left(-u_{t}+c u+\frac{1}{2} \Delta u\right)\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \exp \left(c_{r}\right) d r \\
+\int_{0}^{s} \exp \left(c_{r}\right) \nabla u\left(t-r, B_{r}\right) \cdot d B_{r}
\end{gathered}
$$

which proves the desired result, since $-u_{t}+c u+\frac{1}{2} \Delta u=0$, so using Theorem 7.6.4 the second term is a local martingale.

The next step, again, is a uniqueness result.
Theorem 9.4.2. If there is a solution that is bounded on $[0, T] \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ for any $T<\infty$, it must be

$$
\begin{equation*}
v(t, x) \equiv E_{x}\left\{f\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}^{t}\right)\right\} \tag{9.4.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Under our assumptions on $c$ and $u, M_{s}, 0 \leq s<t$, is a bounded martingale and $M_{t} \equiv \lim _{s \rightarrow t} M_{s}=f\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}\right)$. Since $M_{s}$ is uniformly integrable it follows that

$$
u(t, x)=E_{x} M_{0}=E_{x} M_{t}=v(t, x)
$$

which proves the desired result.

As before, it is easy to show that if $v$ is smooth enough it is a solution.
Theorem 9.4.3. If $v \in C^{1,2}$, then it satisfies (a).
Proof. When we write the solution in (9.4.2), we think about running space-time Brownian motion $\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)$ from time 0 to $t$, so we have

$$
v(t+h, x)=E_{x}\left[v\left(t, B_{h}\right) \exp \left(c_{h}\right)\right]
$$

Subtracting $v(t, x)$ from each side

$$
v(t+h, x)-v(t, x)=E_{x}\left[v\left(t, B_{h}\right) \exp \left(c_{h}\right)-v(t, x)\right]
$$

Adding and subtracting $v\left(t, B_{h}\right)$ and the right hand side and using Taylor's theorem

$$
\begin{aligned}
& v\left(t, B_{h}\right) \exp \left(c_{h}\right)-v(t, x) \approx \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(t, x)\left(B_{h}^{i}-x\right) \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(t, x)\left(B_{h}^{i}-x\right)\left(B_{h}^{j}-x\right)+v\left(t, B_{h}\right)\left(\exp \left(c_{h}\right)-1\right)+o(h)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, W_{i}(t), W_{i}^{2}(t)=t$ and $W_{i}(t) W_{j}(t)$ are martingales.
$E_{x}\left[v\left(t, B_{h}\right) \exp \left(c_{h}\right)-v(t, x)\right] \approx \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i}^{2}} h+E_{x}\left[v\left(t, B_{h}\right)\left(\exp \left(c_{h}\right)-1\right)\right]+o(h)$
Dividing each side by $h$ and letting $h \rightarrow 0$ we get

$$
\frac{\partial v}{\partial t}=\frac{1}{2} \Delta v(t, x)+c(x) v(t, x)
$$

which proves the desired result.
Theorem 9.4.4. $v$ satisfies (b).
Proof. If $|c| \leq M$, then $e^{-M t} \leq \exp \left(c_{t}^{t}\right) \leq e^{M t}$, so $\exp \left(c_{t}^{t}\right) \rightarrow 1$ as $t \rightarrow 0$. Letting $\|f\|_{\infty}=\sup _{x}|f(x)|$ this result implies

$$
\left|E_{x} \exp \left(c_{t}^{t}\right) f\left(B_{t}\right)-E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)\right| \leq\|f\|_{\infty} E_{x}\left|\exp \left(c_{t}^{t}\right)-1\right| \rightarrow 0
$$

Theorem 9.2.4 implies that $(t, x) \rightarrow E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)$ is continuous at each point of $\{0\} \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$ and the desired result follows.

This brings us to the problem of determining when $v$ is smooth enough to be a solution.

Theorem 9.4.5. If $f$ and $c$ Hölder continuous, then $v \in C^{1,2}$ and, hence, satisfies (a).

To solve the problem in this case, we use a trick to reduce our result to the previous case. We begin by observing that

$$
c_{s}=\int_{0}^{s} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r
$$

is continuous and locally of bounded variation. So using the ordinary change of variables formula if $h \in C^{1}$ then

$$
h\left(c_{t}\right)-h\left(c_{0}\right)=\int_{0}^{t} h^{\prime}\left(c_{s}\right) c\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

Taking $h(x)=e^{-x}$ we have

$$
\exp \left(-c_{t}\right)-1=-\int_{0}^{t} \exp \left(-c_{s}\right) c\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

Multiplying by $-\exp \left(c_{t}\right)$ gives

$$
\exp \left(c_{t}\right)-1=\int_{0}^{t} c\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}-c_{s}\right) d s
$$

Plugging in the definitions of $c_{t}^{t}$ and $c_{s}^{t}$ we have

$$
\exp \left(\int_{0}^{t} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r\right)=1+\int_{0}^{t} c\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(\int_{s}^{t} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r\right) d s
$$

Multiplying by $f\left(B_{t}\right)$, taking expected values, and using Fubini's theorem, which is justified since everything is bounded, gives

$$
v(t, x)=E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)+\int_{0}^{t} E_{x}\left\{c\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(\int_{s}^{t} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r\right) f\left(B_{t}\right)\right\} d s
$$

Conditioning on $\mathcal{F}_{s}$, noticing $c\left(B_{s}\right) \in \mathcal{F}_{s}$, and using the Markov property

$$
E_{x}\left(c\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(\int_{s}^{t} c\left(B_{r}\right) d r\right) f\left(B_{t}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{s}\right)=c\left(B_{s}\right) v\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)
$$

Taking the expected value of the last equation and plugging into the previous one, we have

$$
\begin{align*}
v(t, x)= & E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right)+\int_{0}^{t} E_{x}\left\{c\left(B_{s}\right) v\left(t-s, B_{s}\right)\right\} d s \\
& \equiv v_{1}(t, x)+v_{2}(t, x) \tag{9.4.3}
\end{align*}
$$

The first term on the right, $v_{1}(t, x)$, is $C^{1,2}$ by Theorem 9.2.3. The second term, $v_{2}(t, x)$, is of the form considered in the last section with $g(r, x)=c(x) v(r, x)$. If we start with the trivial observation that if $c$ and $f$ are bounded, then $v$ is bounded on $[0, N] \times \mathbf{R}^{d}$, and apply (2.6b), we see that

$$
\left|v_{2}(t, x)-v_{2}(t, y)\right| \leq C_{N}|x-y| \quad \text { whenever } t \leq N
$$

To get a similar estimate for $v_{1}$ let $\bar{B}_{t}$ be a Brownian motion starting at 0 and observe that since $f$ is Hölder continuous

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|v_{1}(t, x)-v_{1}(t, y)\right| & =\left|E\left\{f\left(x+\bar{B}_{t}\right)-f\left(y+\bar{B}_{t}\right)\right\}\right| \\
& \leq E\left|f\left(x+\bar{B}_{t}\right)-f\left(y+\bar{B}_{t}\right)\right| \leq C|x-y|^{\alpha}
\end{aligned}
$$

Combining the last two estimates we see that $v(r, x)$ is Hölder continuous locally in $t$. Since $c$ and $v$ are bounded and we have supposed that $c, x)$ is Hölder continuous the triangle inequality implies $g(r, x)$ is Hölder continuous locally in $t$. The result then follows from smoothness results for the inhomogeneous heat equation.

### 9.5 Dirichlet problem

The developments here and in Section 9.7 follow port and Stone (1978). In this section, we will consider the Dirichlet problem. That is, given an open set $G$ we will seek a function that satisfies
(a) $u \in C^{2}$ and $\Delta u=0$ in $G$.
(b) At each point of $\partial G, u$ is continuous and $u=f$, a bounded function.

Physically $u$ is an equilibrium temperature distribution when $\partial G$ is held at a fixed temperature profile $f$.

Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin G\right\}$. We assume throughout this section that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{x}(\tau<\infty)=1 \quad \text { for all } x \in G . \tag{9.5.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $u$ satisfies (a), then $M_{t}=u\left(B_{t}\right)$ is a local martingale on [ $0, \tau$ ). That is, there is an increasing sequence of stopping times $T_{n}$ so that $u\left(B\left(t \wedge T_{n}\right)\right.$ is a martingale. To turn a local martingale into an honest martingale we introduce a function $\gamma:[0, \infty) \rightarrow[0, \tau)$ stays constant for one unit of time when its value reaches $T_{n}$. That is, $\gamma(t)=t$ for $0 \leq t \leq T_{1}$, $\gamma(t)=T_{1}$ for $T_{1} \leq t \leq T_{1}+1$, and in general

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \gamma(t)=t-k \text { for } T_{k}+k \leq t \leq T_{k+1}+k \\
& \gamma(t)=T_{k+1} \text { for } T_{k+1}+k \leq T_{k+1}+k+1
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $\gamma(t)$ is a stopping time and $\gamma(n) \leq T_{n}$.
Lemma 9.5.1. $M_{t}=u(B(\gamma(t)))$ is a martingale.
Theorem 9.5.2. If there is a solution of the Dirichlet problem that is bounded, it must be

$$
v(x) \equiv E_{x} f\left(B_{\tau}\right)
$$

Proof. Since $u$ is bounded $M_{t}$ is a bounded martingale, and hence $\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} M_{t}$ exists. The boundary condition implies $M_{\infty}=f\left(B_{\tau}\right)$. Since the martingale is uniformly integrable, $u(x)=E_{x} M_{\infty}=E_{x} f\left(B_{\tau}\right)$.
Theorem 9.5.3. If $v \in C^{2}$, then it satisfies (a).
Proof. We learned this argument, which avoids the use of stochastic calculus, from Liggett (2010). Let $x \in D$ and $B=B(x, r) \subset D$. If $\tau_{B}$ is the exit time from $B$ then the strong Markov property implies

$$
v(x)=E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)
$$

Using Taylor's theorem

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)-v(x) & =E_{x} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right] \\
& +\frac{1}{2} E_{x} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right]\left[B_{j}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{j}\right]+o\left(r^{2}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, B_{i}(t), B_{i}^{2}(t)-t$ and $B_{i}(t) B_{j}(t)$ are martingales, we have

$$
\begin{array}{ll}
E_{x}\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right]=0 & E_{x}\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right]\left[B_{j}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{j}\right]=0 \quad \text { when } i \neq j \\
& E_{x}\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right]^{2}=E_{0}\left[B_{i}^{2}\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right]=E_{x} \tau_{B}
\end{array}
$$

Using these results in the previous formula

$$
0=E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)-v(x)=\frac{1}{2} \Delta v(x) E_{x} \tau_{B}+o\left(r^{2}\right)
$$

which proves the desired result.
Theorem 9.5.4. $v \in C^{\infty}$.
Proof. Let $x \in D$ and $B=B(x, r) \subset D$. If $\tau_{B}$ is the exit time from $B$ then the strong Markov property implies

$$
v(x)=E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)=\int_{\partial D(x, r)} \bar{v}(y) \pi(d y)
$$

where $\pi$ is surface measure on the boundary $\partial D(x, r)$ normalized to be a probability measure.

The last result is the averaging property of harmonic functions. Now a simple analytical argument takes over to show $v \in C^{\infty}$. To prove this we let $\psi$ be a nonnegative infinitely differentiable function that vanishes on $\left[\delta^{2}, \infty\right)$ but is not $\equiv 0$. It is routine to show that

$$
h(x)=\int_{D(x, \delta)} \psi\left(|y-x|^{2}\right) v(y) d y \in C^{\infty}
$$

Changing to spherical coordinates and using the averaging property it is easy to see that $h(x)$ is a constant multiple of $v(x)$ so $v \in C^{\infty}$.

As the next example shows, $v$ does not always satisfy (b).
Example 9.5.5. Punctured Disc. Let $d=3$ and let $G=D-K$, where $D=\{x:|x|<1\}$, and $K=\left\{x: x_{1}=x_{2}=0, x_{3} \geq 0\right\}$. Our uniqueness result and the fact that three dimensional Brownian motion never hits a line implies that the solution must be $v(x)=E_{x} f\left(B_{\tau(D}\right)$ where $\tau(D)$ is the exit time from $D$. If we let $f$ be 0 on the boundary of $D$ and $1-x_{3}$ on $K$ then the solution $v \equiv 0$ in $G$ and boundary condition will not be satisfied

Example 9.5.6. Lebesgue's Thorn. Let $d \geq 3$ and let

$$
G=(-1,1)^{d}-\cup_{1 \leq n \leq \infty}\left\{\left[2^{-n}, 2^{-n+1}\right] \times\left[-a_{n}, a_{n}\right]^{d-1}\right\}
$$

where the $n=\infty$ term is the single point $\{0\}$. We will now show that if $a_{n} \downarrow 0$ sufficiently fast, then $P_{0}(\tau=0)=0$. From this it will follow that if we define $f$ to be 0 on the boundary of $(-1,1)^{d}$ and $1-x_{1}$ on the rest of the boundary, $E_{0} f\left(B_{\tau}\right)<1$ and the boundary condition is not satisfied.

Proof. $P_{0}\left(\left(B_{t}^{2}, B_{t}^{3}\right)=(0,0)\right.$ for some $\left.t>0\right)=0$, so with probability 1 , a Brownian motion $B_{t}$ starting at 0 will not hit

$$
I_{n}=\left\{x: x_{1} \in\left[2^{-n}, 2^{-n+1}\right], x_{2}=x_{3}=\ldots=x_{d}=0\right\}
$$

Since $B_{t}$ is transient in $d \geq 3$ it follows that for a.e. $\omega$ the distance between $\left\{B_{s}: 0 \leq s<\infty\right\}$ and $I_{n}$ is positive. From the last observation, it follows immediately that if we let $T_{n}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \in\left[2^{-n}, 2^{-n+1}\right] \times\right. \left.\left[a_{n}, a_{n}\right]^{d-1}\right\}$ and pick $a_{n}$ small enough, then $P_{0}\left(T_{n}<\infty\right) \leq 3^{-n}$. Now $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} 3^{-n}=3^{-1}(3 / 2)=1 / 2$, so if we let $\tau=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin G\right\}$ and $\sigma=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin(-1,1)^{d}\right\}$, then we have

$$
P_{0}(\tau<\sigma) \leq \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} P_{0}\left(T_{n}<\infty\right) \leq \frac{1}{2}
$$

Thus $P_{0}(\tau>0) \geq P_{0}(\tau=\sigma) \geq 1 / 2$. Since $\tau=0$ is measurable with respect to $\mathcal{F}_{0}^{+}$we have $P_{0}(\tau=0)=1$.

A point $y \in \partial G$ is said to be a regular point if $P_{y}(\tau=0)=1$.
Theorem 9.5.7. Let $G$ be any open set. Suppose $f$ is bounded and continuous and $y$ is a regular point of $\partial G$. If $x_{n} \in G$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, then $v\left(x_{n}\right) \rightarrow f(y)$.

The first step is to show
Lemma 9.5.8. If $t>0$, then $x \rightarrow P_{x}(\tau \leq t)$ is lower semicontinuous. That is, if $x_{n} \rightarrow x$, then

$$
\liminf _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{x_{n}}(\tau \leq t) \geq P_{x}(\tau \leq t)
$$

Proof. By the Markov property

$$
P_{x}\left(B_{s} \in G^{c} \text { for some } s \in(\epsilon, t]\right)=\int p_{\epsilon}(x, y) P_{y}(\tau \leq t-\epsilon) d y
$$

Since $y \rightarrow P_{y}(\tau \leq t-\epsilon)$ is bounded and measurable and

$$
p_{\epsilon}(x, y)=(2 \pi \epsilon)^{-d / 2} e^{-|x-y|^{2} / 2 \epsilon}
$$

it follows from the dominated convergence theorem that

$$
x \rightarrow P_{x}\left(B_{s} \in G^{c} \text { for some } s \in(\epsilon, t]\right)
$$

is continuous for each $\epsilon>0$. Letting $\epsilon \downarrow 0$ shows that $x \rightarrow P_{x}(\tau \leq t)$ is an increasing limit of continuous functions and, hence, lower semicontinuous.

If $y$ is regular for $G$ and $t>0$, then $P_{y}(\tau \leq t)=1$, so it follows from Lemma 9.5.8 that if $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, then

$$
\liminf _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{x_{n}}(\tau \leq t) \geq 1 \quad \text { for all } t>0
$$

With this established, it is easy to complete the proof. Since $f$ is bounded and continuous, it suffices to show that if $D(y, \delta)=\{x:|x-y|<\delta\}$, then
Lemma 9.5.9. If $y$ is regular for $G$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, then for all $\delta>0$

$$
P_{x_{n}}\left(\tau<\infty, B_{\tau} \in D(y, \delta)\right) \rightarrow 1
$$

Proof. Let $\epsilon>0$ and pick $t$ so small that

$$
P_{0}\left(\sup _{0 \leq s \leq t}\left|B_{s}\right|>\frac{\delta}{2}\right)<\epsilon
$$

Since $P_{x_{n}}(\tau \leq t) \rightarrow 1$ as $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, it follows from the choices above that

$$
\begin{gathered}
\liminf _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{x_{n}}\left(\tau<\infty, B_{\tau} \in D(y, \delta)\right) \geq \liminf _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{x_{n}}\left(\tau \leq t, \sup _{0 \leq s \leq t}\left|B_{s}-x_{n}\right| \leq \frac{\delta}{2}\right) \\
\geq \liminf _{n \rightarrow \infty} P_{x_{n}}(\tau \leq t)-P_{0}\left(\sup _{0 \leq s \leq t}\left|B_{t}\right|>\frac{\delta}{2}\right)>1-\epsilon
\end{gathered}
$$

Since $\epsilon$ was arbitrary, this proves the lemma and hence Theorem 9.5.7.

The next result shows that if $G^{c}$ is not too small near $y$, then $y$ is regular.
Theorem 9.5.10. Cone Condition. If there is a cone $V$ having vertex $y$ and an $r>0$ such that $V \cap D(y, r) \subset G^{c}$, then $y$ is a regular point.
Proof. The first thing to do is to define a cone with vertex $y$, pointing in direction $v$, with opening $a$ as follows:
$V(y, v, a)=\{x: x=y+\theta(v+z)$ where $\theta \in(0, \infty), z \perp v$, and $|z|<a\}$ Now that we have defined a cone, the rest is easy. Since the normal distribution is spherically symmetric,

$$
P_{y}\left(B_{t} \in V(y, v, a)\right)=\epsilon_{a}>0
$$

where $\epsilon_{a}$ is a constant that depends only on the opening $a$. Let $r>0$ be such that $V(y, v, a) \cap D(y, r) \subset G^{c}$. The continuity of Brownian paths implies

$$
\lim _{t \rightarrow 0} P_{y}\left(\sup _{s \leq t}\left|B_{s}-y\right|>r\right)=0
$$

Combining the last two results with a trivial inequality we have

$$
\epsilon_{a} \leq \liminf _{t \downarrow 0} P_{y}\left(B_{t} \in G^{c}\right) \leq \lim _{t \downarrow 0} P_{y}(\tau \leq t) \leq P_{y}(\tau=0)
$$

and it follows from Blumenthal's zero-one law that $P_{y}(\tau=0)=1$.

### 9.5.1 Exit distributions

Half-space. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin H\right\}$ where $H=\left\{(x, y): x \in \mathbf{R}^{d-1}, y>\right. 0\}$. For $\theta \in \mathbf{R}^{d-1}$ let

$$
\begin{equation*}
h_{\theta}(x, y)=\frac{C_{d} y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{d / 2}} \tag{9.5.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $C_{d}$ is chosen so that $\int d \theta h_{\theta}(0,1)=1$.
Lemma 9.5.11. $\Delta h_{\theta}=0$ in $H$
Proof. (a) Ignoring $C_{d}$ and differentiating gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
D_{x_{i}} h_{\theta} & =-\frac{d}{2} \cdot \frac{2\left(x_{i}-\theta_{i}\right) y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+2) / 2}} \\
D_{x_{i} x_{i}} h_{\theta} & =-d \frac{y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+2) / 2}}+\frac{d(d+2)\left(x_{i}-\theta\right)^{2} y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+4) / 2}} \\
D_{y} h_{\theta} & =\frac{1}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{d / 2}}-d \cdot \frac{y^{2}}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+2) / 2}} \\
D_{y y} h_{\theta} & =-d \cdot \frac{y+2 y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+2) / 2}}+\frac{d(d+2) y^{3}}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+4) / 2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

Adding up we see that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{i=1}^{d-1} D_{x_{i} x_{i}} h_{\theta}+D_{y y} h_{\theta} & =\frac{\{(-d)(d-1)+(-d) \cdot 3\} y}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+2) / 2}} \\
& +\frac{d(d+2) y\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)}{\left(|x-\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{(d+4) / 2}}=0
\end{aligned}
$$

which completes the proof.
Theorem 9.5.12. Let $f$ is bounded and continuous and

$$
u(x, y)=\int d \theta h_{\theta}(x, y) f(\theta, 0)
$$

$u$ satisfies the Dirichlet problem in $H$ with boundary value $f$. From this it follows that $h_{\theta}(x, y)$ gives the probability density $P_{x, y}\left(B\left(\tau_{H}\right)=(\theta, 0)\right)$.

Proof. Interchanging the derivative and integral we see that $\Delta u=0$. The next step is to show that it satisfies the boundary condition.

Clearly $\int d \theta h_{\theta}(x, y)$ is independent of $x$. To show that it is independent of $y$, let $x=0$ and change variables $\theta_{i}=y \phi_{i}$ for $1 \leq i \leq d-1$ to get

$$
\int d \theta h_{\theta}(0, y)=\int \frac{C_{d} y}{\left(y^{2}|\phi|^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{d / 2}} \cdot y^{d-1} d \phi=\int d \phi h_{\phi}(0,1)=1
$$

Changing variables $\theta_{i}=x_{i}+r_{i} y$ and using dominated convergence

$$
\int_{D(x, \epsilon)^{c}} d \theta h_{\theta}(x, y)=\int_{D(0, \epsilon / y)^{c}} d r h_{r}(0,1) \rightarrow 0
$$

Since $P_{x, y}(\tau<\infty)=1$ for all $x \in H$, the last conclusion follows from Theorem 9.5.2.

When $d=2$

$$
h_{\theta}(0, y)=\frac{y}{\pi\left(|\theta|^{2}+y^{2}\right)}
$$

is Cauchy density. To see why this should be true start the Brownian motion at ( 0,0 ) and let $T_{y}=\inf \left\{t: B_{t}^{2}=y\right\}$. The strong Markov property implies that $B^{1}\left(T_{y}\right)$ has independent increments. Brownian scaling implies $B^{1}\left(T_{y}\right)={ }_{d} y B^{1}\left(T_{1}\right)$. Since $B^{1}\left(T_{y}\right)={ }_{d}-B^{1}\left(T_{y}\right)$ the distribution must be the symmetric stable law with $\alpha=1$.
Exit distributions for the ball. Let $D=\{x:|x|<1\}$, let $\tau_{D}= \inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin D \|\right.$, and define the Poisson kernel by

$$
\begin{equation*}
k_{y}(x)=\frac{1-|x|^{2}}{|x-y|^{d}} \tag{9.5.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Theorem 9.5.13. If $f$ is bounded and continuous, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
E_{x} f\left(B_{\tau}\right)=u(x)=\int_{\partial D} k_{y}(x) f(y) \pi(d y) \tag{9.5.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $\pi$ is surface measure on $\partial D$ normalized to be a probability measure.
The proof technique is the same as for the result in the half-space. We show that $u$ satisfies the Dirichlet problem with boundary condition $f$. We leave it to the reader to show that for fixed $y, \Delta k_{y}(x)=0$ and then complete the proof as above.
Exercises
9.5.1. Suppose $h$ is bounded and harmonic on $\mathbf{R}^{d}$. Prove that $h$ is constant.
9.5.2. Suppose $f \in C^{2}$ is superharmonic, takes values in $[0, \infty]$ and is continuous. Show that in dimensions 1 and $2, f$ must be constant. Give an example of the form $f(x)=g(|x|)$ in $d \geq 3$.
9.5.3. Let $B_{t}$ be a $d$-dimensional Brownian motion and let $\tau=\inf \{t: \left.B_{t} \notin G\right\}$. Suppose $G$ is a connected open set $G$, all boundary points are regular and $P_{x}(\tau<\infty)<1$ in $G$. Show that all bounded solutions of the Dirichlet problem with boundary value 0 on $\partial G$ have the form $u(x)=c P_{x}(\tau=\infty)$. Hint: Define $\bar{u}=u+\|u\|_{\infty} P_{x}(\tau=\infty)$ in $G, \bar{u}=0$ on $G^{c}$, and show that $u\left(B_{y}\right)$ is a submartingale.
9.5.4. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin G\right\}$ and consider now the Dirichlet problem in a connected open set $G$ in $d \geq 3$ where all boundary points are regular and which has $P_{x}(\tau<\infty)<1$ in $G$. Suppose that the boundary condition $f \equiv 0$. Generalize the previous result to conclude that all bounded solutions have the form $u(x)=c P_{x}(\tau=\infty)$.
9.5.5. Define a flat cone $\bar{V}(y, v, a)$ to be the intersection of $V(y, v, a)$ with a $d-1$ dimensional hyperplane that contains the line $\{y+\theta v: \theta \in \mathbf{R}\}$. Show that Theorem 9.5.10 remains true if "cone" is replaced by "flat cone."

### 9.6 Green's Functions and Potential Kernels

Let $D=B(0, r)=\{y:|y|<r\}$ the ball of radius $r$ centered at $0 . B_{t}$ will return to $D$ i.o. in $d \leq 2$ but not in $d \geq 3$. In this section we will investigate the occupation time $\int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t$. The first step is to show that for any $x$

$$
\begin{align*}
& P_{x}\left(\int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t=\infty\right)=1 \quad \text { in } d \leq 2  \tag{9.6.1}\\
& E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t<\infty \quad \text { in } d \geq 3 \tag{9.6.2}
\end{align*}
$$

Proof. Let $T_{0}=0$ and $G=B(0,2 r)$. For $k \geq 1$, let

$$
\begin{aligned}
& S_{k}=\inf \left\{t>T_{k-1}: B_{t} \in D\right\} \\
& T_{k}=\inf \left\{t>S_{k}: B_{t} \in G\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

Writing $\tau$ for $T_{1}$ and using the strong Markov property, we get for $k \geq 1$

$$
P_{x}\left(\int_{S_{k}}^{T_{k}} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t \geq s \mid \mathcal{F}_{S_{k}}\right)=P_{B\left(S_{k}\right)}\left(\int_{0}^{\tau} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t \geq s\right)=H(s)
$$

From the strong Markov property it follows that

$$
\int_{S_{k}}^{T_{k}} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t \quad \text { are i.i.d. }
$$

Since these random variables have positive mean it follows from the strong law of large numbers that

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t \geq \lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \sum_{k=1}^{n} \int_{S_{k}}^{T_{k}} 1_{D}\left(B_{t}\right) d t=\infty \quad \text { a.s. }
$$

proving (9.6.1).

If $f$ is a nonnegative function, then Fubini's theorem implies

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t & =\int_{0}^{\infty} E_{x} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t=\int_{0}^{\infty} \int p_{t}(x, y) f(y) d y d t \\
& =\iint_{0}^{\infty} p_{t}(x, y) d t f(y) d y
\end{aligned}
$$

where $p_{t}(x, y)=(2 \pi t)^{-d / 2} e^{-|x-y|^{2} / 2 t}$ is the transition density for Brownian motion. As $t \rightarrow \infty, p_{t}(x, y) \sim(2 \pi t)^{-d / 2}$, so if $d \leq 2$ then $\int p_{t}(x, y) d t= \infty$. When $d \geq 3$, changing variables $t=|x-y|^{2} / 2 s$ gives

$$
\begin{align*}
\int_{0}^{\infty} p_{t}(x, y) d t & =\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{1}{(2 \pi t)^{d / 2}} e^{-|y-x|^{2} / 2 t} d t \\
& =\int_{\infty}^{0}\left(\frac{s}{\pi|x-y|^{2}}\right)^{d / 2} e^{-s}\left(-\frac{|x-y|^{2}}{2 s^{2}}\right) d s \\
& =\frac{|x-y|^{2-d}}{2 \pi^{d / 2}} \int_{0}^{\infty} s^{(d / 2)-2} e^{-s} d s \\
& =\frac{\Gamma\left(\frac{d}{2}-1\right)}{2 \pi^{d / 2}}|x-y|^{2-d} \tag{9.6.3}
\end{align*}
$$

where $\Gamma(\alpha)=\int_{0}^{\infty} s^{\alpha-1} e^{-s} d s$ is the usual gamma function. If we define

$$
G(x, y)=\int_{0}^{\infty} p_{t}(x, y) d t
$$

then in $d \geq 3, G(x, y)<\infty$ for $x \neq y$, and

$$
\begin{equation*}
E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t=\int G(x, y) f(y) d y \tag{2.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

To complete the proof now we observe that taking $f=1_{D}$ with $D= B(0, r)$ and changing to polar coordinates

$$
\int_{D} G(0, y) d y=\int_{0}^{r} s^{d-1} C_{d} s^{2-d} d s=\frac{C_{d}}{2} r^{2}<\infty
$$

To extend the last conclusion to $x \neq 0$ observe that applying the strong Markov property at the exit time $\tau$ from $B(0,|x|)$ for a Brownian motion starting at 0 and using the rotational symmetry we have

$$
E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{s}\right) d s=E_{0} \int_{\tau}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{s}\right) d s \leq E_{0} \int_{0}^{\infty} 1_{D}\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

which completes the proof.
We call $G(x, y)$ the potential kernel, because $G(\cdot, y)$ is the electrostatic potential of a unit charge at $y$. See Chapter 3 of Port and Stone
(1978) for more on this. In $d \leq 2, \int_{0}^{\infty} p_{t}(x, y) d t \equiv \infty$ so we have to take another approach to define a useful $G$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
G(x, y)=\int_{0}^{\infty}\left(p_{t}(x, y)-a_{t}\right) d t \tag{9.6.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

where the $a_{t}$ are constants we will choose to make the integral converge (at least when $x \neq y$ ). To see why this modified definition might be useful note that if $\int f(y) d y=0$ then

$$
\int G(x, y) f(y) d y=\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} \int_{0}^{t} E_{x} f\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

When $d=1$ we let $a_{t}=p_{t}(0,0)$. With this choice,

$$
G(x, y)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{0}^{\infty}\left(e^{-(y-x)^{2} / 2 t}-1\right) t^{-1 / 2} d t
$$

and the integral converges, since the integrand is $\leq 0$ and $\sim-(y- x)^{2} / 2 t^{3 / 2}$ as $t \rightarrow \infty$. Changing variables $t=(y-x)^{2} / 2 u$ gives

$$
\begin{align*}
G(x, y) & =\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \int_{\infty}^{0}\left(e^{-u}-1\right)\left(\frac{2 u}{(y-x)^{2}}\right)^{1 / 2} \frac{-(y-x)^{2}}{2 u^{2}} d u \\
& =-\frac{|y-x|}{2 \sqrt{\pi}} \int_{0}^{\infty}\left(\int_{0}^{u} e^{-s} d s\right) u^{-3 / 2} d u \\
& =-\frac{|y-x|}{\sqrt{\pi}} \int_{0}^{\infty} d s e^{-s} \int_{s}^{\infty} \frac{u^{-3 / 2}}{2} d u \\
& =-\frac{|y-x|}{\sqrt{\pi}} \int_{0}^{\infty} d s e^{-s} s^{-1 / 2}=-|y-x| \tag{9.6.5}
\end{align*}
$$

since

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} d s e^{-s} s^{-1 / 2}=\int_{0}^{\infty} d r e^{-r^{2} / 2} \sqrt{2}=\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{2} \cdot \sqrt{2 \pi}=\sqrt{\pi}
$$

The computation is almost the same for $d=2$. The only thing that changes is the choice of $a_{t}$. If we try $a_{t}=p_{t}(0,0)$ again, then for $x \neq y$ the integrand $\sim-t^{-1}$ as $t \rightarrow 0$ and the integral diverges, so we let
$a_{t}=p_{t}\left(0, e_{1}\right)$ where $e_{1}=(1,0)$. With this choice of $a_{t}$, we get

$$
\begin{align*}
G(x, y) & =\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{0}^{\infty}\left(e^{-|x-y|^{2} / 2 t}-e^{-1 / 2 t}\right) t^{-1} d t \\
& =\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{0}^{\infty}\left(\int_{|x-y|^{2} / 2 t}^{1 / 2 t} e^{-s} d s\right) t^{-1} d t \\
& =\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{0}^{\infty} d s e^{-s} \int_{|x-y|^{2} / 2 s}^{1 / 2 s} t^{-1} d t \\
& =\frac{1}{2 \pi}\left(\int_{0}^{\infty} d s e^{-s}\right)\left(-\log \left(|x-y|^{2}\right)\right) \\
& =\frac{-1}{\pi} \log (|x-y|) \tag{9.6.6}
\end{align*}
$$

To sum up, the potential kernels are given by

$$
G(x, y)= \begin{cases}\left(\Gamma(d / 2-1) / 2 \pi^{d / 2}\right) \cdot|x-y|^{2-d} & d \geq 3  \tag{9.6.7}\\ (-1 / \pi) \cdot \log (|x-y|) & d=2 \\ -1 \cdot|x-y| & d=1\end{cases}
$$

The reader should note that in each case $G(x, y)=C \varphi(|x-y|)$ where $\varphi$ is the harmonic function we used in Section 9.1. This is, of course, no accident. $x \rightarrow G(x, 0)$ is obviously spherically symmetric and, as we will see, satisfies $\Delta G(x, 0)=0$ for $x \neq 0$, so the results above imply $G(x, 0)=A+B \varphi(|x|)$.

The formulas above correspond to $A=0$, which is nice and simple. But what about the weird looking $B$ 's? What is special about them? The answer is simple: They are chosen to make $\frac{1}{2} \Delta G(x, 0)=-\delta_{0}$ (a point mass at 0 ) in the distributional sense. It is easy to see that this happens in $d=1$. In that case $\phi(x)=|x|$ then

$$
\varphi^{\prime}(x)= \begin{cases}1 & x>0 \\ -1 & x<0\end{cases}
$$

so if $B=-1$ then $B \varphi^{\prime \prime}(x)=-2 \delta_{0}$. More sophisticated readers can check that this is also true in $d \geq 2$. (See F. John (1982), pages 96-97.)

### 9.7 Poisson's Equation

In this section, we study the following equation in a bounded open set $G$ :
(a) $u \in C^{2}$ and $\frac{1}{2} \Delta u=-g$ in $G$, where $g$ is bounded and continuous.
(b) At each point of $\partial G, u$ is continuous and $u=0$.

We can add a solution of the Dirichlet problem to replace $u=0$ in (b) by $u=f$. It follows from Theorem 9.1.1 that
Theorem 9.7.1. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin G\right\}$. If $u$ satisfies (a), then

$$
M_{t}=u\left(B_{t}\right)+\int_{0}^{t} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s
$$

is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$.
The next step is to prove a uniqueness result.
Theorem 9.7.2. If there is a solution of (a) that is bounded, it must be

$$
v(x) \equiv E_{x}\left(\int_{0}^{\tau} g\left(B_{t}\right) d t\right)
$$

Note that if $g \equiv 1$ the solution is $v(x)=E_{x} \tau$.
Proof. If $u$ satisfies (a) then $M_{t}$ defined is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$. If $G$ is bounded, then $E_{x} \tau<\infty$ for all $x \in G$. If $u$ and $g$ are bounded then for $t<\tau$

$$
\left|M_{t}\right| \leq\|u\|_{\infty}+\tau\|g\|_{\infty}
$$

Since $M_{t}$ is dominated by an integrable random variable it follows that

$$
M_{\tau} \equiv \lim _{t \uparrow \tau} M_{t}=\int_{0}^{\tau} g\left(B_{t}\right) d t
$$

and $u(x)=E_{x}\left(M_{\tau}\right)$.
As in our treatment of the Dirichlet problem:
Theorem 9.7.3. If $v \in C^{2}$, then it satisfies (a).
Proof. Let $x \in D$ and $B(x, r) \subset D$. The strong Markov property implies

$$
v(x)=E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau_{B}} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s+E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)
$$

Using Taylor's theorem

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)-v(x) & =E_{x} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{B}\right)-x_{i}\right] \\
& +\frac{1}{2} E_{x} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{D}\right)-x_{i}\right]\left[B_{j}\left(\tau_{D}\right)-x_{j}\right]+o\left(r^{2}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, B_{i}(t), B_{i}^{2}(t)-t$ and $B_{i}(t) B_{j}(t)$ are martingales, we have

$$
E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{B}\right)\right)-v(x)=\frac{1}{2} \Delta v(x) E_{x} \tau_{B}+o\left(r^{2}\right)
$$

On the other hand $E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau_{B}} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s=[g(x)+o(1)] E_{x} \tau_{B}$ so letting $r \rightarrow 0$ we have $(1 / 2) \Delta v=-g$

After the extensive discussion in the last section on the Dirichlet problem, the conditions needed to guarantee that the boundary conditions hold should come as no surprise.

Theorem 9.7.4. Suppose that $G$ and $g$ are bounded. Let $y$ be a regular point of $\partial G$. If $x_{n} \in G$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, then $v\left(x_{n}\right) \rightarrow 0$.
Proof. We begin by observing:
(i) It follows from Lemma 9.5.9 that if $\epsilon>0$, then $P_{x_{n}}(\tau>\epsilon) \rightarrow 0$.
(ii) If $G$ is bounded, then $C=\sup _{x} E_{x} \tau<\infty$ and, hence, $\|v\|_{\infty} \leq C\|g\|_{\infty}<\infty$.

Let $\epsilon>0$. Beginning with some elementary inequalities then using the Markov property we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|v\left(x_{n}\right)\right| & \leq E_{x_{n}}\left(\int_{0}^{\tau \wedge \epsilon}\left|g\left(B_{s}\right)\right| d s\right)+E_{x_{n}}\left(\left|\int_{\epsilon}^{\tau} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right| ; \tau>\epsilon\right) \\
& \leq \epsilon\|g\|_{\infty}+E_{x_{n}}\left(\left|v\left(B_{\epsilon}\right)\right| ; \tau>\epsilon\right) \leq \epsilon\|g\|_{\infty}+\|v\|_{\infty} P_{x_{n}}(\tau>\epsilon)
\end{aligned}
$$

Letting $n \rightarrow \infty$, and using (i) and (ii) proves the desired result since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary.

Last, but not least, we come to the question of proving $v \in C^{2}$. For these developments we will assume that $g$ is defined on $\mathbf{R}^{d}$ not just in $G$ and has compact support. Recall we are supposing $G$ is bounded and notice that the values of $g$ on $G^{c}$ are irrelevant, so there will be no loss of generality if we later want to suppose that $\int g(x) d x=0$. We begin with the case $d \geq 3$, because in this case implies

$$
\bar{w}(x)=E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty}\left|g\left(B_{t}\right)\right| d t<\infty
$$

and, moreover, is a bounded function of $x$. This means we can define

$$
w(x)=E_{x} \int_{0}^{\infty} g\left(B_{t}\right) d t
$$

use the strong Markov property to conclude

$$
w(x)=E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau} g\left(B_{t}\right) d t+E_{x} w\left(B_{\tau}\right)
$$

and change notation to get

$$
\begin{equation*}
v(x)=w(x)-E_{x} w\left(B_{\tau}\right) \tag{9.7.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

Theorem 9.5.4 tells us that the second term is $C^{\infty}$ in $G$, so to verify that $v \in C^{2}$ we need only prove that $w$ is, a task in $d \geq 3$ that is made simpler by the fact that gives the following explicit formula

$$
w(x)=C_{d} \int|x-y|^{2-d} g(y) d y
$$

The details are tedious, so we will content ourselves to state the result.
Theorem 9.7.5. In $d \geq 2$ if $g$ is Hölder continuous, then $w$ is $C^{2}$. In $d=1$ it is sufficient to suppose $g$ is continuous

The last result settles the question of smoothness in $d \geq 3$. To extend the result to $d \leq 2$, we use the potential kernels defined in (9.6.4). Using this interpretation of $w$, it is easy to see that (9.7.1) holds, so again our problem is reduced to proving that $w$ is $C^{2}$, which is a problem in calculus. Once all the computations are done, we find that Theorem 9.7.5 holds in $d=2$ and that in $d=1$, it is sufficient to assume that $g$ is continuous. The reader can find details in The reader can find a proof either in Port and Stone (1978), pages 116-117, or in Gilbarg and Trudinger (1977), pages 53-55.

### 9.7.1 Occupation times

Half space. To explain why we want to define the potential kernels, we return to Brownian motion in the half space, $H=\left\{y: y_{d}>0\right\}$. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin H\right\}$, and let

$$
\bar{y}=\left(y_{1}, \ldots, y_{d-1},-y_{d}\right)
$$

be the reflection of $y$ through the plane $\left\{y \in \mathbf{R}^{d}: y_{d}=0\right\}$
Theorem 9.7.6. If $x \in H, f \geq 0$ has compact support, and $\{x: f(x)> 0\} \subset H$ then

$$
E_{x}\left(\int_{0}^{\tau} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t\right)=\int G(x, y) f(y) d y-\int G(x, \bar{y}) f(y) d y
$$

Proof. Using Fubini's theorem which is justified since $f \geq 0$, then using the reflection principle and the fact that $\{x: f(x)>0\} \subset H$ we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t & =\int_{0}^{\infty} E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{t}\right) ; \tau>t\right) d t \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{H}\left(p_{t}(x, y)-p_{t}(x, \bar{y})\right) f(y) d y d t \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{H}\left(p_{t}(x, y)-a_{t}\right) f(y) d y d t \\
& -\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{H}\left(p_{t}(x, \bar{y})-a_{t}\right) f(y) d y d t \\
& =\int G(x, y) f(y) d y-\int G(x, \bar{y}) f(y) d y
\end{aligned}
$$

The compact support of $f$ and the formulas for $G$ imply that $\int|G(x, y) f(y)| d y$ and $\int|G(x, \bar{y}) f(y)| d y$ are finite, so the last two equalities are valid.

The proof given above simplifies considerably in the case $d \geq 3$; however, part of the point of the proof above is that, with the definition we have chosen for $G$ in the recurrent case, the formulas and proofs can be the same for all $d$.

Let $G_{H}(x, y)=G(x, y)-G(x, \bar{y})$. We think of $G_{H}(x, y)$ as the "expected occupation time (density) at $y$ for a Brownian motion starting at $x$ and killed when it leaves $H$." The rationale for this interpretation is that (for suitable $f$ )

$$
E_{x}\left(\int_{0}^{\tau} f\left(B_{t}\right) d t\right)=\int G_{H}(x, y) f(y) d y
$$

The answer is very simple in $d=1$.

$$
\begin{equation*}
G_{H}(x, y)=2(x \wedge y) \quad \text { for all } x, y>0 \tag{9.7.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

It is somewhat surprising that $y \rightarrow G_{H}(x, y)$ is constant $=2 x$ for $y \geq x$, that is, all points $y>x$ have the same expected occupation time!

Proof. $G(x, y)=-|x-y|$, so $G_{H}(x, y)=-|x-y|+|x+y|$. Separating things into cases, we see that

$$
G_{H}(x, y)= \begin{cases}-(x-y)+(x+y)=2 y & \text { when } 0<y<x \\ -(y-x)+(x+y)=2 x & \text { when } x<y\end{cases}
$$

which gives the desired formula.
Ball. Let $G(x, y)$ be the Green's functions defined in (9.6.7)
Theorem 9.7.7. If $g$ is bounded and measurable then

$$
E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau} g\left(B_{t}\right) d t=\int G_{D}(x, y) g(y) d y
$$

where

$$
\begin{equation*}
G_{D}(x, y)=G(x, y)-\int \frac{1-|x|^{2}}{|x-z|^{d}} G(z, y) \pi(d z) \tag{9.7.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

This follows from (9.7.1) and (9.5.4). This formula can be simplified considerably. Turning to page 123 in Folland (1976) we find

Theorem 9.7.8. In $d \geq 3$, if $0<|y|<1$ then

$$
G_{D}(x, y)=G(x, y)-|y|^{2-d} G\left(x, y /|y|^{2}\right)
$$

Proof. By (9.7.1) it is enough to show that (i) the second term is harmonic in $D$ and (ii) $=G(x, y)$ on the boundary. $y \rightarrow y /|y|^{2}$ maps points $y \in D$
to $D^{c}$, so (i) holds. To prove (ii), we note

$$
\begin{aligned}
G(x, y)-|y|^{2-d} G\left(x, y /|y|^{2}\right) & =\frac{C_{d}}{|x-y|^{d-2}}-\frac{C_{d}}{|y|^{d-2}}\left|x-\frac{y}{|y|^{2}}\right|^{-(d-2)} \\
& =\frac{C_{d}}{|x-y|^{d-2}}-\frac{C_{d}}{\left.\left.|x| y|-y| y\right|^{-1}\right|^{d-2}}=0
\end{aligned}
$$

The last equality follows from a fact useful for the next proof:
Lemma 9.7.9. If $|x|=1$ then $\left.|x| y|-y| y\right|^{-1}|=|x-y|$.
Using $|z|^{2}=z \cdot z$ and then $|x|^{2}=1$ we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left.\left.|x| y|-y| y\right|^{-1}\right|^{2} & =|x|^{2}|y|^{2}-2 x \cdot y+1 \\
& =|y|^{2}-2 x \cdot y+|x|^{2}=|x-y|^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

which completes the proof.

Again turning to page 123 of Folland (1976) we "guess"
Theorem 9.7.10. In $d=2$ if $0<|y|<1$ then

$$
G_{D}(x, y)=\frac{-1}{\pi}\left(\ln |x-y|-\left.\ln |x| y|-y| y\right|^{-1} \mid\right)
$$

We leave the proof to the reader.
In $d=1$, where $D=(-1,1),(9.7 .3)$ tells us that

$$
\begin{aligned}
G_{D}(x, y) & =G(x, y)-\frac{x+1}{2} G(1, y)-\frac{1-x}{2} G(-1, y) \\
& =-|x-y|+\frac{x+1}{2}(1-y)+\frac{1-x}{2}(y+1) \\
& =-|x-y|+1-x y
\end{aligned}
$$

Considering the two cases $x \geq y$ and $x \leq y$ leads to

$$
G_{D}(x, y)= \begin{cases}(1-x)(1+y) & -1 \leq y \leq x \leq 1  \tag{9.7.4}\\ (1-y)(1+x) & -1 \leq x \leq y \leq 1\end{cases}
$$

Geometrically, if we fix $y$ then $x \rightarrow G_{D}(x, y)$ is determined by the conditions that it is linear on $[0, y]$ and on $[y, 1]$ with $G_{D}(0, y)=G_{D}(1, y)=0$, and $(1 / 2) G_{D}^{\prime \prime}(y, y)=-\delta_{y}$, a point mass at $y$. That is, $G_{D}^{\prime}$ is discontinuous at $y$ and $G_{D}^{\prime}(y-, y)-G_{D}^{\prime}(y+, y)=2$.

Exercises
Exercise 9.7.1. Let $G$ be a bounded open set and let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin\right. G\}$. What equation should we solve to find $w(x)=E_{x} \tau^{2}$ ?

### 9.8 Schrödinger Equation

The developments here follow Chung and Zhao (1995). In this section, we will consider what happens when we add $c u$ were $c$ is a bounded continuous function to the left-hand side of the Dirichlet problem. That is, given a bounded continuous function $f$ on $\partial G$, we will study
(a) $u \in C^{2}$ and $\frac{1}{2} \Delta u+c u=0$ in $G$.
(b) At each point of $\partial G, u$ is continuous and $u=f$.

As always, the first step in solving the PDE is to find a local martingale.
Theorem 9.8.1. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin G\right\}$. If $u$ satisfies (a) then

$$
M_{t}=u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(\int_{0}^{t} c\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right)
$$

is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$.
Proof. Let $c_{t}=\int_{0}^{t} c\left(B_{s}\right) d s$. Applying the version of Itô's from (9.4.1) gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}\right)-u\left(B_{0}\right) & =\int_{0}^{t} \exp \left(c_{s}\right) \nabla u\left(B_{s}\right) \cdot d B_{s}+\int_{0}^{t} u\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{s}\right) c\left(B_{s}\right) d s \\
& +\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{t} \Delta u\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{s}\right) d s
\end{aligned}
$$

for $t<\tau$. This proves result, since $\frac{1}{2} \Delta u+c u=0$, and using theorem 7.6.4 the first term on the right-hand side is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$.

At this point, the reader might expect that the next step, as it has been five times before, is to assume that everything is bounded and conclude that if there is a solution that is bounded, it must be

$$
v(x) \equiv E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{\tau}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)\right)
$$

We will not do this, however, because the following simple example shows that this result is false.

Example 9.8.2. Let $d=1, G=(-a, a), c \equiv \gamma$, and $f \equiv 1$. The equation we are considering is

$$
\frac{1}{2} u^{\prime \prime}+\gamma u=0 \quad u(a)=u(-a)=1
$$

The general solution is $A \cos b x+B \sin b x$, where $b=\sqrt{2 \gamma}$. So if we want the boundary condition to be satisfied we must have

$$
\begin{aligned}
& 1=A \cos b a+B \sin b a \\
& 1=A \cos (-b a)+B \sin (-b a)=A \cos b a-B \sin b a
\end{aligned}
$$

Adding the two equations and then subtracting them it follows that

$$
2=2 A \cos b a \quad 0=2 B \sin b a
$$

From this we see that $B=0$ always works and we may or may not be able to solve for $A$.

If $\cos b a=0$ then there is no solution. If $\cos b a \neq 0$ then $u(x)= \cos b x / \cos b a$ is a solution. We will see later that if $a b<\pi / 2$ then

$$
v(x)=\cos b x / \cos b a
$$

However this cannot possibly hold for $a b>\pi / 2$ since $v(x) \geq 0$ while the right- hand side is $<0$ for some values of $x$.

We will see below that the trouble with the last example is that if $a b>\pi / 2$ then $c \equiv \gamma$ is too large, or to be precise, if we let

$$
w(x)=E_{x} \exp \left(\int_{0}^{\tau} c\left(B_{s}\right) d s\right)
$$

then $w \equiv \infty$ in $(-a, a)$. The rest of this section is devoted to showing that if $w \not \equiv \infty$, then "everything is fine." The development will require several stages. The first step is to show
Lemma 9.8.3. Let $\theta>0$. There is a $\mu>0$ so that if $H$ is an open set with Lebesgue measure $|H| \leq \mu$ and $\tau_{H}=\inf \left\{t>0: B_{t} \notin H\right\}$ then

$$
\sup _{x} E_{x}\left(\exp \left(\theta \tau_{H}\right)\right) \leq 2
$$

Proof. Pick $\gamma>0$ so that $e^{\theta \gamma} \leq 4 / 3$. Clearly,

$$
P_{x}\left(\tau_{H}>\gamma\right) \leq \int_{H} \frac{1}{(2 \pi \gamma)^{d / 2}} e^{-|x-y|^{2} / 2 \gamma} d y \leq \frac{|H|}{(2 \pi \gamma)^{d / 2}} \leq \frac{1}{4}
$$

if we pick $\mu$ small enough. Using the Markov property we can conclude that

$$
\begin{aligned}
P_{x}\left(\tau_{H}>k \gamma\right) & =E_{x}\left(P_{B_{\gamma}}\left(\tau_{H}>(k-1) \gamma\right) ; \tau_{H}>\gamma\right) \\
& \leq \frac{1}{4} \sup _{y} P_{y}\left(\tau_{H}>(k-1) \gamma\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

So it follows by induction that for all integers $k \geq 0$ we have

$$
\sup _{x} P_{x}\left(\tau_{H}>k \gamma\right) \leq \frac{1}{4^{k}}
$$

Since $e^{x}$ is increasing, and $e^{\theta \gamma} \leq 4 / 3$ by assumption, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} \exp \left(\theta \tau_{H}\right) & \leq \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \exp (\theta \gamma k) P_{x}\left((k-1) \gamma<\tau_{H} \leq k \gamma\right) \\
& \leq \sum_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(\frac{4}{3}\right)^{k} \frac{1}{4^{k-1}}=\frac{4}{3} \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{3^{k-1}}=\frac{4}{3} \cdot \frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{3}}=2
\end{aligned}
$$

Careful readers may have noticed that we left $\tau_{H}=0$ out of the expected value. However, by the Blumenthal $0-1$ law either $P_{x}\left(\tau_{H}=0\right)=0$ in which case our computation is valid, or $P_{x}\left(\tau_{H}=0\right)=1$ in which case $E_{x} \exp \left(\theta \tau_{H}\right)=1$.

Let $c^{*}=\sup _{x}|c(x)|$. By Lemma 9.8.3, we can pick $r_{0}$ so small that if $T_{r}=\inf \left\{t:\left|B_{t}-B_{0}\right|>r\right\}$ and $r \leq r_{0}$, then $E_{x} \exp \left(c^{*} T_{r}\right) \leq 2$ for all $x$.

Lemma 9.8.4. Let $2 \delta \leq r_{0}$. If $D(x, 2 \delta) \subset G$ and $y \in D(x, \delta)$, then

$$
w(y) \leq 2^{d+2} w(x)
$$

The reason for our interest in this is that it shows $w(x)<\infty$ implies $w(y)<\infty$ for $y \in D(x, \delta)$.

Proof. If $D(y, r) \subset G$, and $r \leq r_{0}$ then the strong Markov property implies

$$
\begin{align*}
w(y) & =E_{y}\left[\exp \left(c_{T_{r}}\right) w\left(B\left(T_{r}\right)\right)\right] \leq E_{y}\left[\exp \left(c^{*} T_{r}\right) w\left(B\left(T_{r}\right)\right)\right] \\
& =E_{y}\left[\exp \left(c^{*} T_{r}\right)\right] \int_{\partial D(y, r)} w(z) \pi(d z) \leq 2 \int_{\partial D(y, r)} w(z) \pi(d z) \tag{9.8.1}
\end{align*}
$$

where $\pi$ is surface measure on $\partial D(y, r)$ normalized to be a probability measure, since the exit time, $T_{r}$, and exit location, $B\left(T_{r}\right)$, are independent.

If $\delta \leq r_{0}$ and $D(y, \delta) \subset G$, multiplying the last inequality by $r^{d-1}$ and integrating from 0 to $\delta$ gives

$$
\frac{\delta^{d}}{d} w(y) \leq 2 \cdot \frac{1}{\sigma_{d}} \int_{D(y, \delta)} w(z) d z
$$

where $\sigma_{d}$ is the surface area of $\{x:|x|=1\}$. Rearranging we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{D(y, \delta)} w(z) d z \geq 2^{-1} \frac{\delta^{d}}{C_{o}} w(y) \tag{9.8.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $C_{o}=d / \sigma_{d}$ is a constant that depends only on $d$.
Repeating the argument in (9.8.1) with $y=x$ and using the fact that $c_{T_{r}} \geq-c^{*} T_{r}$ gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
w(x) & =E_{x}\left[\exp \left(c_{T_{r}}\right) w\left(B\left(T_{r}\right)\right)\right] \geq E_{x}\left[\exp \left(-c^{*} T_{r}\right) w\left(B\left(T_{r}\right)\right)\right] \\
& =E_{x}\left[\exp \left(-c^{*} T_{r}\right)\right] \int_{\partial D(x, r)} w(z) \pi(d z)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $1 / x$ is convex, Jensen's inequality implies

$$
E_{x}\left[\exp \left(-c^{*} T_{r}\right)\right] \geq 1 / E_{x}\left[\exp \left(c^{*} T_{r}\right)\right] \geq 1 / 2
$$

Combining the last two displays, multiplying by $r^{d-1}$, and integrating from 0 to $2 \delta$ we get the lower bound

$$
\frac{(2 \delta)^{d}}{d} w(x) \geq 2^{-1} \cdot \frac{1}{\sigma_{d}} \int_{D(x, 2 \delta)} w(z) d z
$$

Rearranging and using $D(x, 2 \delta) \supset D(y, \delta), w \geq 0$ we have

$$
\begin{equation*}
w(x) \geq 2^{-1} \frac{C_{o}}{(2 \delta)^{d}} \int_{D(y, \delta)} w(z) d z \tag{9.8.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

where again $C_{o}=d / \sigma_{d}$. Combining (9.8.3) and (9.8.2) it follows that

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \geq 2^{-1} \frac{C_{o}}{(2 \delta)^{d}} \int_{D(y, \delta)} w(z) d z \\
& \geq 2^{-1} \frac{C_{o}}{(2 \delta)^{d}} \cdot 2^{-1} \frac{\delta^{d}}{C_{o}} w(y)=2^{-(d+2)} w(y)
\end{aligned}
$$

which gives the desired result.
Lemma 9.8.4 and a simple covering argument lead to
Theorem 9.8.5. Let $G$ be a connected open set. If $w \not \equiv \infty$ then

$$
w(x)<\infty \quad \text { for all } x \in G
$$

Proof. From Lemma 9.8.4, we see that if $w(x)<\infty, 2 \delta \leq r_{0}$, and $D(x, 2 \delta) \subset G$, then $w<\infty$ on $D(x, \delta)$. From this result, it follows that $G_{0}=\{x: w(x)<\infty\}$ is an open subset of $G$. To argue now that $G_{0}$ is also closed (when considered as a subset of $G$ ) we observe that if $2 \delta<r_{0}, D(y, 3 \delta) \subset G$, and we have $x_{n} \in G_{0}$ with $x_{n} \rightarrow y \in G$ then for $n$ sufficiently large, $y \in D\left(x_{n}, \delta\right)$ and $D\left(x_{n}, 2 \delta\right) \subset G$, so $w(y)<\infty$.

Before we proceed to the uniqueness result, we want to strengthen the last conclusion.

Theorem 9.8.6. Let $G$ be a connected open set with finite Lebesgue measure, $|G|<\infty$. If $w \not \equiv \infty$ then

$$
\sup _{x} w(x)<\infty
$$

Proof. Let $K \subset G$ be compact so that $|G-K|<\mu$ the constant in Lemma 9.8.3 for $\theta=c^{*}$. For each $x \in K$ we can pick a $\delta_{x}$ so that $2 \delta_{x} \leq r_{0}$ and $D\left(x, 2 \delta_{x}\right) \subset G$. The open sets $D\left(x, \delta_{x}\right)$ cover $K$ so there is a finite subcover $D\left(x_{i}, \delta_{x_{i}}\right), 1 \leq i \leq I$. Clearly,

$$
\sup _{1 \leq i \leq I} w\left(x_{i}\right)<\infty
$$

Lemma 9.8.4 implies $w(y) \leq 2^{d+2} w\left(x_{i}\right)$ for $y \in D\left(x_{i}, \delta_{x_{i}}\right)$, so

$$
M=\sup _{y \in K} w(y)<\infty
$$

If $y \in H=G-K$, then $E_{y}\left(\exp \left(c^{*} \tau_{H}\right)\right) \leq 2$ by (6.3a) so using the strong Markov property

$$
\begin{aligned}
w(y) & =E_{y}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau_{H}}\right) ; \tau_{H}=\tau\right)+E_{y}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau_{H}}\right) w\left(B_{\tau_{H}}\right) ; B_{\tau_{H}} \in K\right) \\
& \leq 2+M E_{y}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau_{H}}\right) ; B_{\tau_{H}} \in K\right) \leq 2+2 M
\end{aligned}
$$

we have the desired result.
With Theorem 9.8.6 established, we are now more than ready to prove our uniqueness result. To simplify the statements of the results that follow we will now list the assumptions that we will make for the rest of the section.
(A1) $G$ is a bounded connected open set.
(A2) $f$ and $c$ are bounded and continuous.
(A3) $w \not \equiv \infty$.
Theorem 9.8.7. If there is a solution that is bounded, it must be

$$
v(x) \equiv E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{\tau}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)\right)
$$

Proof. Theorem 9.8.1 implies that $M_{s}=u\left(B_{s}\right) \exp \left(c_{s}\right)$ is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$. Since $f, c$, and $u$ are bounded, letting $s \uparrow \tau \wedge t$ and using the bounded convergence theorem gives

$$
u(x)=E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{\tau}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) ; \tau \leq t\right)+E_{x}\left(u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}\right) ; \tau>t\right)
$$

Since $f$ is bounded and $w(x)=E_{x} \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)<\infty$, the dominated convergence theorem implies that as $t \rightarrow \infty$, the first term converges to $E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{\tau}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)\right)$. To show that the second term $\rightarrow 0$, we begin with the observation that since $\{\tau>t\} \in \mathcal{F}_{t}$, the definition of conditional expectation and the Markov property imply

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x}\left(u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) ; \tau>t\right) & =E_{x}\left(E_{x}\left(u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) \mid \mathcal{F}_{t}\right) ; \tau>t\right) \\
& =E_{x}\left(u\left(B_{t}\right) \exp \left(c_{t}\right) w\left(B_{t}\right) ; \tau>t\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Now we claim that for all $y \in G$

$$
w(y) \geq \exp \left(-c^{*}\right) P_{y}(\tau \leq 1) \geq \epsilon>0
$$

The first inequality is trivial. The last two follow easily from (A1).
Replacing $w\left(B_{t}\right)$ by $\epsilon$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x}\left(\left|u\left(B_{t}\right)\right| \exp \left(c_{t}\right) ; \tau>t\right) & \leq \epsilon^{-1} E_{x}\left(\left|u\left(B_{t}\right)\right| \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) ; \tau>t\right) \\
& \leq \epsilon^{-1}\|u\|_{\infty} E_{x}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) ; \tau>t\right) \rightarrow 0
\end{aligned}
$$

as $t \rightarrow \infty$, by the dominated convergence theorem since $w(x)=E_{x} \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)< \infty$ and $P_{x}(\tau<\infty)=1$. Going back to the first equation in the proof, we have shown $u(x)=v(x)$ and the proof is complete.

This completes our consideration of uniqueness. The next stage in our program, fortunately, is as easy as it always has been. Recall that here and in what follows we are assuming (A1)-(A3).

Theorem 9.8.8. If $v \in C^{2}$, then it satisfies (a) in $G$.
Proof. Let $x \in D$ and $B(x, r) \subset D$. Let $\sigma$ be the exit time from $B(x, r)$. The strong Markov property implies

$$
v(x)=E_{x} v\left(B_{\sigma} \exp \left(c_{\sigma}\right)\right.
$$

Using Taylor's theorem

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x} v\left(B(\sigma) \exp \left(c_{\sigma}\right)\right)-v(x) & =E_{x} \sum_{i=1}^{d} \frac{\partial v}{\partial x_{i}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{D}\right)-x_{i}\right] \\
& +\frac{1}{2} E_{x} \sum_{1 \leq i, j \leq d} \frac{\partial^{2} v}{\partial x_{i} \partial x_{j}}(x)\left[B_{i}\left(\tau_{D}\right)-x_{i}\right]\left[B_{j}\left(\tau_{D}\right)-x_{j}\right]+o\left(r^{2}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since for $i \neq j, B_{i}(t), B_{i}^{2}(t)-t$ and $B_{i}(t) B_{j}(t)$ are martingales, we have

$$
E_{x} v\left(B\left(\tau_{D}\right)\right)-v(x)=\frac{1}{2} \Delta v(x) E_{x} \tau_{B}+o\left(r^{2}\right)
$$

On the other hand $E_{x} \int_{0}^{\tau_{B}} g\left(B_{s}\right) d s=[g(x)+o(1)] E_{x} \tau_{B}$ so letting $r \rightarrow 0$ we have $(1 / 2) \Delta v=-g$

Having proved Theorem 9.8.8, the next step is to consider the boundary condition. As in the last two sections, we need the boundary to be regular.

Theorem 9.8.9. $v$ satisfies (b) at each regular point of $\partial G$.
Proof. Let $y$ be a regular point of $\partial G$. We showed in (4.5a) and (4.5b) that if $x_{n} \rightarrow y$, then $P_{x_{n}}(\tau \leq \delta) \rightarrow 1$ and $P_{x_{n}}\left(B_{\tau} \in D(y, \delta)\right) \rightarrow 1$ for all $\delta>0$. Since $c$ is bounded and $f$ is bounded and continuous, the bounded convergence theorem implies that

$$
E_{x_{n}}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) f\left(B_{\tau}\right) ; \tau \leq 1\right) \rightarrow f(y)
$$

To control the contribution from the rest of the space, we observe that if $|c| \leq M$ then using the Markov property and the boundedness of $w$ established in Theorem 9.8.6 we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{x_{n}}\left(\exp \left(c_{\tau}\right) f\left(B_{\tau}\right) ; \tau>1\right) & \leq e^{M}\|f\|_{\infty} E_{x_{n}}\left(w\left(B_{1}\right) ; \tau>1\right) \\
& \leq e^{M}\|f\|_{\infty}\|w\|_{\infty} P_{x_{n}}(\tau>1) \rightarrow 0
\end{aligned}
$$

which completes the proof.

This brings us finally to the problem of determining when $v$ is smooth enough to be a solution.

Theorem 9.8.10. If in addition to (A1)-(A3), $c$ is Hölder continuous, then $v \in C^{2}$ and, hence, satisfies (a).

Return to Example 9.8.2. Recall $d=1, G=(-a, a), c(x) \equiv \gamma>0$, and $f \equiv 1$.

Theorem 9.8.11. Let $\tau=\inf \left\{t: B_{t} \notin(-a, a)\right\}$. If $0<\gamma<\pi^{2} / 8 a^{2}$, then

$$
E_{x} e^{\gamma \tau}=\frac{\cos (x \sqrt{2 \gamma})}{\cos (a \sqrt{2 \gamma})}
$$

If $\gamma \geq \pi^{2} / 8 a^{2}$ then $E_{x} e^{\gamma \tau} \equiv \infty$.
Proof. By results in Example 9.8.2

$$
u(x)=\cos (x \sqrt{2 \gamma}) / \cos (a \sqrt{2 \gamma})
$$

is a nonnegative solution of

$$
\frac{1}{2} u^{\prime \prime}+\gamma u=0 \quad u(-a)=u(a)=1
$$

To check that $w \not \equiv \infty$, we observe that Theorem 9.8.1 implies that $M_{t}= u\left(B_{t}\right) e^{\gamma t}$ is a local martingale on $[0, \tau)$. If we let $T_{n}$ be a sequence of stopping times that reduces $M_{t}$, then

$$
u(x)=E_{x}\left(u\left(B_{T_{n} \wedge n}\right) e^{\gamma\left(T_{n} \wedge n\right)}\right)
$$

Letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ and noting $T_{n} \wedge n \uparrow \tau$, it follows from Fatou's lemma that

$$
u(x) \geq E_{x} e^{\gamma \tau}
$$

The last equation implies $w \not \equiv \infty$, so Theorem 9.8.8 implies the result for $\gamma<\pi^{2} / 8 a^{2}$.

To see that $w(x) \equiv \infty$ when $\gamma \geq \pi^{2} / 8 a^{2}$, suppose not. In this case (6.3) implies $v(x)=E_{x}\left(f\left(B_{\tau}\right) \exp \left(c_{\tau}\right)\right)$ is the unique solution of our equation but computations in Example 9.8.2 imply that in this case there is no nonnegative solution.

## Appendix A

## Measure Theory Details

This Appendix proves the results from measure theory that were stated but not proved in the text.

## A. 1 Caratheéodory's Extension Theorem

This section is devoted to the proof of:
Theorem A.1.1. Let $\mathcal{S}$ be a semialgebra and let $\mu$ defined on $\mathcal{S}$ have $\mu(\emptyset)=0$. Suppose (i) if $S \in \mathcal{S}$ is a finite disjoint union of sets $S_{i} \in \mathcal{S}$ then $\mu(S)=\sum_{i} \mu\left(S_{i}\right)$, and (ii) if $S_{i}, S \in \mathcal{S}$ with $S=+_{i \geq 1} S_{i}$ then $\mu(S) \leq \sum_{i} \mu\left(S_{i}\right)$. Then $\mu$ has a unique extension $\bar{\mu}$ that is a measure on $\overline{\mathcal{S}}$ the algebra generated by $\mathcal{S}$. If the extension is $\sigma$-finite then there is a unique extension $\nu$ that is a measure on $\sigma(\mathcal{S})$.

Proof. Lemma 1.1.7 shows that $\overline{\mathcal{S}}$ is the collection of finite disjoint unions of sets in $S$. We define $\bar{\mu}$ on $\overline{\mathcal{S}}$ by $\bar{\mu}(A)=\sum_{i} \mu\left(S_{i}\right)$ whenever $A=+{ }_{i} S_{i}$. To check that $\bar{\mu}$ is well defined, suppose that $A=+{ }_{j} T_{j}$ and observe $S_{i}=+{ }_{j}\left(S_{i} \cap T_{j}\right)$ and $T_{j}=+{ }_{i}\left(S_{i} \cap T_{j}\right)$, so (i) implies

$$
\sum_{i} \mu\left(S_{i}\right)=\sum_{i, j} \mu\left(S_{i} \cap T_{j}\right)=\sum_{j} \mu\left(T_{j}\right)
$$

In Section 1.1 we proved:
Lemma A.1.2. Suppose only that (i) holds.
(a) If $A, B_{i} \in \overline{\mathcal{S}}$ with $A=+_{i=1}^{n} B_{i}$ then $\bar{\mu}(A)=\sum_{i} \bar{\mu}\left(B_{i}\right)$.
(b) If $A, B_{i} \in \overline{\mathcal{S}}$ with $A \subset \cup_{i=1}^{n} B_{i}$ then $\bar{\mu}(A) \leq \sum_{i} \bar{\mu}\left(B_{i}\right)$.

To extend the additivity property to $A \in \overline{\mathcal{S}}$ that are countable disjoint unions $A=+_{i \geq 1} B_{i}$, where $B_{i} \in \bar{S}$, we observe that each $B_{i}=+{ }_{j} S_{i, j}$ with $S_{i, j} \in \mathcal{S}$ and $\sum_{i \geq 1} \bar{\mu}\left(B_{i}\right)=\sum_{i \geq 1, j} \mu\left(S_{i, j}\right)$, so replacing the $B_{i}$ 's by $S_{i, j}$ 's we can without loss of generality suppose that the $B_{i} \in \mathcal{S}$. Now $A \in \overline{\mathcal{S}}$
implies $A=+{ }_{j} T_{j}$ (a finite disjoint union) and $T_{j}=+{ }_{i \geq 1} T_{j} \cap B_{i}$, so (ii) implies

$$
\mu\left(T_{j}\right) \leq \sum_{i \geq 1} \mu\left(T_{j} \cap B_{i}\right)
$$

Summing over $j$ and observing that nonnegative numbers can be summed in any order,

$$
\bar{\mu}(A)=\sum_{j} \mu\left(T_{j}\right) \leq \sum_{i \geq 1} \sum_{j} \mu\left(T_{j} \cap B_{i}\right)=\sum_{i \geq 1} \mu\left(B_{i}\right)
$$

the last equality following from (i). To prove the opposite inequality, let $A_{n}=B_{1}+\cdots+B_{n}$, and $C_{n}=A \cap A_{n}^{c} . C_{n} \in \overline{\mathcal{S}}$, since $\overline{\mathcal{S}}$ is an algebra, so finite additivity of $\bar{\mu}$ implies

$$
\bar{\mu}(A)=\bar{\mu}\left(B_{1}\right)+\cdots+\bar{\mu}\left(B_{n}\right)+\bar{\mu}\left(C_{n}\right) \geq \bar{\mu}\left(B_{1}\right)+\cdots+\bar{\mu}\left(B_{n}\right)
$$

and letting $n \rightarrow \infty, \bar{\mu}(A) \geq \sum_{i \geq 1} \bar{\mu}\left(B_{i}\right)$.
Having defined a measure on the algebra $\overline{\mathcal{S}}$ we now complete the proof by establishing

Theorem A.1.3. Carathéodory's Extension Theorem. Let $\mu$ be a $\sigma$-finite measure on an algebra $\mathcal{A}$. Then $\mu$ has a unique extension to $\sigma(\mathcal{A})=$ the smallest $\sigma$-algebra containing $\mathcal{A}$.

Uniqueness. We will prove that the extension is unique before tackling the more difficult problem of proving its existence. The key to our uniqueness proof is Dynkin's $\pi-\lambda$ theorem, a result that we will use many times in the book. As usual, we need a few definitions before we can state the result. $\mathcal{P}$ is said to be a $\pi$-system if it is closed under intersection, i.e., if $A, B \in \mathcal{P}$ then $A \cap B \in \mathcal{P}$. For example, the collection of rectangles $\left(a_{1}, b_{1}\right] \times \cdots \times\left(a_{d}, b_{d}\right]$ is a $\pi$-system. $\mathcal{L}$ is said to be a $\lambda$-system if it satisfies: (i) $\Omega \in \mathcal{L}$. (ii) If $A, B \in \mathcal{L}$ and $A \subset B$ then $B-A \in \mathcal{L}$. (iii) If $A_{n} \in \mathcal{L}$ and $A_{n} \uparrow A$ then $A \in \mathcal{L}$. The reader will see in a moment that the next result is just what we need to prove uniqueness of the extension.

Theorem A.1.4. $\pi-\lambda$ Theorem. If $\mathcal{P}$ is a $\pi$-system and $\mathcal{L}$ is a $\lambda$ system that contains $\mathcal{P}$ then $\sigma(\mathcal{P}) \subset \mathcal{L}$.

Proof. We will show that
(a) if $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is the smallest $\lambda$-system containing $\mathcal{P}$ then $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is a $\sigma$-field.

The desired result follows from (a). To see this, note that since $\sigma(\mathcal{P})$ is the smallest $\sigma$-field and $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is the smallest $\lambda$-system containing $\mathcal{P}$ we have

$$
\sigma(\mathcal{P}) \subset \ell(\mathcal{P}) \subset \mathcal{L}
$$

To prove (a) we begin by noting that a $\lambda$-system that is closed under intersection is a $\sigma$-field since

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { if } A \in \mathcal{L} \text { then } A^{c}=\Omega-A \in \mathcal{L} \\
& A \cup B=\left(A^{c} \cap B^{c}\right)^{c} \\
& \cup_{i=1}^{n} A_{i} \uparrow \cup_{i=1}^{\infty} A_{i} \text { as } n \uparrow \infty
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, it is enough to show
(b) $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is closed under intersection.

To prove (b), we let $\mathcal{G}_{A}=\{B: A \cap B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})\}$ and prove
(c) if $A \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ then $\mathcal{G}_{A}$ is a $\lambda$-system.

To check this, we note: (i) $\Omega \in \mathcal{G}_{A}$ since $A \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$.
(ii) if $B, C \in \mathcal{G}_{A}$ and $B \supset C$ then $A \cap(B-C)=(A \cap B)-(A \cap C) \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ since $A \cap B, A \cap C \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ and $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is a $\lambda$-system.
(iii) if $B_{n} \in \mathcal{G}_{A}$ and $B_{n} \uparrow B$ then $A \cap B_{n} \uparrow A \cap B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ since $A \cap B_{n} \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ and $\ell(\mathcal{P})$ is a $\lambda$-system.

To get from (c) to (b), we note that since $\mathcal{P}$ is a $\pi$-system,
if $A \in \mathcal{P}$ then $\mathcal{G}_{A} \supset \mathcal{P}$ and so (c) implies $\mathcal{G}_{A} \supset \ell(\mathcal{P})$
i.e., if $A \in \mathcal{P}$ and $B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ then $A \cap B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$. Interchanging $A$ and $B$ in the last sentence: if $A \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ and $B \in \mathcal{P}$ then $A \cap B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ but this implies
if $A \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ then $\mathcal{G}_{A} \supset \mathcal{P}$ and so (c) implies $\mathcal{G}_{A} \supset \ell(\mathcal{P})$.
This conclusion implies that if $A, B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$ then $A \cap B \in \ell(\mathcal{P})$, which proves (b) and completes the proof.

To prove that the extension in Theorem A.1.3 is unique, we will show:
Theorem A.1.5. Let $\mathcal{P}$ be a $\pi$-system. If $\nu_{1}$ and $\nu_{2}$ are measures (on $\sigma$-fields $\mathcal{F}_{1}$ and $\mathcal{F}_{2}$ ) that agree on $\mathcal{P}$ and there is a sequence $A_{n} \in \mathcal{P}$ with $A_{n} \uparrow \Omega$ and $\nu_{i}\left(A_{n}\right)<\infty$, then $\nu_{1}$ and $\nu_{2}$ agree on $\sigma(\mathcal{P})$.

Proof. Let $A \in \mathcal{P}$ have $\nu_{1}(A)=\nu_{2}(A)<\infty$. Let

$$
\mathcal{L}=\left\{B \in \sigma(\mathcal{P}): \nu_{1}(A \cap B)=\nu_{2}(A \cap B)\right\}
$$

We will now show that $\mathcal{L}$ is a $\lambda$-system. Since $A \in \mathcal{P}, \nu_{1}(A)=\nu_{2}(A)$ and $\Omega \in \mathcal{L}$. If $B, C \in \mathcal{L}$ with $C \subset B$ then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\nu_{1}(A \cap(B-C)) & =\nu_{1}(A \cap B)-\nu_{1}(A \cap C) \\
& =\nu_{2}(A \cap B)-\nu_{2}(A \cap C)=\nu_{2}(A \cap(B-C))
\end{aligned}
$$

Here we use the fact that $\nu_{i}(A)<\infty$ to justify the subtraction. Finally, if $B_{n} \in \mathcal{L}$ and $B_{n} \uparrow B$, then part (iii) of Theorem 1.1.1 implies

$$
\nu_{1}(A \cap B)=\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \nu_{1}\left(A \cap B_{n}\right)=\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} \nu_{2}\left(A \cap B_{n}\right)=\nu_{2}(A \cap B)
$$

Since $\mathcal{P}$ is closed under intersection by assumption, the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem implies $\mathcal{L} \supset \sigma(\mathcal{P})$, i.e., if $A \in \mathcal{P}$ with $\nu_{1}(A)=\nu_{2}(A)<\infty$ and $B \in \sigma(\mathcal{P})$ then $\nu_{1}(A \cap B)=\nu_{2}(A \cap B)$. Letting $A_{n} \in \mathcal{P}$ with $A_{n} \uparrow \Omega$, $\nu_{1}\left(A_{n}\right)= \nu_{2}\left(A_{n}\right)<\infty$, and using the last result and part (iii) of Theorem 1.1.1, we have the desired conclusion.

Exercise A.1.1. Give an example of two probability measures $\mu \neq \nu$ on $\mathcal{F}=$ all subsets of $\{1,2,3,4\}$ that agree on a collection of sets $\mathcal{C}$ with $\sigma(\mathcal{C})=\mathcal{F}$, i.e., the smallest $\sigma$-algebra containing $\mathcal{C}$ is $\mathcal{F}$.

Existence. Our next step is to show that a measure (not necessarily $\sigma$-finite) defined on an algebra $\mathcal{A}$ has an extension to the $\sigma$-algebra generated by $\mathcal{A}$. If $E \subset \Omega$, we let $\mu^{*}(E)=\inf \sum_{i} \mu\left(A_{i}\right)$ where the infimum is taken over all sequences from $\mathcal{A}$ so that $E \subset \cup_{i} A_{i}$. Intuitively, if $\nu$ is a measure that agrees with $\mu$ on $\mathcal{A}$, then it follows from part (ii) of Theorem 1.1.1 that

$$
\nu(E) \leq \nu\left(\cup_{i} A_{i}\right) \leq \sum_{i} \nu\left(A_{i}\right)=\sum_{i} \mu\left(A_{i}\right)
$$

so $\mu^{*}(E)$ is an upper bound on the measure of $E$. Intuitively, the measurable sets are the ones for which the upper bound is tight. Formally, we say that $E$ is measurable if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu^{*}(F)=\mu^{*}(F \cap E)+\mu^{*}\left(F \cap E^{c}\right) \quad \text { for all sets } F \subset \Omega \tag{A.1.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

The last definition is not very intuitive, but we will see in the proofs below that it works very well.

It is immediate from the definition that $\mu^{*}$ has the following properties:
(i) monotonicity. If $E \subset F$ then $\mu^{*}(E) \leq \mu^{*}(F)$.
(ii) subadditivity. If $F \subset \cup_{i} F_{i}$, a countable union, then $\mu^{*}(F) \leq \sum_{i} \mu^{*}\left(F_{i}\right)$.

Any set function with $\mu^{*}(\emptyset)=0$ that satisfies (i) and (ii) is called an outer measure. Using (ii) with $F_{1}=F \cap E$ and $F_{2}=F \cap E^{c}$ (and $F_{i}=\emptyset$ otherwise), we see that to prove a set is measurable, it is enough to show

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu^{*}(F) \geq \mu^{*}(F \cap E)+\mu^{*}\left(F \cap E^{c}\right) \tag{A.1.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

We begin by showing that our new definition extends the old one.
Lemma A.1.6. If $A \in \mathcal{A}$ then $\mu^{*}(A)=\mu(A)$ and $A$ is measurable.

Proof. Part (ii) of Theorem 1.1.1 implies that if $A \subset \cup_{i} A_{i}$ then

$$
\mu(A) \leq \sum_{i} \mu\left(A_{i}\right)
$$

so $\mu(A) \leq \mu^{*}(A)$. Of course, we can always take $A_{1}=A$ and the other $A_{i}=\emptyset$ so $\mu^{*}(A) \leq \mu(A)$.

To prove that any $A \in \mathcal{A}$ is measurable, we begin by noting that the inequality is (A.1.2) trivial when $\mu^{*}(F)=\infty$, so we can without loss of generality assume $\mu^{*}(F)<\infty$. To prove that (A.1.2) holds when $E=A$, we observe that since $\mu^{*}(F)<\infty$ there is a sequence $B_{i} \in \mathcal{A}$ so that $\cup_{i} B_{i} \supset F$ and

$$
\sum_{i} \mu\left(B_{i}\right) \leq \mu^{*}(F)+\epsilon
$$

Since $\mu$ is additive on $\mathcal{A}$, and $\mu=\mu^{*}$ on $\mathcal{A}$ we have

$$
\mu\left(B_{i}\right)=\mu^{*}\left(B_{i} \cap A\right)+\mu^{*}\left(B_{i} \cap A^{c}\right)
$$

Summing over $i$ and using the subadditivity of $\mu^{*}$ gives
$\mu^{*}(F)+\epsilon \geq \sum_{i} \mu^{*}\left(B_{i} \cap A\right)+\sum_{i} \mu^{*}\left(B_{i} \cap A^{c}\right) \geq \mu^{*}(F \cap A)+\mu^{*}\left(F^{c} \cap A\right)$
which proves the desired result since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary.
Lemma A.1.7. The class $\mathcal{A}^{*}$ of measurable sets is a $\sigma$-field, and the restriction of $\mu^{*}$ to $\mathcal{A}^{*}$ is a measure.

Remark. This result is true for any outer measure.
Proof. It is clear from the definition that:
(a) If $E$ is measurable then $E^{c}$ is.

Our first nontrivial task is to prove:
(b) If $E_{1}$ and $E_{2}$ are measurable then $E_{1} \cup E_{2}$ and $E_{1} \cap E_{2}$ are.

Proof of (b). To prove the first conclusion, let $G$ be any subset of $\Omega$. Using subadditivity, the measurability of $E_{2}$ (let $F=G \cap E_{1}^{c}$ in (A.1.1), and the measurability of $E_{1}$, we get

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mu^{*}\left(G \cap\left(E_{1} \cup E_{2}\right)\right) & +\mu^{*}\left(G \cap\left(E_{1}^{c} \cap E_{2}^{c}\right)\right) \\
& \leq \mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{1}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{1}^{c} \cap E_{2}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{1}^{c} \cap E_{2}^{c}\right) \\
& =\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{1}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{1}^{c}\right)=\mu^{*}(G)
\end{aligned}
$$

To prove that $E_{1} \cap E_{2}$ is measurable, we observe $E_{1} \cap E_{2}=\left(E_{1}^{c} \cup E_{2}^{c}\right)^{c}$ and use (a).
(c) Let $G \subset \Omega$ and $E_{1}, \ldots, E_{n}$ be disjoint measurable sets. Then

$$
\mu^{*}\left(G \cap \cup_{i=1}^{n} E_{i}\right)=\sum_{i=1}^{n} \mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{i}\right)
$$

Proof of (c). Let $F_{m}=\cup_{i \leq m} E_{i}$. $E_{n}$ is measurable, $F_{n} \supset E_{n}$, and $F_{n-1} \cap E_{n}=\emptyset$, so

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n}\right) & =\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n} \cap E_{n}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n} \cap E_{n}^{c}\right) \\
& =\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{n}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n-1}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

The desired result follows from this by induction.
(d) If the sets $E_{i}$ are measurable then $E=\cup_{i=1}^{\infty} E_{i}$ is measurable.

Proof of (d). Let $E_{i}^{\prime}=E_{i} \cap\left(\cap_{j<i} E_{j}^{c}\right)$. (a) and (b) imply $E_{i}^{\prime}$ is measurable, so we can suppose without loss of generality that the $E_{i}$ are pairwise disjoint. Let $F_{n}=E_{1} \cup \ldots \cup E_{n} . F_{n}$ is measurable by (b), so using monotonicity and (c) we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mu^{*}(G) & =\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n}^{c}\right) \geq \mu^{*}\left(G \cap F_{n}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E^{c}\right) \\
& =\sum_{i=1}^{n} \mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{i}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E^{c}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ and using subadditivity

$$
\mu^{*}(G) \geq \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \mu^{*}\left(G \cap E_{i}\right)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E^{c}\right) \geq \mu^{*}(G \cap E)+\mu^{*}\left(G \cap E^{c}\right)
$$

which is (A.1.2).
The last step in the proof of Theorem A.1.7 is
(e) If $E=\cup_{i} E_{i}$ where $E_{1}, E_{2}, \ldots$ are disjoint and measurable, then

$$
\mu^{*}(E)=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \mu^{*}\left(E_{i}\right)
$$

Proof of (e). Let $F_{n}=E_{1} \cup \ldots \cup E_{n}$. By monotonicity and (c)

$$
\mu^{*}(E) \geq \mu^{*}\left(F_{n}\right)=\sum_{i=1}^{n} \mu^{*}\left(E_{i}\right)
$$

Letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ now and using subadditivity gives the desired conclusion.

## A. 2 Which Sets Are Measurable?

The proof of Theorem A.1.3 given in the last section defines an extension to $\mathcal{A}^{*} \supset \sigma(\mathcal{A})$. Our next goal is to describe the relationship between these two $\sigma$-algebras. Let $\mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$ denote the collection of countable unions of sets in $\mathcal{A}$, and let $\mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$ denote the collection of countable intersections of sets in $\mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$. Our first goal is to show that every measurable set is almost a set in $\mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$.

Define the symetric difference by $A \Delta B=(A-B) \cup(B-A)$.
Lemma A.2.1. Let $E$ be any set with $\mu^{*}(E)<\infty$.
(i) For any $\epsilon>0$, there is an $A \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$ with $A \supset E$ and $\mu^{*}(A) \leq \mu^{*}(E)+\epsilon$.
(ii) For any $\epsilon>0$, there is a $B \in \mathcal{A}$ with $\mu(B \Delta E) \leq 2 \epsilon$, where
(ii) There is a $C \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$ with $C \supset E$ and $\mu^{*}(C)=\mu^{*}(E)$.

Proof. By the definition of $\mu^{*}$, there is a sequence $A_{i}$ so that $A \equiv \cup_{i} A_{i} \supset E$ and $\sum_{i} \mu\left(A_{i}\right) \leq \mu^{*}(E)+\epsilon$. The definition of $\mu^{*}$ implies $\mu^{*}(A) \leq \sum_{i} \mu\left(A_{i}\right)$, establishing (i).

For (ii) we note that there is a finite union $B=\cup i=1^{n} A_{i}$ so that $\mu(A-B) \leq \epsilon$, and hence $\mu(E-B) \leq \epsilon$. Since $\mu(B-E) \leq \mu(A-E) \leq \epsilon$ the desired result follows.

For (iii), let $A_{n} \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$ with $A_{n} \supset E$ and $\mu^{*}\left(A_{n}\right) \leq \mu^{*}(E)+1 / n$, and let $C=\cap_{n} A_{n}$. Clearly, $C \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}, B \supset E$, and hence by monotonicity, $\mu^{*}(C) \geq \mu^{*}(E)$. To prove the other inequality, notice that $B \subset A_{n}$ and hence $\mu^{*}(C) \leq \mu^{*}\left(A_{n}\right) \leq \mu^{*}(E)+1 / n$ for any $n$.

Theorem A.2.2. Suppose $\mu$ is $\sigma$-finite on $\mathcal{A}$. $B \in \mathcal{A}^{*}$ if and only if there is an $A \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$ and a set $N$ with $\mu^{*}(N)=0$ so that $B=A-N\left(=A \cap N^{c}\right)$.

Proof. It follows from Lemma A.1.6 and A.1.7 if $A \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$ then $A \in \mathcal{A}^{*}$. A.1.2 in Section A. 1 and monotonicity imply sets with $\mu^{*}(N)=0$ are measurable, so using Lemma A.1.7 again it follows that $A \cap N^{c} \in \mathcal{A}^{*}$. To prove the other direction, let $\Omega_{i}$ be a disjoint collection of sets with $\mu\left(\Omega_{i}\right)<\infty$ and $\Omega=\cup_{i} \Omega_{i}$. Let $B_{i}=B \cap \Omega_{i}$ and use Lemma A.2.1 to find $A_{i}^{n} \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$ so that $A_{i}^{n} \supset B_{i}$ and $\mu\left(A_{i}^{n}\right) \leq \mu^{*}\left(E_{i}\right)+1 / n 2^{i}$. Let $A_{n}=\cup_{i} A_{i}^{n}$. $B \subset A_{n}$ and

$$
A_{n}-B \subset \sum_{i=1}^{\infty}\left(A_{i}^{n}-B_{i}\right)
$$

so, by subadditivity,

$$
\mu^{*}\left(A_{n}-B\right) \leq \sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \mu^{*}\left(A_{i}^{n}-B_{i}\right) \leq 1 / n
$$

Since $A_{n} \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma}$, the set $A=\cap_{n} A_{n} \in \mathcal{A}_{\sigma \delta}$. Clearly, $A \supset B$. Since $N \equiv A-B \subset A_{n}-B$ for all $n$, monotonicity implies $\mu^{*}(N)=0$, and the proof of is complete.

A measure space ( $\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \mu$ ) is said to be complete if $\mathcal{F}$ contains all subsets of sets of measure 0. In the proof of Theorem A.2.2, we showed that $\left(\Omega, \mathcal{A}^{*}, \mu^{*}\right)$ is complete. Our next result shows that $\left(\Omega, \mathcal{A}^{*}, \mu^{*}\right)$ is the completion of $(\Omega, \sigma(\mathcal{A}), \mu)$.

Theorem A.2.3. If $(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \mu)$ is a measure space, then there is a complete measure space ( $\Omega, \overline{\mathcal{F}}, \bar{\mu}$ ), called the completion of ( $\Omega, \mathcal{F}, \mu$ ), so that: (i) $E \in \overline{\mathcal{F}}$ if and only if $E=A \cup B$, where $A \in \mathcal{F}$ and $B \subset N \in \mathcal{F}$ with $\mu(N)=0$, (ii) $\bar{\mu}$ agrees with $\mu$ on $\mathcal{F}$.

Proof. The first step is to check that $\overline{\mathcal{F}}$ is a $\sigma$-algebra.
